Mishneh Torah, Divorce
משנה תורה, הלכות גירושין
Mishneh Torah, trans. by Eliyahu Touger. Jerusalem, Moznaim Pub. c1986-c2007
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001020101/NLI

Mishneh Torah, Divorce



Chapter 1

A woman may be divorced only by receiving a bill [of divorce].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In the Guide for the Perplexed (Vol. III, Chapter 49), the Rambam explains a rationale for this mitzvah. If it were possible to effect a divorce through speech alone, a woman might commit adultery and then try to free herself of liability by claiming that she had been divorced previously. Requiring a written bill of divorce prevents such a possibility from arising.</i> This bill is called a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The word <i>get</i> is Aramaic for legal document, and indeed when accompanied by a modifier is used to refer to other types of legal documents. Nevertheless, the most common use of the word is within the context of divorce, and when the term <i>get</i> appears without a modifier, it generally refers to a bill of divorce. (See the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Gittin</i> 2:5.)</i><br>The Torah establishes ten principles as fundamental [for a divorce to be effective].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 222) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 579) consider this to be one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.<br>The intent is not that it is a mitzvah to divorce one's wife, but rather that if a man does desire to divorce his wife, it is a mitzvah for him to follow the rules prescribed by the Torah. To cite a parallel example: There is not a mitzvah to eat meat. If, however, one does desire to eat meat, it is a mitzvah to have the animal slaughtered according to the rules prescribed by the Torah.</i> They are:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The details of all these principles are described in the halachot and chapters that follow.</i><br>a) That a man must voluntarily initiate the divorce;<br>b) That he must effect the divorce by means of a written document and through no other means;<br>c) That this document must communicate that he is divorcing [his wife] and releasing her from his domain;<br>d) That it should utterly sever the connection between the husband and his wife;<br>e) That [the <i>get</i>] should be written for the sake [of the woman being divorced];<br>f) That once [the <i>get</i>] is written, there should be no action [necessary] except its transfer to the woman;<br>g) That he should actually transfer [the <i>get</i>] to her;<br>h) That he should transfer [the <i>get</i>] to her in the presence of witnesses;<br>i) That he should actually transfer it to her for the sake of divorce;<br>j) That the husband or his agent should be the one who gives it to her.<br>The other requirements of a <i>get</i> - e.g., dating it, having it signed by witnesses and the like - are all Rabbinic institutions.
What are the sources that indicate that these ten requirements stem from Scripture itself? [They are derived from Deuteronomy 24:1, which] states: "And if it comes to pass that she does not find favor in his eyes, and he will write a bill of divorce for her, place it in her hand and send her from his home."<br>"If... she does not find favor in his eyes" - this indicates that he divorces her only on his own initiative. If a woman is divorced against her husband's will, the divorce is invalid. A woman may, however, be divorced either voluntarily or against her will.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This is the law as prescribed by Scriptural and Talmudic law. Nevertheless, Rabbenu Gershom, one of the Sages who laid the foundations for Ashkenazi custom and tradition, ordained that, except in several unique instances, a man may not divorce his wife without her consent. This practice is followed universally within the Ashkenazi community (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 119:6) and has been accepted by many Sephardim as well.</i>
"And he will write" - this teaches that a woman can be divorced only by means of a written document.<br>"For her" - that it should be written for her sake.<br>"A bill of divorce" - i.e., a deed that severs the relationship between [the husband and his wife], without leaving him any jurisdiction over her. If [the relationship] between them is not entirely severed, the divorce is not effective, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 8, Halachah 2.</i><br>"He will... place it in her hand" - this teaches that she is not divorced until the bill of divorce is placed in her hand, in the hand of her agent - which is considered to be her hand - or in her domain - which is considered to be her hand - as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 1.</i><br>"And he will... send her" - [the wording of] the <i>get</i> should indicate that he is sending her away and not that he is sending himself away from her.
What is implied? If he writes to her: "Behold you are sent away," "Behold you are divorced," "You are [now] independent," "You are now permitted [to marry] any man," or the like, the divorce is effective. The essence [of the text] of a <i>get</i> is the statement: "You are now permitted to [marry] any man."<br>If, by contrast, he writes to her: "I am no longer your husband," "I am no longer the one who consecrates you," or "I am no longer your man," the divorce is not effective. For "and he will... send her" implies that he should not send himself away from her.<br>Similarly, if a man writes to his wife: "Behold you are free," the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This wording is used in the deed freeing a slave from servitude and is not appropriate with regard to a woman's divorce.</i>
The Torah's expression, "And he will... send her from his home," does not mean that the divorce does not become effective until she leaves his home. Instead, the divorce becomes effective when the <i>get</i> reaches [a woman's] hand, even though she still is in her husband's home, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 8.</i> "And he will... send her" teaches that if he divorces her, but does not send her away from his home, it is as if he divorced her and then remarried [her]. Therefore, she requires another <i>get</i>, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachah 18.</i>
What is the source that teaches that once [the <i>get</i>] is written, there should be no action [necessary] except its transfer? The [sequence of the verbs] "And he will write..., [and] place," indicating that a <i>get</i> is acceptable only when [the only things] lacking are writing and transfer. This excludes an article that must be detached after it has been written.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 124:3), which states that the scribe should cut the paper or parchment used for the <i>get</i> to the right size before writing it. If he cuts it to size after he writes it, it is not acceptable according to Rabbinic law.</i><br>Therefore, if a man writes a <i>get</i> on the horn of a cow, he must give [his wife] the cow [for the divorce to be effective]. If he cut off the horn after he wrote [the <i>get</i>] on it, it is not effective. Similarly, if he wrote [a <i>get</i>] on a plant that was still attached to its source of nurture, the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the man gives the woman the entire plant and the land on which it grows, the divorce is void (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 124:4).</i> [This applies] even if the witnesses signed after it was detached.
We may not even write the standard text<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the standard text of the <i>get</i>, leaving blank places for the name of the man, his wife, the date and the line that states: "Behold, you are free to [marry] any man." See Chapter 3, Halachah 7.</i> of [a <i>get</i>] on an article that is attached to its source of nurture.<br>If [a scribe] writes the standard text of [a <i>get</i>] on an article that is attached to its source of nurture, and detaches it and afterwards writes the names of the husband and the wife, the date<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The date of the <i>get</i> is only a Rabbinic requirement. Nevertheless, if it is written while the <i>get</i> is still attached, the <i>get</i> is not acceptable according to Rabbinic law.</i> and the words, "Behold, you are permitted [to marry] any man," and the witnesses signed and it was given to her, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., at the outset, one should not write any portion of the <i>get</i> while it is attached to its source of nurture. After the fact, as long as the fundamental elements of the <i>get</i> were written after it was detached, it is acceptable.</i>
If the husband writes the <i>get</i> on a leaf growing in a flowerpot with a hole at the bottom, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable, even if he gives her the entire flowerpot. [This is] a decree, [lest] one detach [the leaf].<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 124:12 and the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 124:12 both emphasize that the term "unacceptable" - <i>pasul</i> in Hebrew - implies that while the <i>get</i> is acceptable according to Scriptural law, it is unacceptable according to Rabbinic decree. Accordingly, they explain that this law applies when there is an interruption between the flowerpot and the earth. If there is no interruption, the <i>get</i> is void according to Scriptural law, because when a flowerpot has a hole at its base, it is considered as though it were attached to the ground.<br>Similarly, according to the <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 124:13, even when a <i>get</i> is written on the leaves of a plant growing in a flowerpot that does not have a hole at its base, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable according to Rabbinic decree.</i> He may, however, write [the <i>get</i>] on the pottery of the flowerpot and give it to her.
What is the source that teaches that [the <i>get</i>] must be given to her for the sake of divorce? It is written: "... a bill of divorce for her, place it in her hand," [implying that] he must place [it in her hand] for the sake of divorce. If, however, he gave it to her as a promissory note or as a <i>mezuzah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">From this and the following halachah, it appears that the Rambam maintains that it is sufficient that the husband state his intent to the witnesses; the woman need not know that she is being divorced. <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Gittin</i> 78a, quoted by the Ramah in <i>Even HaEzer</i> 136:5) states that although the woman need not be informed by her husband at the time the <i>get</i> is given, she must be informed by the witnesses afterwards, or in some way have this matter made known to her. The commentaries question whether or not the Rambam differs with this view.</i> or he placed it in her hand while she was sleeping, and she awoke and [discovered] it in her hand, the <i>get</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to the latter instance, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 138:3) states that even if he tells the witnesses: "See the <i>get</i> that I am giving her," the divorce is void.<br>From this clause, it appears that, in addition to the husband's intent, the Rambam requires that the woman take an active part in receiving the <i>get</i>, even if she is not totally aware of what it is.</i> If, however, he told her afterwards, "Behold this is your <i>get</i>, the divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rav David Arameah, this clause applies to all the instances mentioned in the halachah.</i>
[The above principle must be clarified in the context of the following law.] A man tells witnesses, "See the <i>get</i> I am giving her," and then he tells [his wife]: "Take this promissory note," [the <i>get</i>] is effective. For he has told the witnesses that he was giving it to her for the sake of divorce. He told her that it was a promissory note only because he was embarrassed [to face] her.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">We accept this rationale and do not say that he nullified the <i>get</i> with his statements.</i>
At the time he gives the <i>get</i>, [a man who] divorces [his wife] must tell her: "Behold, your <i>get</i>," or "This is your <i>get</i>," or the like. If he places the <i>get</i> in her hand without saying anything, the <i>get</i> is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned previously, the word <i>pasul</i> generally means "unacceptable according to Rabbinic decree." (See Chapter 2, Halachah 7; Chapter 10, Halachah 2.) In this instance, however, there are authorities who maintain that the Rambam's intent is that the <i>get</i> is utterly void. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 136) and Rabbenu Nissim indeed rule in that manner.<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 136:1, however, explains that the <i>get</i> itself indicates the purpose for which it is given. Hence, in contrast to <i>kiddushin</i> (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:8), even if it is given in silence, it is acceptable according to Scriptural law and disqualified only by the Rabbis.</i><br>When does the above apply? When the husband was not speaking to her about divorce [immediately beforehand]. If, however, he had been speaking to her about divorce [at the time], and he takes the <i>get</i> and places it in her hand without saying anything, the divorce is acceptable.
[The following rules apply when] a <i>get</i> has been placed on the ground, and [the husband] tells [his wife], "Pick your <i>get</i> up from the ground," or it was tied to his hand or to his thigh, and she takes it from him. Even if after it reaches her possession, he tells her, "Behold this is your <i>get</i>," it is void.<br>[The rationale is] that it is written: "And he will... place it in her hand," [implying] that she may not take it on her own accord.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 1, where the Rambam states this explicitly.</i> And [in these instances,] neither [the husband] nor his agent gave it to her.<br>If, however, he bends his body toward her,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Gittin</i> 78a, Rabbenu Asher offers a slightly different conception of the activity necessary for a husband to perform so that helping his wife take the <i>get</i> is considered "giving." The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 138:1) quotes both views, but appears to favor that of Rabbenu Asher.</i> or tilts his hand until she takes the <i>get</i> from him and says,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 8:2), it appears that this statement must be made in the process of transferring the <i>get</i>, and not afterwards.</i> "Behold this is your <i>get</i>," the <i>get</i> is [effective].<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For he has performed an action making it easier for her to take the <i>get</i>.</i>
What is the source that teaches that [a <i>get</i>] must be given in the presence of witnesses? [Deuteronomy 19:15] states: "According to the words of two witnesses or those of three witnesses will the matter be established."<br>It is impossible that on one day a woman will be considered to be forbidden and sexual relations with her punishable by execution, and on the next day she should be permitted [to any man] unless [the divorce is observed by] witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The transition of a woman from a state where she is forbidden to one in which she is permitted is one of the "matters" referred to in the above verse.</i><br>Therefore, if [a husband] gives [his wife] a <i>get</i> in private, or even if the exchange is observed by one witness, the divorce is utterly void.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries explain that there is a fundamental difference between the function of witnesses in cases involving financial matters and their function with regard to marriage and divorce. With regard to financial matters, the function of witnesses is to clarify the truth (<i>eidei berur</i>). With regard to marriage and divorce, by contrast, the witnesses' function is to notarize the event (<i>eidei kiyyum</i>). For a marriage bond to be established - or broken - even when the husband and wife agree that the event took place, witnesses must observe the proceedings (<i>Tumim</i> 90:14; <i>Tzafenat Paneach; K'lallei HaTorah</i>).</i>
When does the above apply? When the <i>get</i> is written by a scribe.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">And not signed by the scribe. If the scribe also signs the <i>get,</i> the signature of one witness is enough for the <i>get</i> to be acceptable according to the Rambam. (See the notes on Chapter 2, Halachah 2.)</i> When, however, the husband writes the <i>get</i> himself, one witness signs it,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">According to Scriptural law, the <i>get</i> is acceptable, for the husband's writing is equivalent to the testimony of one hundred witnesses. It is unacceptable only because of a Rabbinic decree. Indeed, even if the witness did not sign the <i>get</i>, the exact same laws would apply. The Rambam mentions the signature of the <i>get</i> by a witness only to clarify that, even with such a signature - in contrast to a <i>get</i> signed by a scribe and another witness - the <i>get</i> is still disqualified by the Rabbis (<i>Beit Yosef, Even HaEzer</i> 130).</i> and [the husband] gives it to her, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable [only by Rabbinic decree,]<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In such an instance, if the woman transgresses this Rabbinic prohibition and marries again on the basis of this <i>get</i>, she is allowed to remain married. Other authorities, however, maintain that since the <i>get</i> was given without being observed by witnesses, it is void according to Scriptural law, and the woman must leave her second husband (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 130:31).</i> {and the woman is forbidden to marry a priest}.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Although present in the standard texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, this phrase is lacking in the authoritative manuscripts and early printings of the text. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that it is self-evident from the previous clause, and therefore is most likely a printer's addition. For this reason, we have set it off in braces.</i>
It is an ordinance [enacted] by our Sages that witnesses should sign a <i>get</i>, lest a [husband] give [his wife] a <i>get</i> in the presence of two [witnesses], and they die.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 34b, 36a explains that there is a difference of opinion between Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Meir. Rabbi Eliezer maintains, as stated here by the Rambam, that the fundamental requirement for witnesses is with regard to the witnesses who observe the transfer of the <i>get</i>. The signing of the <i>get</i> by witnesses is merely a Rabbinic ordinance. Rabbi Meir, by contrast, maintains that the fundamental requirement is for the witnesses to sign the <i>get</i>. Most authorities follow the perspective of Rabbi Eliezer. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> maintains, however, that <i>a priori</i>, the perspective of Rabbi Meir should also be respected.<br>At present, the custom is that the witnesses who sign the <i>get</i> also observe its transfer (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:1).</i> [In such an instance,] the <i>get</i> she possesses is no more than a shard, for there are no witnesses [to testify to its authenticity]. [To prevent such a situation, our Sages] ordained that the testimony [regarding the authenticity of the <i>get</i> should be contained] within it.<br>Although witnesses [have signed] within, [the husband] must give [the <i>get</i>] to [his wife] in the presence of two [witnesses] - whether the same witnesses who signed it or two others. For in essence, divorce is established by virtue of the witnesses [who observe] the transfer [of the <i>get</i>].
When two witnesses sign [the <i>get</i>], and [the husband] transgresses and gives [the <i>get</i>] to [his wife] in private, or if it is discovered that the witnesses [who observed] the transfer [of the <i>get</i>] were unsuitable,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they were deemed unworthy of serving as witnesses because of family ties to the couple, their violation of Scriptural law or other reasons, as detailed in <i>Hilchot Edut</i>, Chapters 9-16).</i> the divorce is effected.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling indicates that although Rabbi Eliezer places an emphasis on the witnesses who observe the transfer of the <i>get</i>, he does not negate the effect of the signature of the <i>get</i> by witnesses.</i> For the witnesses [who signed the <i>get</i>] are acceptable, and the <i>get</i> exists in the woman's possession. Some of the <i>geonim</i> have ruled that [the <i>get</i>] is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 133:3 notes that <i>Tosafot</i> and Rabbenu Asher maintain that such a <i>get</i> is utterly void. He questions why the Rambam states that this opinion considers it unacceptable merely according to Rabbinic decree. If Rabbi Eliezer does not accept the signature of witnesses as sufficient, seemingly the <i>get</i> would be of no consequence whatsoever, not merely disqualified by the Sages.</i>
If the witnesses [who signed the <i>get</i>] are unsuitable - or even if one was unsuitable and one was acceptable - and [the husband] gave it to her in the presence of two acceptable witnesses, [the <i>get</i>] is unacceptable. It is as if it were a forgery.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 4a explains that even Rabbi Eliezer, who puts the emphasis on the witnesses who observe the transfer of the <i>get</i>, would disqualify such a <i>get</i>. Although - as stated in the following halachah, a <i>get</i> that was not signed by any witnesses is acceptable - when it is signed by unsuitable witnesses, it is not.</i>
If the witnesses signed [the <i>get</i> at a position] more than two lines away from the text [of the <i>get</i>], it is unacceptable.<br>How far may the witnesses sign away from the text? Less than two lines, so that their [names] will be read together with [the text].<br>When does the [disqualification] mentioned above apply? When the <i>get</i> is in the woman's possession, and there are no witnesses [who observed its] transfer. If, however, [the husband] gave [his wife the <i>get</i>] in the presence of witnesses, [the <i>get</i>] is acceptable even if the witnesses'[signatures] are far removed from the text.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad states that if such a <i>get</i> were given, it would be unacceptable despite the fact that witnesses observed its transfer. Many authorities, however, follow the Rambam's view. (See Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:1.)<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 130:3 questions the Rambam's decision, noting that in <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 27:5, the Rambam states that a contract of loan is invalid if the witnesses sign more than two lines away from the text, and he does not mention the option of making it acceptable by giving it in the presence of witnesses. He explains that laws regarding business contracts differ, because they must serve as proof over an extended period of time.<br><i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i> explains that this explanation is supported by the distinction between the role of witnesses with regard to marriage and divorce, and their role with regard to business agreements mentioned in the notes on Halachah 13. Since the role of witnesses in business agreements is to clarify the terms of the agreement, such a document is not acceptable, because particulars can be added. With regard to marriage and divorce, by contrast, the witnesses' function is merely to notarize the event, and this is accomplished by the witnesses who observe the transfer of the <i>get</i>.</i> [Moreover, this ruling applies] even when there were not any witnesses who signed [the <i>get</i>]. For in essence, divorce is effected by virtue of the witnesses [who observe] the transfer [of the <i>get</i>].
It is required to read the <i>get</i> [aloud] in the presence of the witnesses who observe its transfer.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since the witnesses who observe the transfer of the <i>get</i> are essential for the divorce to be effective, they must know that the document transferred is in fact a <i>get</i> (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 135:1).</i> Afterwards, it should be given to her. If it was given to her in their presence first, it should be taken from her and read [aloud] after it was given to her.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 135:1) states that it is customary to read the <i>get</i> aloud both before and after it is given.</i><br>[The following rule applies when the witnesses] read [the <i>get</i>] while it is the possession of the husband or his agent, and they return it to him. If he encloses it within his hand, [obscuring it from the witnesses' view] and then gives it to her, they should read it again.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">After quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 135:2) states that if the husband manifests possession of the <i>get</i> after giving a document of unknown contents to the woman, his word is accepted if she has not remarried already. If, however, she has remarried, his word is not accepted.</i>
[In the latter instance,] if they did not read it, and it is taken and thrown into the sea or into a fire, the divorce is effective. Since [the witnesses] read it first, we do not suspect that it was exchanged [for another document]. Moreover, even if the husband said, "It was another document [that I gave her] and not the <i>get</i> that you read," his word is not accepted and the divorce is effective.
[If, however,] they did not read the <i>get</i> beforehand, the husband gives it to [his wife] in their presence, and then it is thrown into a fire or into the sea, the status of the divorce is doubtful.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A second <i>get</i> is necessary. Even if the woman remarries, she must leave her second husband, as explained in Chapter 10, Halachah 3.</i> [This applies] even if the husband says that it was an acceptable <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to this, <i>Gittin</i> 19b states: "Just as his statements are not acceptable to cause her to be forbidden, they are not acceptable to cause her to be permitted."</i>
If he threw the <i>get</i> into [the woman's] courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Placing the <i>get</i> in a domain belonging to the woman is equivalent to placing it in her hand.</i> among barrels in the presence of witnesses,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This instance also describes a situation in which the witnesses have not read the <i>get</i>. If they have read the <i>get</i>, the woman's status is in doubt, and we suspect that she has been divorced (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 135:5).</i> and when they looked for it they found a <i>mezuzah</i> or another document, we do not suspect that she [has been divorced]. [We assume that] the article that was found was the one that was thrown.<br>If two or three <i>mezuzot</i> or documents were discovered there, and we suspect that perhaps he threw a <i>get</i> and it was dragged away by mice, the status of the woman's divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since he threw only one document and others were discovered, we cannot say with assurance that the article discovered was the one thrown.</i>
The witnesses who sign the <i>get</i> must know how to read and sign [their names]. If they do not know how to read, we read [the <i>get</i>] in their presence,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The present custom is that both of the witnesses and the Rabbi arranging the divorce read the <i>get</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 154; <i>Seder HaGet</i> 66).</i> and they sign, provided they understand the wording of the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130), Rav Yosef Karo explains that the Rambam maintains that if the witnesses do not understand the wording of the <i>get</i>, the divorce is not effective. There is no option of translating it for them, for a witness must have first-hand experience of the matter concerning which he testifies. Hearing its translation from another person would be considered second-hand experience. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 130:27, however, quotes other opinions that do not accept this understanding.</i><br>If they do not know how to sign [their names], we write out their signatures for them on the paper with spittle or with other substances that will not leave a permanent mark,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, a permanent mark would be left, it is forbidden, for writing that covers an existing text is inadmissible.</i> and they sign [their names] over these markings.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:16) states that this is not acceptable, and instead stencils for the witnesses' signature should be made.</i><br>This practice is not followed with regard to other legal documents. It is a leniency adopted with regard to bills of divorce, so that Jewish women will not be forced to live without a marriage partner.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">We fear that the woman's husband will leave for a foreign country without giving her a divorce, or that he will die and she will be forced to perform either <i>yibbum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i> (<i>Gittin</i> 19a).</i> [This leniency is granted] because the signature of witnesses on a bill of divorce is a Rabbinic institution, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 15 above. I.e., if the matter were a point of Scriptural law, there would not be room for such leniency.</i>
Although the signature of witnesses on a bill of divorce is a Rabbinic institution,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, we follow the principle אין גוזרין גזרה לגזרה - i.e., a second Rabbinic safeguard is never attached to the first one. In these instances, however, an exception is made. Although the requirement for witnesses to sign a <i>get</i> is itself Rabbinic in origin, several safeguards were attached to facilitate the divorce laws.</i> our Sages ordained that the witnesses state their names in the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., originally the witnesses would sign, "I sign as a witness," without mentioning their names. This made the verification of their signatures a very difficult process. Therefore, our Sages required that they mention their names (Rashi, <i>Gittin</i> 36a).</i><br>Similarly, they ordained that the witnesses to the <i>get</i> must sign in the presence of each other. If either signed without the other, [the <i>get</i>] is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 9, Halachah 29 explains, based on <i>Gittin</i> 10b, that this decree was instituted lest a husband tell a group of people that they should all act as witnesses, in which case each of them would be obligated to sign the <i>get</i>. Such a <i>get</i> would look acceptable if signed by only two witnesses, but in fact would not be acceptable. To prevent such a circumstance from arising, our Sages required that all the witnesses sign in the presence of each other.</i><br>Similarly, our Sages ordained that the date of a <i>get</i> and the place where it was written be recorded [within it], as is required with regard to other legal documents.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 23:6.</i> [This was required] lest one's wife also be one's relative and she commit adultery. [Because of the husband's feelings for his wife,] he [might conceivably] write her a <i>get</i> after she had committed adultery and give it to her. If the <i>get</i> was not dated, she could say: "I was divorced before I committed adultery."<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This would enable her to avoid receiving the death penalty. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Gittin</i> 4:3) records that such an incident did in fact take place.</i> [To prevent this from happening, our Sages] ordained that <i>gittin</i> be dated.
In all the following instances, [the <i>get</i>] is unacceptable: a) a <i>get</i> signed by witnesses that is not dated, b) one that is predated,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the <i>get</i> was written in Kislev and dated in Marcheshvan. This would allow for the difficulty mentioned in the previous halachah and notes.<br>According to Rabbenu Asher, despite the fact that the requirement is Rabbinic in origin, a <i>get</i> that is predated is void entirely (<i>bateil</i>), not merely unacceptable (<i>pasul</i>). The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 127:2) quotes a third opinion, which states that even though the <i>get</i> is <i>pasul</i>, the second husband is required to divorce her, but she is forbidden to remarry her first husband.<br>The Rambam does not consider predated a <i>get</i> that was not given to the woman on the day it was written, as reflected in Chapter 2, Halachah 2. Other authorities differ, and their opinion is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 127:5), which states that a <i>get</i> that was not given on the day it was written is acceptable only when given by an agent.</i> or postdated,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., the <i>get</i> was written in Kislev and dated in Tevet.<br>In this instance, the Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that a postdated <i>get</i> is acceptable. There are two reasons given by Rav Yosef Karo in the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> to justify the Rambam's decision:<br>a) This could also be used as a cover-up, as explained in the previous halachah. For if we see that the date of the <i>get</i> is incorrect, we would disregard the date and consider the <i>get</i> as if it had no date at all.<br>b) The woman would not receive the rights to her property when due her. As long as she is married, her husband is entitled to the benefits from her property, but not after divorce. Postdating the <i>get</i> would entitle him to benefits beyond what is due him.<br><i>Tosafot</i> agree with the Ra'avad that a postdated <i>get</i> is acceptable, but unlike the Ra'avad, maintain that the <i>get</i> is not effective until the date mentioned within. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 127:9) mentions both the views of <i>Tosafot</i> and of the Rambam, but appears to favor that of <i>Tosafot</i>.</i> c) one that was written during the day and signed on the following night;<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 127:2) mentions an opinion that states that in extenuating situations - e.g., when the woman has already remarried, or her husband has gone overseas - such a <i>get</i> is acceptable. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> states that under such circumstances a <i>get</i> is acceptable, even if it is signed several days after it was written.</i> this applies even if they remained involved with the matter [of the divorce until the <i>get</i> was signed],<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Other legal documents, by contrast, are acceptable if the principals were involved in the discussion of the matter that extended from the afternoon until after nightfall. For from the time the contract was written, it was a matter of public knowledge (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 127:3).</i> d) the <i>get</i> was written in Jerusalem and [the scribe] erred and wrote [that it was written] in Lod.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 128:1) differs and states that what is of fundamental importance is the place where the <i>get</i> was signed, and not the place where it was written. It is, however, customary for it to be written and signed in the same place. See also Chapter 7, Halachah 11.<br>Our translation of the paragraph to follow is based on the interpretation of the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i>. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> follows a slightly different perspective.</i><br>[For a <i>get</i> to be acceptable,] it is necessary that it be signed at the time it was written and in the place where it was written.
A <i>get</i> is acceptable if [the husband] cut off [the portion of the <i>get</i> that contained] the date and gave it to her,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam appears to follow Rabbenu Chanan'el's interpretation of <i>Gittin</i> 17b, that our Sages did not suspect that a person would attempt a deception of such a scale to deceive the court. Therefore, the <i>get</i> is acceptable. Rashi and others explain that when saying "our Sages did not suspect that a person would not attempt such large scale deception," the Talmud explains why this instance is not mentioned in the <i>mishnah</i> discussed beforehand. It should not be interpreted to mean that the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 127:8) mentions both views, but appears to favor that of the Rambam. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 127:14, however, states that most authorities accept Rashi's view.</i> or did not write the date, merely [the week - i.e.,] the first or the second week of a given month, or he specified merely the month or [merely] the year without mentioning the month, or even if he specified merely the seven-year cycle<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In this context, <i>Gittin</i> 17b explains that even in this instance, the date of the <i>get</i> - though imprecise - is somewhat useful: it excludes the time before or after the time period mentioned. Even when the date of the month is mentioned, the time is not pinpointed exactly, because the woman could have committed adultery in the morning and received the <i>get</i> in the afternoon. Therefore, even these less precise dates are also acceptable.<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 127:12 explains that a <i>get</i> that was predated or postdated is unacceptable because the date is false, not merely imprecise. In the former instances, although there is imprecision, there is no falsehood.</i> [in which the <i>get</i> was composed].<br>Similarly, a <i>get</i> is acceptable if [the husband] writes within it: "Today I divorced her." This implies the day on which the <i>get</i> was released.
Similarly, [our Sages] ordained that the year of the ruling kingdom of that time should be mentioned in a <i>get</i> to gain the favor of the ruling authorities.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., dating our legal documents according to the rulers' years indicates the rulers' importance (Rashi, <i>Gittin</i> 80a).</i><br>[The following rules apply if] a person writes a <i>get</i> and dated it according to the years of a kingdom other [than that of his locale] or according to the years beginning from the Temple's construction or destruction. If it is customary for people in that locale to date [their documents] in this manner, it is acceptable. If this is not the local custom, it is unacceptable.<br>It has already become the universal Jewish custom to date <i>gittin</i> from the time of creation,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">This is the present custom. The practice of dating documents according to the years of the gentile rulers was discontinued because those rulers ceased to attach importance to the matter.</i> or from the crowning of Alexander the Great, which is [the accepted means of dating] for legal documents.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Alexander's kingdom began 3450 years after creation. Since it encompassed the entire Western world, it provided a uniform dating pattern for people throughout the world. It is, however, no longer customary to date legal documents in this manner.</i> If one dates [a <i>get</i>] according to the years of a contemporary kingdom, it is acceptable only in the country over which that kingdom rules.
[The following rules apply when] a person tells two [colleagues]: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife, sign it and give it to her," and the matter was delayed several days or years, or the <i>get</i> was [discovered to contain an imperfection causing it] to be [considered] void, and it was necessary to write a new <i>get</i> that was acceptable, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 8.</i> In such an instance, the date and the place when and where the <i>get</i> was written are recorded, and not the date and place when and where the husband told them to compose the <i>get</i>.<br>What is implied? If the husband told them [to write the <i>get</i>] in Jerusalem, in [the month of] Tishrei, and [the agents] delayed and did not write it until Nisan, at which time they were located in Lod, the <i>get</i> should be dated in Nisan, and Lod [should be recorded as its place], for this is where the <i>get</i> was written. [This is also the practice] with regard to other legal documents.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 23:6.</i>

Chapter 2

The Torah's statement [Deuteronomy 24:1], "And he will write a bill of divorce for her and place it in her hand," refers both to [a husband] who writes [the <i>get</i>] with his own hand and to one who tells [another person] to write it for him. Similarly, [the husband] may place it in her hand or tell an agent to give it to her.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In most matters of Torah law, a person is able to charge an agent to act on his behalf. From the Rambam's wording, <i>Terumat HaDeshen</i> (Responsum 228) and the <i>Kovetz</i> deduce that the Rambam requires that not only the person who gives the <i>get</i>, but also the scribe, must be appointed as agents. This decision is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 120:1). Moreover, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> continues, stating that in addition, the scribe must write the <i>get</i> with paper and ink belonging to the husband.<br>There are, however, other opinions, which maintain that the scribe need not be appointed as an agent. (See Chapter 3, Halachah 16 and notes.)</i><br>The verse states, "And he will write," only to teach that divorce is effective only through the medium of a written document. "And [he will] place it" teaches that the woman may not take [the <i>get</i>] herself.
When [a husband] tells two [colleagues]: "Write a <i>get</i>, sign it, and give it to my wife," the two may write it, sign it,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difficulty in this instance, for the scribe must also act as one of the witnesses who sign the <i>get</i>. <i>Tosafot</i> and many other authorities rule that a <i>get</i> signed by a scribe is unacceptable. In the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130), Rav Yosef Karo explains that the Rambam agrees that <i>a priori</i>, the scribe should not act as one of the witnesses who sign the <i>get</i>. When there is no alternative, however, it is acceptable. (See <i>Lechem Mishneh</i>.)</i> and give it to her. They are [the husband's] agents [for the divorce], and they are the witnesses [to it].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the act the agent effects does not concern him. Therefore, there is no difficulty in his serving as a witness to it. (See also <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:16.)</i><br>Similarly, if [the husband] tells a scribe: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife for me," and he tells the witnesses to sign, the <i>get</i> may be written, signed and given to [the husband]. He may use it to divorce his wife whenever he desires.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">There are two points of clarification that must be made with regard to this ruling: First, if the husband enters into privacy with his wife after having the <i>get</i> written, the <i>get</i> is nullified, for we assume that the couple engaged in sexual relations, as reflected in Chapter 3, Halachah 5.<br>Second, there is the problem that the <i>get</i> is predated - i.e., although it was dated on the day it was written, it is the giving of the <i>get</i> and not the writing of the <i>get</i> that effects the divorce. Thus, the date on which the <i>get</i> was given and the date of the divorce are not the same.<br>With regard to that issue, we are forced to say that the Rambam does not consider it to be problematic. Indeed, when discussing the issue of predated <i>gittin</i> (Chapter 1, Halachah 25), the Rambam makes no mention of this matter at all. Other authorities differ, and it is their view that is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 127:5), which states that if a <i>get</i> is not given on the day on which it was written, it is acceptable only when given by an agent. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 127:7.)</i>
We may write a <i>get</i> for a man even when he is not accompanied by his wife, provided the witnesses and the scribe who wrote and signed [the <i>get</i>] know the identity of the man and his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 24:3-4. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> emphasizes that there are several points to be derived from an analysis of the Rambam's choice of wording in the present halachah and in that source:<br>a) Even if the man is accompanied by a woman he claims to be his wife, the witnesses may not sign a <i>get</i> for her until this fact is established.<br>b) It is not significant whether or not the scribe knows the identity of the man and his wife. When the scribe does not sign the <i>get</i> (see the notes to the previous halachah), it is the witnesses alone who must be aware of the identity of the husband and wife.<br>c) As long as a person's name has been established in a city for thirty days - even if it is only according to his own statements - this is sufficient.<br>d) The witnesses must also know the names of the father of the husband and of the wife.<br>e) The person's father's name can also be established on the basis of his own statements.</i><br>If there are two men in a city whose names are the same and whose wives' names are the same, one may divorce his wife only in the presence of the other. [This safeguard was enacted] lest one [of the men] have a <i>get</i> written and give it to the wife of the other man, causing her to [be considered] to be divorced from [her husband].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Bava Batra</i> 10:3), it appears that the intent is to prevent the woman receiving the <i>get</i> from engineering such an act of deception.<br>Our translation is based on authoritative manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard printed text states "causing her to be forbidden to him," i.e., she will think that she has been divorced, and on that basis, she will marry another man, and thus become forbidden to her first husband.</i>
In a time of danger,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A time of danger means an instance when the husband is in danger of dying and asks to have a <i>get</i> written for his wife, so that she will not have to undergo the rites of either <i>yibbum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i> (<i>Gittin</i> 66a).</i> we may write a <i>get</i> [on behalf of a husband] and give it [to his wife], even though we are not aware of [their identities].<br>It is the woman who always pays the scribe's fee.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Although according to Scriptural law it is the husband who should pay for the writing of the <i>get</i> (for it is his responsibility), <i>Bava Batra</i> 168a explains that our Sages transferred this obligation to the woman, lest the husband lack the financial resources and cause the woman to wait unnecessarily for her <i>get</i>.</i>
The husband himself must tell the scribe to write [the <i>get</i>] and the witnesses to sign [it]. When a court of law<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam mentions a court of law to indicate that in this instance, their authority is no greater than that of an ordinary person.</i> or two [colleagues] ask a man: "Shall we write a <i>get</i> for your wife?" and he tells them to write [it and sign it],<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The bracketed addition is made on the basis of the gloss of the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>.</i> it is acceptable if they themselves write [the <i>get</i>] and sign it.<br>If, however, they tell a scribe [to write] and he does, and they tell witnesses [to sign] and they do, the <i>get</i> is void, because it was written by someone who was not told to write it by the husband himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The husband charged these individuals with the composition and the signing of the <i>get</i>. They do not have the authority to convey this responsibility to someone else.<br>In other instances, an agent can transfer his agency to another person, but here the scribe and the witness were charged with only a verbal command, and a verbal command cannot be transferred to another agent (<i>Gittin</i> 66b).</i> [This ruling applies] even when they give [the <i>get</i>] to the husband, who in turn gives it to his wife in the presence of witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">For the difficulty is with the composition of the <i>get</i> itself.</i>
When [a man] tells two or three [colleagues]: "Tell a scribe to write a <i>get</i> for my wife and tell the witnesses to sign," and they convey these instructions, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the previous halachah, in which the <i>get</i> is deemed void, here the Rambam rules that it is unacceptable. This more lenient ruling is given because in this instance, the husband's colleagues carried out his instructions.<br>The Ra'avad and many other authorities also consider this <i>get</i> to be void, for they maintain that the scribe must hear his appointment from the husband directly. The Rambam, however, maintains that the divorce is acceptable according to Scriptural law. As a safeguard, the Rabbis did not accept it, lest a woman hire agents who will tell the scribe and witnesses to compose a <i>get</i> in the husband's name, even though he did not convey such instructions (<i>Gittin</i> 67a).</i> [The same ruling applies if the husband] tells two [colleagues] to tell a scribe to write a <i>get</i> and tells them to sign it.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For the husband did not convey the instructions to the scribe himself.</i><br>This matter should be deliberated upon at length, because the <i>get</i> is close to being considered void.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Rav David HaCohen interprets this to mean that this case is judged more severely than other <i>gittin</i> that are unacceptable, and the status of the woman's divorce is in doubt. This is also the ruling of the Ramah. Rav Moshe Alshacar, however, differs and maintains that there is no difference between this instance and others in which the <i>get</i> is deemed unacceptable.</i>
What is the difference between the terms "unacceptable" (<i>pasul</i>) and "void" (<i>bateil</i>)? Whenever this text refers to a <i>get</i> as "void," that means that according to Scriptural law it is void. Whenever the term "unacceptable" is used, it refers to a <i>get</i> deemed unacceptable by Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The practical difference between these two terms is explained in Chapter 10, Halachot 1 and 2. When a <i>get</i> is void, the woman's first marriage is not annulled, and she must leave her second husband if she has remarried. Any children she bears the second husband are considered to be <i>mamzerim</i>. When the <i>get</i> is deemed unacceptable by our Rabbis, by contrast, the woman should not remarry, but if she does remarry she is allowed to remain married to her second husband.</i>
When a husband brings a <i>get</i> that has been signed in his hand and tells [two colleagues]: "Give this <i>get</i> to my wife," they should give it to her [and it is acceptable].<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">We do not suspect that the husband had a void or unacceptable <i>get</i> composed and gave it to his wife to cause her difficulties (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>If he tells others to write a <i>get</i>, sign it and give to his wife, and they write it, sign it and give it to her, and then it is discovered that the <i>get</i> is void or unacceptable, they may write another <i>get</i> - or even 100 other <i>gittin</i> - until the woman receives an acceptable <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">We do not say that the agency with which they were entrusted involved only the composition of one <i>get</i>, and that they must receive a new charge from the husband before writing another one.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 122:1) states that if the <i>get</i> is not unacceptable, but does not conform with the standard demanded by the local Rav, the scribe does not have the right to write a second <i>get</i>, for the agency with which he was charged by the husband has already been discharged. Therefore, it is customary for a husband to tell the scribe and the witnesses to write as many <i>gittin</i> as necessary, until one finds favor in the eyes of the Rav arranging the divorce.</i>
If the husband tells [two colleagues]: Write and sign [a <i>get</i>] and give it to an agent to bring to her, and they do as they were charged, but it was discovered that the <i>get</i> was void or unacceptable, they may not write another [<i>get</i>] until they consult the husband.<br>[The rationale is] that [the husband] did not charge them with [effecting the divorce]. Perhaps he wanted merely that they write [the <i>get</i>] and give it to the agent. [If so, their agency was completed,] and nothing remains for them to do, for they have written it and given it [to the agent]. Therefore, they should not write another [<i>get</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband had in fact given them only such a limited agency, were they to have another <i>get</i> written, they would no longer be acting in the agency of the husband, but rather on their own initiative.<br>Rav Moshe HaCohen differs with the Rambam on this point, maintaining that since they did not write an acceptable <i>get</i>, their agency was not completed. For the husband did not charge them with writing a worthless piece of paper; he wanted them to write a <i>get</i>. Note the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 122:7, who interprets the disqualifying factor mentioned by the Rambam as referring to a disqualification that occurred after the <i>get</i> was given to the agent. He maintains that if a <i>get</i> that was void or unacceptable was given to the agent, even the Rambam would allow the writing of another <i>get</i>.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 122:2) quotes the ruling of the Rambam, while the Ramah follows the opinion of Rav Moshe HaCohen.</i><br>If they do write another <i>get</i> that is acceptable and give it to the agent - who in turn gives it to the wife - the status of the divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">We are not certain that their agency was limited in scope. Perhaps the husband's intent was that they effect the divorce.</i>
When a person tells two or more [colleagues]: "Write a <i>get</i> and give to my wife," "Divorce her," "Send her away," "Release her,"<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following term are acceptable, because they are Aramaic equivalents of the word "divorce."</i> "Discharge her," or "Write a letter and give it to her," [the colleagues] should write an acceptable <i>get</i> and give it to her.<br>If, however, he tells them: "Dismiss her," "Give her what she needs," "Do as our faith requires to her," "Do as the law requires to her," "Do unto her as she deserves," his statements are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">These expressions do not convey adequately enough the intent that the husband desires to divorce his wife for them to be considered effective in bringing about the appointment of the agents for this purpose.</i> If [these individuals] write a <i>get</i> and give it to the woman, the <i>get</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 141:21, who quotes authorities who maintain that even in such instances the status of the divorce should be considered doubtful.</i>
If he tells them: "Get her out," "Let her go," "Permit her [to remarry]," "Let her be," "Assist her," there is doubt whether or not these terms imply that she should be divorced or whether they have another meaning. Therefore, [these individuals] should not write [a <i>get</i>] for her. If they write a <i>get</i> and give it to her, the status of the divorce is in doubt.
When [a man] says: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife," [the people he addresses] should write the <i>get</i>, sign it and give it to him. They should not give it to the woman unless he tells them to. If they do give it to the woman, the <i>get</i> is not [effective].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For the husband has not charged them with effecting the divorce.</i><br>When does the above apply? With regard to a healthy man. If, however, a man is dangerously ill - i.e., he suddenly falls sick and his illness rapidly becomes very severe - or if he is being led away in chains,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 6:5), the Rambam interprets this as referring to a person taken from jail in chains to be judged by a gentile magistrate.</i> even for financial matters, or he departs on an ocean journey, or he departs with a caravan [on a desert journey] and he says, "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife," they should write it, sign it and give it to her. For it is clear that his intent was that they should write the <i>get</i> and give it to her.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that, in all these instances, his intent was to divorce his wife, lest he die and she be required to undergo the rite of either <i>yibbum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i>. Alternatively, he knew that he was entering a dangerous situation, and feared that he might die, without there being any witnesses of his death. In order to prevent his wife from being forced to remain unmarried for the rest of her life, he charges witnesses with the composition of a <i>get</i>.<br>He surely wanted to tell the witnesses to give the <i>get</i>, and it was only because of his concern with his personal situation that he forgot to do so (<i>op. cit.</i>). (See <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 8:24, which relates similar concepts with regard to a person's division of his estate.)</i>
[The following rules apply when] a healthy person says: "Compose a <i>get</i> for my wife," and [his listeners] write a <i>get</i>, sign it and give it to her. If he commits suicide immediately thereafter - e.g., he throws himself down from a roof or jumps into the sea - the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, we assume that the person intended to say that the <i>get</i> should be given, but failed to mention the point because of his preoccupation with his own concerns.</i><br>If, however, he climbs up onto a [high] roof, and he is buffeted by the wind until he falls and dies, the <i>get</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For his intent is not clear, and it is possible that he wanted to deliberate before having the <i>get</i> given.</i> If there is doubt whether he threw himself down [from the roof] or was buffeted by the wind, the <i>get</i> is valid unless it becomes known with certainty that he was buffeted by the wind.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling is debated by the commentaries, for it is questionable why the <i>get</i> would be considered valid, in light of the doubt that exists. Indeed, there are commentaries that suggest that the text before us is a printing error and that the Rambam considers the status of the divorce to be in doubt. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:18) rules that if the husband falls immediately afterwards, the <i>get</i> is acceptable. If not, the status of the divorce is in doubt.</i><br>Similarly, if a man was cast into a pit and he said, "Whoever hears my voice should write a <i>get</i> for my wife," [his listeners] should write a <i>get</i> and give it to her, provided they recognize his identity.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>:19) states that the man must state his name, the name of his wife, the name of the city in which he lives and the name of the city in which she lives.</i> Even if [afterwards,] they took him out of the pit and could not identify him,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., his face had been cut to the point where his identity was no longer recognizable.</i> [the divorce] is acceptable. For in time of danger, [a <i>get</i>] can be written and given to [a man's wife] even if they do not know his identity.<br>Similarly, when a person suffers many severe blows, to the extent that it is impossible for him to survive - or even if the majority of [his windpipe and gullet]<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The slitting of these organs are the two signs that determine whether or not ritual slaughter is acceptable.</i> have been cut - and he makes motions<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 121:11 interprets this to mean that the man shakes his head in response to questions (see Halachah 16) and thus indicates that he desires to divorce his wife.</i> and says: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife," [his listeners] should write [a <i>get</i>] and give it [to her]. Although he will eventually die, he is alive at this time.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>get</i> is not considered to have been given after the man died, and his wife is not required to undergo the rites of <i>yibbum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i>. See Chapter 6, Halachah 28.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a person is troubled by an evil spirit, and at the time when his infirmity begins to take hold of him says: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife." His statements are of no consequence, because his thoughts are not organized and settled.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For a person's actions to have halachic significance, he must be mentally competent at the time he performs them.</i> The same ruling applies to a person who has become as drunk as Lot.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Whom Genesis, Chapter 19, describes as having become so drunk that he was not aware of having sex with his daughters.</i> If a drunkard has not reached that level [of incapacity], [the status of the divorce] is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam appears to be saying that if we are certain that the person is drunk, but see that he is conscious enough to have some control over his behavior, a <i>get</i> that he orders to be given is of doubtful status.<br>The commentaries question this understanding, noting that in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:18, the Rambam rules that <i>kiddushin</i> given by a drunk are viable, unless he reaches Lot's state. Similarly, with regard to the entire Torah, a drunk is considered responsible for his conduct. If he commits a transgression punishable by death while drunk, he is executed. Despite these questions, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 121:1) quotes the Rambam's wording. On this basis, the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> states that both a husband and a wife should not drink alcoholic beverages on the day of their divorce.</i>
[The following rules apply when a] healthy [man] says: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife and give it to her," and afterwards he becomes mentally disturbed. We wait until he becomes healthy again, and then we write the <i>get</i> and give it to her. There is no need to consult with him about the matter again when he regains his heath.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">While the husband (the principal) is incapacitated, his agent (the scribe) cannot act on his behalf. Therefore, the <i>get</i> should not be written until he regains his health. Nevertheless, once he regains his health, the agency that he originally gave is unaffected, and the <i>get</i> should be written and delivered.</i><br>If a <i>get</i> is written and given before he regains his health, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo questions why the Rambam uses the term "unacceptable" (<i>pasul</i>). Seemingly, based on <i>Gittin</i> 70b, this ruling should apply only when we have a means of healing the afflicted person. Otherwise, the <i>get</i> should be void. Indeed, in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 121:2), Rav Yosef Karo rules accordingly. Note, however, the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 121:2, who justifies the Rambam's ruling.<br>[The difference between these two opinions revolves around the fundamental conception of an agent's authority. Is he acting independently of the principal (in which case the principal's state is not of concern to us) or is he considered to be an extension of the principal (in which case, if the principal is unable to perform an act, neither is his agent). See <i>Lekach Tov</i>, Section 1.]<br>Based on the above, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 121:4) writes that we should check that a person who is giving a <i>get</i> when terminally ill is sound of mind.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a person loses his ability to speak, but is of sound mind. We ask him: "Should we write a <i>get</i> for your wife?" If he nods his head affirmatively, we test him with three [questions with different answers].<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, the intent is three questions in which at least one of the answers differs from the other two. There are other authorities who require two sets of three questions. Also, based on <i>Gittin</i> 70b, the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 121:5) suggests that the person should be asked questions about fruit - e.g., would he desire a summer fruit in winter? His ability to discern with regard to these matters will serve as an indicator of whether or not he is of sound mind.</i> If he answers those [questions] to be answered affirmatively in the affirmative and those to be answered negatively in the negative, they should write [a <i>get</i>] and give it [to her]. His mental state must be checked thoroughly, lest he not be of sound mind.<br>Similarly, if he writes by hand: "Write a <i>get</i> and give it to my wife," they should write it and give it to her, if [it appears] that he is of sound mind. For the laws governing a person who loses his ability to speak differ from those governing a deaf-mute.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A deaf-mute is not considered to be of sound mind and is not held responsible for his conduct.</i>
When a person married while sound of mind and became a deaf-mute, he cannot divorce his wife until he regains his mental health.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">For the marriage bond he established is binding according to Scriptural law, and he is not capable of initiating divorce proceedings that have that power.</i> Needless to say, this applies to a person who loses his mental stability.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 9:9, where the Rambam defines the term <i>shoteh</i> as including not only maniacs whose behavior is totally beyond control, but also those who are confused and lack the stability to function normally.</i> We do not rely on the motions of a deaf-mute or on his writing, even if he is of sound mind.<br>If, however, a deaf-mute married a woman while he was in that state, he may divorce her by making motions, for his consecration of her is not valid according to Scriptural law, as has been explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:9.</i> Just as he married her by making motions, he may divorce her by making motions.
When a man consecrates a minor via her father's agency and [seeks to] divorce her while she is a minor, her father should accept her <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Just as the Torah gives him authority to consecrate her, he is responsible for taking part in the divorce, for Deuteronomy 24:2 establishes an association between the forging of the marriage bond and its dissolution (<i>Ketubot</i> 47a).<br>This applies, however, only before the marriage bond is consummated. After <i>nisu'in</i>, the consummation of the marriage, the father no longer has any authority over his daughter even though she is below the age of majority. (See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:12.)<br>We may conclude that, before <i>nisu'in</i>, the Rambam does not consider the girl as having the authority to receive her own <i>get</i>. This opinion is accepted by most authorities and is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:4). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, however, also mentions the opinion of <i>Tosafot</i>, who maintain that the girl possesses this authority.</i> When the <i>get</i> reaches her father's possession, she is divorced.<br>If [the husband seeks to] divorce [his <i>arusah</i>] when she is a <i>na'arah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a girl between the age of twelve and twelve and a half, who has manifested signs of physical maturity. She is already considered to be past majority with regard to certain dimensions of Torah law. Nevertheless, her father is still granted authority over her in certain contexts. (See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 2:1, 3:11.)</i> the divorce is effective when the <i>get</i> reaches her possession or her father's possession. A <i>na'arah</i> who is consecrated may not appoint an agent to receive her <i>get</i> from her husband during her father's lifetime.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">A married girl below the age of majority, by contrast, may not appoint an agent to receive her <i>get</i> even if her father dies or her marriage has been consummated (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:3.)</i> A father, by contrast, may appoint an agent to receive a <i>get</i> for his daughter who has been consecrated, whether she is a minor or a <i>na'arah</i>.
[The following rules apply when] a girl's father consecrates her<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If the girl's father consecrates her, the <i>kiddushin</i> are effective according to Scriptural law, and a divorce is required. If, by contrast, the girl's father died, and her mother, her brother or she herself established a marriage bond, it is not binding according to Scriptural law and a <i>get</i> is not required. Instead, this marriage can be dissolved through the rite of <i>mi'un</i>, as described in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:7.</i> when she is a minor and then [the father] dies. If [the girl] can differentiate between a <i>get</i> and another object,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:6) states that this refers to a girl of six or seven, depending on her intellectual capacities.</i> the divorce is effective after the <i>get</i> reaches her possession. If [she is] incapable [of making such a distinction], she cannot be divorced until she becomes capable of making such distinctions.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, a minor's father is alive, he can accept a <i>get</i> on her behalf regardless of her age or degree of understanding. Rashi (<i>Gittin</i> 64b) differs and maintains that if she is unable to make distinctions, she cannot be divorced even via her father. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:6) quotes both opinions, it appears to favor that of the Rambam.</i> If such a divorce is carried out, it is of no consequence.
When a man whom the law requires to be compelled to divorce his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., a man whose wife says she is repulsed by him (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 14:8), a man who was married to a woman for ten years without her bearing a child (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 15:7), a man who becomes afflicted by [constant] bad breath or an odor from his nose, one who becomes a collector of dog feces, a miner of copper or a tanner or one who becomes a leper (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 25:11-12) or a priest who marries a divorcee (<i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 17:7).</i> does not desire to divorce her, the court should have him beaten until he consents, at which time they should have a <i>get</i> written. The <i>get</i> is acceptable. This applies at all times and in all places.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The application of this law is not confined to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> or to the era when the <i>Sanhedrin</i> (the High Court of Law) was in power.</i><br>Similarly, if gentiles beat him while telling him: "Do what the Jews are telling you to do," and the Jews have the gentiles apply pressure on him until [he consents] to divorce his wife, the divorce is acceptable. If, however, the gentiles compel him to write [a <i>get</i>] on their own initiative, the <i>get</i> is [merely] unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Mesharshia states that the Rabbis deemed the <i>get</i> unacceptable, lest women become accustomed to hiring gentiles for this purpose (<i>Bava Batra</i> 48a). In the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo notes that <i>Gittin</i> 88b appears to reject Rav Mesharshia's view and states that a <i>get</i> that was forced on a man by gentiles is void entirely. (This indeed is the ruling of Rashi, Rabbenu Nissim and Rabbenu Asher.) In his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 134:5), Rav Yosef Karo's wording is not specific when dealing with this issue. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 134:10 states that such a <i>get</i> is void.</i> The rationale is that the law requires him to give a divorce.<br>Why is this <i>get</i> not void? For he is being compelled - either by Jews or by gentiles - [to divorce] against his will [and a <i>get</i> must be given voluntarily].<br>Because the concept of being compelled against one's will applies only when speaking about a person who is being compelled and forced to do something that the Torah does not obligate him to do - e.g., a person who was beaten until he consented to a sale,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 10:1, which states that such a sale is acceptable, after the fact. Nevertheless, if the seller notifies the witnesses that he was compelled to sell against his will, the sale is nullified.</i> or to give a present. If, however, a person's evil inclination presses him to negate [the observance of] a mitzvah or to commit a transgression, and he was beaten until he performed the action he was obligated to perform, or he dissociated himself from the forbidden action, he is not considered to have been forced against his will. On the contrary, it is he himself who is forcing [his own conduct to become debased].<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements have implications far beyond their immediate halachic context. The Rambam is saying that the fundamental desire of every Jew is to affirm his Jewishness and observe the Torah and its mitzvot. Even when a person's conscious mind does not necessarily consent to this inner motivation, it is at work, molding his character without his knowledge. And at times, either because of undesirable circumstances - being compelled against his will as above - or because of desirable ones - an expression of Divine favor - this inner drive will surface.</i><br>With regard to this person who [outwardly] refuses to divorce [his wife] - he wants to be part of the Jewish people, and he wants to perform all the mitzvot and eschew all the transgressions; it is only his evil inclination that presses him. Therefore, when he is beaten until his [evil] inclination has been weakened, and he consents [to the divorce], he is considered to have performed the divorce willfully.<br>[Different laws apply when] the law does not require him to divorce his wife, and a Jewish court or simple people compel him to divorce her. This <i>get</i> is deemed unacceptable. Since, however, it was Jews who compelled him, he [is advised] to complete the divorce [in a proper manner]. If, by contrast, gentiles compel him to divorce when it was not required, the divorce is void. Even though he tells the gentiles that he consented and tells the Jews to write and sign [a <i>get</i>], since the law does not require him to divorce, and he was compelled to do so by gentiles, the <i>get</i> is void.

Chapter 3

A <i>get</i> that is not written for the sake of the man [initiating] the divorce and for the sake of the woman who is being divorced is not valid.<br>What is implied? A scribe wrote a <i>get</i> to learn [scribal arts] or to teach them, and a husband came and discovered that the <i>get</i> was written with his name; the name of the woman was the same as his wife's name; and the name of the city the same as the name of his city.
