If he took [this <i>get</i>] and used it to divorce [his wife], the divorce is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For the <i>get</i> was not written with the intent of being used to effect a divorce (<i>Gittin</i> 24b).</i>
Furthermore, [a <i>get</i> is also void in the following instance:] [A man] had a <i>get</i> written so that he could divorce his wife and changed his mind. An inhabitant of his city discovered it and told him: "My name is the same as yours, and my wife's name is the same as your wife's." Although [the second man] took it and used it to divorce his wife, it is invalid,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For the <i>get</i> was not written for the sake of the man [initiating] the divorce and for the sake of the woman who is being divorced (<i>Ibid.</i>).</i> despite the fact that it was written in order to effect a divorce.
Furthermore, [a <i>get</i> is also void in the following instance:] [A man] had two wives with the same name, and he wrote a <i>get</i> with the intent of divorcing the older one, and changed his mind and used it to divorce the younger one. The divorce is invalid. Although it was written for the sake of the man [initiating] the divorce, it was not written for the sake of the woman who is being divorced.
Moreover, [in the above situation,] if he tells the scribe: "Write the <i>get</i>, and I will use it to divorce whichever one I desire," and the scribe wrote the <i>get</i> with that intent, and the husband divorced one of his wives, the status of the divorce is a matter of doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling depends on the concept of <i>b'reirah</i> - i.e., that since ultimately the husband's intent is clarified, it is considered retroactively as if this had been his intent at the outset.<br>This concept of <i>b'reirah</i> is relevant not only in the present context, but also with regard to many other contexts discussed in the Talmud and later Rabbinic works. Generally, the rule that is followed is that with regard to questions involving Scriptural law, the concept of <i>b'reirah</i> is not accepted, but with regard to Rabbinic law it is accepted. On this basis, the commentaries question the Rambam's ruling. In the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo explains that although with regard to following stringencies, we say that the concept of <i>b'reirah</i> does not apply to questions of Scriptural law, it does apply with regard to the acceptance of a leniency. Therefore, although the woman must consider herself divorced, she does not have the prerogative of remarrying. On this basis, Rav Yosef Karo quotes the Rambam's wording in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:4). (See the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 130:4, who also discusses this issue.)</i><br>Whenever a <i>get</i> was not written with the proper intent, it remains void, even if the scribe rewrites the letters with the proper intent.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., tracing over the letters is ineffective, for writing on top of other writing is insignificant (<i>Gittin</i> 20a). A parallel exists with regard to the Sabbath laws. If a person traces over letters on the Sabbath, he is not considered to have violated the forbidden labor of writing (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 11:16).<br>Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:5) appears to favor the Rambam's ruling, it also quotes the opinion of the Rashba, which states that consideration must be made of a <i>get</i> given under such circumstances.</i>
When a man writes a <i>get</i> [with the intent of] divorcing his wife, but he changes his mind and does not divorce her, he may not divorce her with this same <i>get</i> another time should he so desire, if he entered into privacy with her after [the <i>get</i>] was written.<br>If, however, he did divorce her with this old <i>get</i>, the divorce is effective, and she may remarry on this basis without any qualms.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the notes on Chapter 2, Halachah 2, the Rambam does not consider a <i>get</i> that was written before it was given to be predated. Other commentaries do, however, and it is their view that is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 127:5).<br>According to those authorities, such a <i>get</i> can be acceptable only when it was sent via an agent. This relates to another difference of opinion about this issue. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 148) states that this ruling applies only when the <i>get</i> remained in the husband's possession until after he emerged from privacy with his wife. If he gave it to an agent beforehand, it is void. In the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo states that the Rambam does not accept this distinction, and in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 148:1), although he mentions the <i>Tur's</i> view, he favors his own interpretation of the Rambam's decision.</i> For [the <i>get</i>] was written for her sake and was given to her now in the presence of witnesses, as the law requires.<br>Why at the outset should it not be used for divorce? [This is] a Rabbinic decree, lest [people] say: "Her <i>get</i> preceded [the birth of] her son."
[The following rules apply when a man] tells a scribe, "Write a <i>get</i> for the sake of so and so, and it will remain in my possession. When I marry her, I will divorce her with it." If [the scribe] writes [such a <i>get</i>], and the man marries her and then he divorces her with it, the divorce is void. [The rationale is] that the woman could not be divorced when the <i>get</i> was written. Thus, it is considered not to have been written for the sake of divorce.<br>If, however, [the man] tells [the scribe]: "Write [a <i>get</i>] for the woman who is consecrated to me. After I consummate the marriage, I will divorce her," should he consummate the marriage and divorce her with such [a <i>get</i>], the divorce is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies only after the fact. At the outset, such a <i>get</i> should not be used, as reflected in the previous halachah.<br>When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 132:1) follows the opinion of the <i>Tur</i> and <i>Tosafot</i>, who differ with the Rambam and state that the <i>get</i> is acceptable only when it was post-dated, and the date on which it was given was mentioned. If the date on which the <i>get</i> was written was mentioned, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable. (It is worse than an ordinary "old <i>get</i>," because in that instance, the couple merely entered into privacy together; we are not certain that they had relations. In this instance, by contrast, the marriage bond was consummated.)</i><br>If a man had [a <i>get</i>] written for his <i>yevamah</i>, and he divorced her with it after he performed the rite of <i>yibbum</i>, the status of the divorce is doubtful, for she was not his wife in an absolute sense<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Although the <i>yevam</i> does not have to consecrate his <i>yevamah</i>, he also does not have to divorce her. Hence, there is a question whether the connection between them is sufficient for the <i>get</i> to be acceptable.</i> when [the <i>get</i>] was written.
In order to assist scribes,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">So that they would have a <i>get</i> ready, in order for them to prepare it for a husband in a relatively short time. In this manner, the scribe can use his free time for a useful purpose (Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Gittin</i> 3:2).</i> our Sages permitted them to write the standard texts of <i>gittin</i> [beforehand] and leave empty the place for the man's [name], the woman's [name], the time and the place for [the sentence]: "Behold, you are permitted [to marry] any man."<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">These are the parts of the <i>get</i> that are of fundamental importance, and which are referred to as the <i>toref</i>.</i> Thus, he can write [these parts of the <i>get</i>] for the sake of the man who gives the <i>get</i> and for the sake of the woman who receives it.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">On this issue there is a difference of opinion in the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>) between the Sages and Rabbi Eliezer. It appears that the opinion cited by the Rambam above is that of the Sages. Thus, this ruling is worthy of notice, because it represents a change of mind on the Rambam's part, for he writes in his Commentary on the Mishnah that the halachah follows Rabbi Eliezer. (It is also noteworthy that in the Rambam's manuscripts of his Commentary on the Mishnah, the latter statement was rubbed out and written over, implying that the Rambam deliberated back and forth about the matter when composing that text. See the <i>Kovetz</i> and others, who discuss this issue.)<br>See also the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 131:2, who states that the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> does not mention this law, because most authorities differ with the Rambam and do not grant a scribe such license. (See also Halachah 17 and notes.)</i> Afterwards, he should have the witnesses sign for the sake of [the husband and the wife].
[The following rule applies if] a scribe wrote a <i>get</i> for the sake of the husband and the wife as required, and the witnesses signed without having that intent. Since [the husband] gives it to [his wife] in the presence of witnesses who observe the transfer, the <i>get</i> is valid [according to Scriptural law]. It is, however, {unacceptable [by Rabbinic decree].<br>Why is [it merely unacceptable and] not void?}<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">There is a question whether the words within the brackets { } are the Rambam's or a printer's addition. There is, however, no difference in law between the two versions.</i> Because the requirement for witnesses to sign the <i>get</i> [was instituted] only as a measure to aid society.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 1, Halachah 15, it is not necessary for witnesses to sign the <i>get</i> according to Scriptural law. Our Sages required such signatures only to afford an advantage for the woman lest the witnesses die.</i><br>There is an opinion that states that if the witnesses signed without the proper intent, since it as if it were a forgery, the <i>get</i> is void. Similarly, they maintain that if one of the witnesses was unacceptable, or if it was signed by one acceptable witness only, it is void even though it was transferred in the presence of witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:17) follows the Rambam's approach and rules that such a <i>get</i> is unacceptable by Rabbinic decree, but not void.</i><br>This ruling does not appear [correct] to me. For although [the <i>get</i>] resembles a forgery, it is not an absolute forgery. [Hence,] since it was transferred in the presence of acceptable witnesses, it is [merely] deemed unacceptable by Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 131:6) quote the Rambam's decision, while mentioning the minority opinion.</i>
[The following rule applies when a person] bringing a <i>get</i> loses it, and then it is discovered. If he lost it in a place not frequented by caravans - even if he found it after a long time had passed - we presume that the <i>get</i> that he lost is the <i>get</i> that was found and [the woman] may be divorced with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following two halachot are dependent on the Rambam's understanding of a Talmudic passage that appears in <i>Gittin</i> 27a and <i>Bava Metzia</i> 18a. Many authorities differ with the Rambam in the interpretation of this passage, and it is their view that is favored by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 132:4), although the Rambam's view is also quoted.<br>According to these views, if there are two people who, with their wives, share the same name in the same town, there is a difficulty if the <i>get</i> was not discovered immediately.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) also mentions a difficulty when a person was seen passing by the place where the <i>get</i> was lost, even when the place is not frequented by caravans and it is not known that two people of the same name live in that place. His ruling is, however, questioned by the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 132:10 and others.</i><br>[However, different rules apply if a <i>get</i>] was lost in a place frequented by caravans. If it was discovered immediately, before any of the passersby had tarried there, or if<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries question why the Rambam requires both factors, that the <i>get</i> was in its original container, and that it could be recognized. Seemingly, one factor alone is sufficient. Indeed, when discussing this issue, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>op. cit.</i>) requires only one factor: either that it was found in the original container, or that the agent could recognize the <i>get</i>.<br>Note the <i>Noda BiY'hudah</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i>, Volume II, Responsum 62), who supports the Rambam's ruling, explaining that there are various levels of recognition. Since the agent recognizes the <i>get</i> only by its length and width, it is necessary that it also be found in its container.</i> it was found in the container in which it was [originally] placed, and the agent can recognize the length and the width of the <i>get</i> that was rolled up within, we can presume that [it is the same <i>get</i>], and [the woman] may be divorced with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that if enough time passed for one of the passersby to wait there, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable.</i>
When it was established that there was another man in that place whose name was the same as the name written in the <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">And whose wife's name is the same as the woman's name written in the <i>get</i>.</i> if another man passes by the place [the <i>get</i> was lost] - even if he did not tarry there - we suspect that the <i>get</i> that was found belongs to the other person.<br>If the woman is divorced with this <i>get</i>, the status of the divorce is doubtful. If, however, another person has not passed by this place, we presume that [the <i>get</i> that was discovered was the one that was lost], even though there are two people with the same name.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that if the name of the husband's town was mentioned, and we know that there is only one couple with the name mentioned in the <i>get</i>, we are not concerned about the fact that caravans frequent the place. We do not suspect that there are two towns with the same name. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 131:16 questions the Ramah's ruling.</i>
If the witnesses knew of a clearly distinctive sign by means of which they could recognize the <i>get</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">A sign is, however, necessary. According to the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), it is sufficient for the agent who lost the <i>get</i> to say that he recognizes it; witnesses, by contrast, must give signs through which it can be identified (<i>Shulchan Aruch, loc. cit.</i>).</i> - e.g., they said it had a hole next to a particular letter, or they said: "We signed only one <i>get</i> with these names" - we presume that [the <i>get</i> that was discovered was the one that was lost], and [the woman] may be divorced with it.<br>[This ruling applies] even when [the <i>get</i>] was discovered after a long time had passed, [it was lost in] a place frequented by caravans, and it was established that there were two [men] with the same names.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that if there are other individuals who share the same name as the witnesses, the witnesses must also recognize their own signatures.</i>
[The following rules apply when] two men [with the same names]<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">And whose wives have the same names.</i> sent <i>gittin</i> together, and they were interchanged [and it is no longer known who sent which <i>get</i>]. Both <i>gittin</i> should be given to each of the women in the presence of witnesses who observe the transfer. [In this manner, both divorces are acceptable.]<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the agent[s] give both <i>gittin</i> to one woman, and thus she receives the <i>get</i> intended for her. They then take the <i>gittin</i> from her and give them to the second woman. There is no necessity for the woman to know which <i>get</i> was intended for her originally (<i>Gittin</i> 86b; <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 132:3).</i><br>Therefore, if one of the <i>gittin</i> was lost, the second <i>get</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (quoted in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) explains that if, however, the agent gives the <i>get</i> that remains to one or the other of the women, the status of the divorce[s] is in doubt.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a man has two names and a woman has two names. When a divorce is initiated, [the husband] should write the names that he and his wife use most frequently and that they are most popularly known by. [The <i>get</i>] should say: "So and so,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., his most popular name.</i> or by whatever names that he is called, divorces so and so or by whatever names she is called."<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to the Rambam, there is no obligation to mention the other names with which the husband or wife is known. Other authorities differ and require that all the names by which a man or a woman is known should be mentioned in the <i>get</i>. This latter view is cited by the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 129:1).<br>This issue is particularly relevant today, when many individuals have Jewish names with which they are called to the Torah, and secular names by which they are known by and large. The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>:16) rules that the Jewish name should be mentioned first, but the secular name should also be mentioned in the <i>get</i>.</i><br>If he writes [the husband's] or [the wife's] nickname,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a derivative of the person's name by which he or she is often called - e.g., Danny, which is a derivative of either Dan or Daniel. Other interpretations of the term <i>chanichah</i> are offered by different commentaries.</i> [the <i>get</i>] is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad states that this applies only when the nickname is more extensively used than the person's actual name. Note the discussion of this issue in the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 129:1. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> also discusses whether the intent is that, after the fact, a woman divorced with such a <i>get</i> may remarry, or whether the intent is that at the outset such a <i>get</i> may be given.</i>
If the name that was less popularly known was written [in the <i>get</i>], and it was also written: "or by whatever names he is called," the <i>get</i> is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">By Rabbinic decree. If, however, both names are mentioned in the <i>get</i>, the <i>get</i> is acceptable despite the fact that the less-used name is mentioned first (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 129:2).</i><br>If the name of [the husband] or [the wife,] or the city in which either of them lives<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">At present, it is not customary to mention the woman's city or the man's city. Instead, the location in which the <i>get</i> is given is mentioned.</i> is written incorrectly, the <i>get</i> is void,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even according to Scriptural law.</i> despite the fact that the phrase "or by whatever names he is called" was also written in the <i>get</i>.
All are fit to write a <i>get</i>, with the exception of five: a gentile, a servant, a deaf-mute, a mentally incompetent person and a minor. A woman may even write her <i>get</i> herself.<br>When a Jew becomes an apostate to false gods,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the manuscript copies of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard printed text was changed by the church's censors.<br>The equivalence between the desecration of the Sabbath and the worship of false gods is discussed by the Rambam at the conclusion of <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i>, based on <i>Horayot</i> 8a.</i> or he desecrates the Sabbath in public, he is regarded like a gentile with respect to all matters [and is not fit to write a <i>get</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, who states that although an apostate is not capable of writing a <i>get</i>, if he is married to a Jewess, he may - and indeed should - divorce her with a <i>get</i>. Just as the <i>kiddushin</i> he gives establish a viable marriage bond (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:15), a <i>get</i> that he gives can dissolve such a bond.</i>
Why are these five individuals [prevented] from writing <i>gittin</i>? Because a <i>get</i> must be written for the sake of the man initiating the divorce and the woman receiving the divorce, and a gentile writes with his own intent.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Even if an observant Jew stands over him, and tells him the intent with which he should write the <i>get</i>, it is void. (See <i>Hilchot Tefillin</i> 1:11.)<br>The Rambam puts the emphasis on the gentile's lack of proper intent to explain why a <i>get</i> written by an apostate is unacceptable. For the Rambam could have employed the same reason given with regard to a servant - that the laws of marriage and divorce do not apply to him - to exclude a gentile. He chose to give this reason to allude to the law concerning an apostate because an apostate also cannot be relied upon to write with the proper intent (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 123:5).<br>The reasons given by the Rambam are significant in another context. As mentioned in the notes on Chapter 2, Halachah 1, there is a difference of opinion among the authorities regarding whether or not the scribe writing the <i>get</i> must be appointed as an agent. All the individuals mentioned are not capable of serving as agents. Since that rationale is not given, it would appear that the Rambam does not require such an appointment. Nevertheless, other sources lead to the opposite conclusion.</i> A deaf-mute, a mentally incompetent person and a minor are not of [sufficient] mental capacity. And the laws of divorce and marriage do not apply to a servant; therefore, [in this area] he is disqualified; he is like a gentile in all respects.<br>If one of these five individuals writes a <i>get</i>, it is void,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 123) quote other opinions, which maintain that such <i>gittin</i> are disqualified by Rabbinic law only. Hence, because of this difference of opinion, the status of the divorce is in doubt (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 123:6).</i> even if it was signed by acceptable witnesses and given in the presence of acceptable witnesses.
When one of these five individuals writes the standard portion of the <i>get</i> and leaves the essential portions - i.e., the place for the man's [name], the woman's [name], the date and the sentence: "Behold, you are permitted [to marry] any man" - unwritten, and these were written by an adult mentally competent Jew with the proper intent, the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 123:3) quotes the opinion of the <i>Tur</i>, who maintains that in such an instance the <i>get</i> is deemed unacceptable by Rabbinic law.</i>
At the outset, one may allow a deaf-mute, a mentally incompetent person or a minor to write the standard portions of a <i>get</i>, provided they are supervised by a mentally competent adult.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If these individuals are supervised, they will write the <i>get</i> with the proper intent. Hence, there is no reason to negate the feasibility of using the standard portions of the <i>get</i> that they wrote, out of fear that they will write the essential portions without the proper intent.</i><br>A gentile or a servant, by contrast, should not be allowed to write the standard portions of a <i>get</i> at the outset, even when supervised by a Jew. For permission to write the standard portions of a <i>get</i> at the outset was granted only as assistance to scribes, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 7 above.</i>
When a man writes a <i>get</i> on the Sabbath or on Yom Kippur, without knowing of the transgression,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">It appears that the Rambam's intent is that the <i>get</i> was written by the husband without his knowing of the transgression and not signed. It was given to his wife in the presence of witnesses. Although the same law would apply if the <i>get</i> were signed in unknowing violation of the holy day, the Rambam does not mention that possibility, because it is highly unlikely that both the man writing the <i>get</i> and the witnesses would not know of the transgression (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> and gives it to [his wife], the divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since the transgression was performed unknowingly, the <i>get</i> is acceptable. (See the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Chulin</i> 1:1.)</i> If it was written and signed on that day<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the standard printed text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. There are other versions that state "If it was written and signed on a holiday...."<br>The difference between the two versions is that the prohibition against forbidden labor is less severe on the holidays than on the Sabbath and Yom Kippur. The holidays are mentioned to emphasize that the <i>get</i> is void even in such an instance.</i> as a willful transgression and given to her, the divorce is not effective. For the witnesses [who signed] are disqualified by Scriptural law.<br>If it was written<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">By the husband and was not signed by witnesses.</i> on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Here a holiday is mentioned rather than the Sabbath, because if a <i>get</i> were written as a willful transgression in public on the Sabbath, the writer would be considered to be an apostate, and a <i>get</i> that he wrote would be disqualified by Scriptural law, as mentioned in Halachah 15. Since the desecration of the holidays is not so severe, the person is not considered to be an apostate. The <i>get</i> he wrote is, however, disqualified by Rabbinic decree.</i> as a willful transgression and given to her in the presence of acceptable witnesses on the holiday, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable [by virtue of Rabbinic decree].

Chapter 4

A <i>get</i> may be written only with a substance that leaves a permanent impression - e.g., ink,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Tefillin</i> 1:4, the Rambam describes the process by which ink is made as follows:<br>One collects the vapor of oils, of tar, of wax or the like, [causes it to condense,] and kneads it together with sap from a tree and a drop of honey. It is moistened extensively, crushed until it is formed into flat cakes, dried and then stored.<br>When one desires to write with it, one soaks [the cakes of ink] in gallnut juice or the like and writes with it. Thus, if one attempts to rub it out, he would be able to.<br></i> <i>sikra, kumus, kankantum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Gittin</i> 2:3, the Rambam mentions three Arabic terms for these words. Rav Kapach explains the meaning as follows: <i>sikra</i> refers to red clay that is used for painting. <i>Kumus</i> and <i>kankantum</i> are two similar substances, yellow and green powders, which when mixed with gallnut juice produce a black substance. Other commentaries offer different interpretations.</i> or the like. If, however, [a <i>get</i>] is written with a substance that does not leave a permanent impression - e.g., beverages, fruit juices or the like - the <i>get</i> is void.<br>If [a <i>get</i>] is written with lead, a stylus or charcoal,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation of these terms is based on the <i>Aruch</i>, as quoted by the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 125:2).</i> it is acceptable. At the outset, however, these substances should not be used.
<i>A priori</i>, we may write with gallnut juice on paper, on a hide or the like. We may not use it to write on parchment that has been treated with gallnut juice, for it will not be distinct. If such a <i>get</i> is written, it is void. The same applies in all similar situations.<br>A <i>get</i> may be written on any substance, even a substance from which one is forbidden to benefit.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 124:1 explains that, <i>a priori</i>, the Rambam allows a <i>get</i> to be written on a substance from which we are forbidden to benefit. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 124:1) differs and follows the opinion of <i>Tosafot</i>, which states that it is only after the fact that such a <i>get</i> is acceptable. The Ramah adds that if we are obligated to destroy the substance on which the <i>get</i> was written, the <i>get</i> is void.</i> We may write [a <i>get</i>] on a substance on which an erasure would not be noticed, provided it is given [to the woman] in the presence of witnesses who observe the transfer.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam rules that a <i>get</i> signed by acceptable witnesses is valid even when it was given without witnesses' observing the transfer (Chapter 1, Halachah 16). In this instance, however, even he maintains that witnesses must observe the transfer, for it is possible that the signatures of the witnesses on the <i>get</i> could be forged (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 124:2).</i>
What is implied? If a <i>get</i> is written on paper with erasures,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The difficulty with a <i>get</i> written on a parchment with erasures is that the text can be erased again and additions or deletions made without making a noticeable difference in the final product. Thus, stipulations could be added to the <i>get</i>, or the signatures of the witnesses altered. Similarly, changes could be made to a <i>get</i> written on any of the other substances that follow.<br>Another type of legal document written on such parchment would not be acceptable. The laws pertaining to a <i>get</i> are different, for in this instance the purpose of the document is not to serve as proof of the divorce, but instead to effect the dissolution of the marriage bond. The purpose of other legal documents, by contrast, is to serve as an account of the transactions they record.</i> on parchment that has not been fully processed, on a shard, on leaves, on the arm of a servant or on the horn of a cow,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 1, Halachah 6, one may not detach the horn from the cow after the <i>get</i> was written on it, before giving the cow to the woman.</i> [it is acceptable, provided the husband] gives [his wife] the servant, the cow,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Normally <i>mesirah</i>, transferring the reigns of an animal, is not an acceptable means of acquiring a cow, as stated in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 3:5. Nevertheless, an exception is made in this instance, because the fundamental purpose of this transaction is not to transfer the cow, but to transfer the <i>get</i> (<i>Hafla'ah</i>; see also the gloss of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> to Chapter 9, Halachah 3).</i> the paper, the parchment or the like in the presence of witnesses.
When the <i>get</i> is tattooed on the hand of a servant,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected by a comparison to the second clause, this law applies when it is known that the servant belonged to the woman (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 124:17). (See the notes on the following halachah.)</i> the signature of the witnesses is also tattooed there,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries question why the signature of the witnesses is significant, for by tattooing the servant they have committed a transgression that disqualifies them from serving as witnesses.</i> and he is in the possession of [the wife], the divorce is effective, even though there are no witnesses to [the servant's] transfer,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the situation parallels the law described in Chapter 1, Halachah 16, which rules that a <i>get</i> signed by acceptable witnesses is acceptable, even when there are no witnesses to its transfer.</i> for [the tattoo] cannot be forged.<br>Even when it is known to us that the servant [previously] belonged to the husband, the <i>get</i> is tattooed on his hand, and he is in the possession of the woman, and she says: "He was given to me in the presence of witnesses," the status of the divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 124:19, which states that if there are witnesses to the transfer of the servant, or the husband admits that the servant was given to the woman, the divorce is effective.</i> For the possession of living beings that can move independently is not considered proof of their ownership.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot To'en V'Nit'an</i> 10:1.</i>
If a <i>get</i> is engraved on a tablet [with a stylus], and it is signed by witnesses, and [the tablet] is in [the woman's] possession, the divorce is effective. [This applies] even when it is known to us that the tablet [had previously] belonged to [the wife].<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Although the article on which the husband has the <i>get</i> written must belong to him, there is no difficulty in this instance, as will be explained.</i> For a woman is allowed to write her <i>get</i> herself. The verification of a <i>get</i> is dependent on [the witnesses who] signed it, if there are no witnesses to its transfer.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that the witnesses who signed the <i>get</i> would have signed it only after verifying that the woman gave the tablet to her husband for the purpose of the divorce.</i>
[The following laws apply when] a <i>get</i> is engraved on a board, on a stone or on a metal plate: If the scribe engraved the form of the letters,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he hewed out the lines that would normally be written.</i> it is acceptable. This is considered writing, as [reflected by] the verse, [Jeremiah 17:1]: "Written with a pen of iron" - i.e., hewed out. Similarly, if one engraved the letters from the back of the plate until they projected from the front of the plate [the <i>get</i> is acceptable].<br>If, however, one hewed out the inside of the letter, [and the area around it,] until the form of the letter protruded on both sides, like the protruding writing on golden <i>dinarim</i>, the <i>get</i> is void, because this is not considered writing.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the letter is formed by working around the letter, and not on the form of the letter itself. This law has a corollary that is applicable to contemporary halachah. If ink spills near a letter, the shape of the letter may not be formed by scratching out the ink blotch (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 125:8).</i>
If one etches out the shape of letters on a hide,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Without writing.</i> or one sketches the form of letters on a hide<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the writing will not remain permanently, but instead will be rubbed out in a short time. For this reason, the Rambam rules in <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 11:16 that sketching on a hide is not considered writing. Nevertheless, in this instance, since there is nothing lacking in the writing itself, the Rambam deems the <i>get</i> acceptable.</i> [the <i>get</i> is acceptable].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 125:5), which mentions that there are some authorities who rule that etching is acceptable and sketching unacceptable, and others who accept sketching and disqualify etching.</i><br>A <i>get</i> may be written with any letters<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one may use Rashi script, rather than ordinary Hebrew writing (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 126:1). Indeed, in one of his responsa, the Rambam writes that his teacher, Rav Yosef Migash, preferred having a <i>get</i> written in Rashi script rather than using the letters used in writing a Torah scroll. The univeral custom at present, however, is to use the Assyrian script with which Torah scrolls are written [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 126:1)].</i> and in any language.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there is no requirement to write it in Hebrew or Aramaic. Nevertheless, for centuries, it has been customary to use the standard Aramaic text quoted by the Rambam later on in this chapter.</i> A <i>get</i> that is written with the letters of one language and whose witnesses sign with the letters of another language is acceptable, provided the witnesses comprehend the language and the letters used.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 1, Halachah 23, the Rambam allows a <i>get</i> to be read for the witnesses. Nevertheless, in this instance the Rambam requires the witnesses to read the <i>get</i> themselves. Since signing in another language is already a departure from the norm, no further leniency is granted (<i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>).</i>
If one of the witnesses signed the <i>get</i> with letters [from one language], and the other signed with letters of another language, the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 9:7 gives an example of one witness signing in Greek and the other in Hebrew. Even though the two languages use different characters, and even require writing in different directions, the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<br>Each of the witnesses' signatures is a separate and independent statement. Therefore, there is no need for the two signatures to be in the same language. The <i>get</i>, by contrast, is a single unit and must be written in one language (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>If, however, a portion of the <i>get</i> is written in one language, and another portion is written in another language, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling is accepted by all authorities. Although the standard text of the <i>get</i> that is universally employed uses both Aramaic and Hebrew, this does not represent a contradiction. Our Sages explain that since both these languages were used at Mount Sinai, they are considered to be a single tongue (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 126:1).</i>
Regardless of the language in which a <i>get</i> is written, the scribe must be careful that the wording of the <i>get</i> does not allow for two meanings. It should not [leave] the reader [in doubt, wondering]: "Perhaps this was his intention" - i.e., something other than divorce - "or perhaps this was his intention" - i.e., divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 13 for examples of wording that could create such doubt.</i> Instead, [whatever] the language [of the <i>get</i>], the wording should unequivocally state one concept: that so and so divorces so and so.
Similarly, regardless of the letters used, the penmanship [of the scribe] must be clear, so that children who know those letters would be able to read it. [The intent is] children who are not overly bright, nor those who are overly slow, but rather those of average intelligence.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Tefillin</i> 1:19, which states that when a question arises concerning the writing for the parchments of the tefillin, a determination may be made on the basis of the reading of such a child.</i><br>The writing should not be crooked, nor incoherent, lest one letter be confused with another, changing the meaning of the text.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 13 for examples of steps taken to prevent such confusion from arising.</i>
If [the wording of a <i>get</i>] has two implications, or its writing is crooked or incoherent to the extent that it is possible to understand a different concept from it, it is unacceptable. [It is not void,] because it may be read as referring to a divorce and its meaning involves divorce.<br>Although <i>a priori</i> it is permissible to write a <i>get</i> in any language, it has already become universal Jewish practice to write <i>gittin</i> in Aramaic, using the following text.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This basic text was already employed during the Talmudic era. At present, the later authorities in both the Sephardic and Ashkenazic communities have suggested minor emendations to the text quoted by the Rambam. As such, there are two standard <i>gittin</i> employed in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> today.</i>
This is the text of the <i>get</i>:<br>On this day of the week, on this day of the month, in this year from creation - or according to the chronology employed for legal documents<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the years dating back to the beginning of Alexander the Great's kingdom, as mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 27.</i> - according to the reckoning that we keep here in [the name of the place where the <i>get</i> is being given], in the following way, I [the husband's name,] the son of [his father's name], from [the name of his city]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the city in which he dwells, not the city in which he was born (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 128:2). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> continues, stating "At present, when we are constantly being exiled and must wander, our place of residence is not defined." Instead, if the man is present at the composition of the <i>get</i>, it is written "who is located at present in this and this place."</i> or by whatever names or nicknames<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Ashkenazi custom is not to employ this phrase and instead to specify all the names by which a person is called (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 129:1).</i> by which I, my father, my place or his place are called, desire out of independent will without anyone forcing me, to dismiss, to release, to divorce you, [the woman's name,] the daughter of [her father's name], from [the name of her city] or by whatever names or nicknames you, your father, your place or his place is known.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The concepts mentioned in the notes above with regard to the husband's place and names also apply with regard to his wife's.</i><br>[Although] previously, you were my wife, now I am dismissing, releasing and divorcing you, so that you have the license and the authority to go and marry any man whom you desire. No one will protest [your actions] from the present day onward. You are free [to marry] any man. This will serve you as a bill of divorce, a <i>get</i> dismissing you, and a letter releasing you, from me, according to the faith of Moses and Israel.<br>The witnesses sign below, as we explained:<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 1, Hilchot 15, 18, <i>et al</i>. In many manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the phrases that follow are omitted, leading some to consider them to be the additions of a printer.</i> [The witness's name] the son of [his father's name], a witness, [the witness's name] the son of [his father's name], a witness.
When a <i>get</i> is written using the above text and wording, [the following rules should be adhered to:] The word ודן should not be written with a <i>yud</i>, so that it might be read as ודין, which might be interpreted as meaning: "There will be a judgment between me and you."<br>The word ואגרת should not be written with a <i>yud</i>, lest it be read as ואי גרת, meaning "If you commit adultery."<br>The word למהך should not be written with a <i>yud</i>, lest it be read as לי מהך - i.e., that it is a joke for me.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Some texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> also include the statement that space should be left between the leg of the <i>heh</i> and its roof. According to that text, לי מהך "for me, from now on."</i><br>The words תהוייין and תצבייין should not be written with only two <i>yuddin</i>, lest a reader get the impression that the man is speaking with two women, and rather than divorce the one, he is divorcing two others.<br>Similarly, he should elongate the [latter] <i>vav</i> in the word וכדו lest it resemble a <i>yud</i>, which would cause the word to be read as <i>uch'dei</i>, in which instance, it would mean that "I am divorcing you on this condition." By the same token, he should elongate the <i>vav</i> in the words תרוכין and שבוקין, lest they resemble <i>yuddin</i>, in which instance it would mean that he is telling her that she is releasing and divorcing him.<br>In a similar fashion, care must be taken in every language and with every form of lettering to make certain that [the wording of the <i>get</i>] does not leave the possibility for two implications.
If [a scribe] wrote [a <i>get</i>] using this text and did not elongate the <i>vavin</i> mentioned, did not write the extra <i>yuddin</i>, or wrote the <i>yuddin</i> that we said should not be written, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that the <i>get</i> is unacceptable only when the husband protests and maintains that he intentionally had the scribe make such a mistake. If he does not issue such a protest, the <i>get</i> is acceptable. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> also mentions that there is a difference if the woman subsequently married on the basis of the <i>get</i> or not, and the <i>Tur</i> states that the <i>get</i> may be disqualified on this basis only if the husband writes it himself, or the scribe writes it in response to his explicit instructions.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 126:22) quotes the Rambam's ruling, but also mentions that of the Ra'avad and the <i>Tur</i>. An exception, however, is made with regard to writing the three <i>yuddin</i> in the words תצבייין and תהוייין. If only two <i>yuddin</i> are written, the <i>get</i> is acceptable. (See also the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 126:32.)</i> Similarly, [if a <i>get</i> is written] in another language, and imprecise wording or writing is used, it is unacceptable.
[The following rules apply if] a letter or a word of a <i>get</i> was rubbed out or written between the lines. If it is from the standard portion of the <i>get</i>, it is acceptable. If it is from the portions of the <i>get</i> that are of fundamental importance, the <i>get</i> is void. If, however, at the conclusion of the <i>get</i>, it is stated that the particular letter has been written between the lines or has been rubbed out, the <i>get</i> is acceptable, as other legal documents would be,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 27:8.</i> even if [the difficulty] concerns the portions of the <i>get</i> that are of fundamental importance.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 125:19) states that at present it is customary to discard a <i>get</i> with a portion that has been rubbed out, instead of adding this line at the conclusion of the <i>get</i>. If, however, there is no alternative, a <i>get</i> is acceptable with an erasure, even if there is no explanation at the end, for we rely on the witnesses who observe the transfer (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 125:35).</i><br>Similarly, if a <i>get</i> was discovered to be torn [both] horizontally and vertically, as is customary for a court [to tear a legal document], the <i>get</i> is void, as would be other legal documents.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad states that if the <i>get</i> had been certified by the court, it is acceptable even if it is torn in the above manner. The rationale is that we assume that the <i>get</i> was torn so that the woman would not be able to use it to demand payment of her <i>ketubah</i> a second time. The Rambam's ruling is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 125:20), and the Ra'avad's clarification is mentioned by the <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 125:41.</i> If, however, it is torn in a way other than the manner customary for a court [to tear a legal document], it is acceptable.
If [the <i>get</i>] has faded, rotted or become [filled with holes like] a sieve, it is acceptable. [The following rules apply if] it has been rubbed out or the letters have become blurred, but their form remains. If it can be read, it is acceptable;<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected by the following halachah, this refers to an instance in which the <i>get</i> was given when it was acceptable, and its condition deteriorated afterwards.</i> if not, it is void.
When does the above<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the disqualifications mentioned in the previous two halachot.</i> apply? When the woman is in possession of a <i>get</i> that has been signed by witnesses, and there are no witnesses to its transfer. If, however, there are witnesses who testify that the <i>get</i> was transferred in their presence, and at that time it was acceptable, the divorce is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This represents a difference between <i>gittin</i> and other legal documents, as reflected in Halachah 3 and notes (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i><br>[This applies] even if the fundamental portions of the <i>get</i> were written on erased parchment or between the lines, or [the <i>get</i>] was torn [both] horizontally and vertically when it was given to [the woman] in their presence.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> emphasizes that if the <i>get</i> is faded to the point that it cannot be read at the time it is transferred, the fact that the transfer is observed by witnesses is to no avail, and the divorce is void.<br>He also mentions the opinion of the Rashba, who states that a <i>get</i> that is torn vertically and horizontally before it is given to the woman is not acceptable. He does not, however, accept that decision. His rulings are paralleled by those of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 125:21), which mentions both the Rambam's and the Rashba's views, but appears to favor that of the Rambam.</i>
[The following rules apply when] five men write a single <i>get</i> to divorce their five wives. The <i>get</i> is acceptable if the wording they use is inclusive - i.e., they wrote "On the day of the week, so and so divorced so and so, and so and so divorced so and so..." - every husband making a statement to his wife. Similarly, each of them told his wife: "so that you have the license to...," including the entire standard portion of the <i>get</i>. Two witnesses must sign below.<br>[This <i>get</i>] must be given to each of the women in the presence of witnesses who observe its transfer. If no witnesses observe its transfer at all, the woman who is in possession of the get is considered to be divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The others, by contrast, are not considered to be divorced, for we have no proof that the woman's husband in fact gave her this get. (See <i>Gittin</i> 86b.)</i><br>[Different rules apply if,] by contrast, [the scribe] wrote: "On the day of the week, so and so divorced so and so...," completing the entire text of the <i>get</i> and then began writing a second <i>get</i> directly under it in the same scroll. [He continued] writing: "On the day of the week, so and so divorced so and so...," and so completed the entire text of the second <i>get</i> and similarly completed all the <i>gittin</i> in this manner [on the same scroll], and the witnesses signed below [the final <i>get</i>].<br>If this scroll was given to each of the women in the presence of witnesses who observed the transfer, all their divorces are effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., no attention is paid to the witnesses who signed the get. Each of the women received a <i>get</i> that was written in acceptable manner, and whose transfer was observed by witnesses. This is sufficient for a divorce to be acceptable.</i> [More stringent rules apply] if there are no witnesses who observed the transfer, and the scroll is in the possession of one of the women: If hers was the last <i>get</i> written on the scroll and the signatures of the witnesses are read together with [that statement], her divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">For hers is an acceptable <i>get</i>, signed by witnesses. We are not concerned with the fact that the scroll contains other <i>gittin</i> above it.</i> If the scroll is in the possession of one of the women whose <i>gittin</i> precede the last one, the status of her divorce is in doubt.
If [the scribe] writes: "We, so and so and so and so, divorce our wives, so and so and so and so,..." and then completes the <i>get</i>, the <i>get</i> is void, even though it was given to each one of them in the presence of witnesses who observed its transfer. [The rationale:] two women cannot be divorced with the same <i>get</i>, as [we can infer from Deuteronomy 24:1]: "And he shall write for her," [implied is:] for her and not for her and her colleague.<br>If after [writing the text as above], he continued and wrote individual statements for each one within the <i>get</i>, stating, "So and so is divorcing so and so, and so and so is divorcing so and so on this date,"<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>get</i> is acceptable only if the date that is stated in the beginning of the <i>get</i> is restated in the individual statement written for each woman. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 130:13.)</i> the divorces are acceptable.
[The following rules apply when a scribe] wrote two <i>gittin</i> in two columns on one scroll side by side. If there are two witnesses [whose signatures] are read [as a continuation of] one <i>get</i>, and two witnesses [whose signatures] are read [as a continuation of] the other <i>get</i>, the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>get</i> is given to both women in the presence of witnesses who observe the transfer, both are divorced (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> Whichever woman is in possession of the <i>get</i> is divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Even when there are no witnesses who observe the transfer.</i>
[In the above instance, if the <i>gittin</i>] were signed by only two witnesses, who signed across the width of both <i>gittin</i>, the divorce is valid only when the scroll is in the possession of the woman under whose <i>get</i> the signatures appear.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., under the first column, the witness signed "Ya'akov, the son of," and in the second column he continued "Yitzchak, a witness." The second witness did the same. In this instance, the witnesses have signed under the first <i>get</i>. If the witnesses signed their first names under one column, and signed "the son of so and so" in the second column, both <i>gittin</i> would be acceptable. For "the son of Ya'akov" is an acceptable signature."<br>This interpretation is based on the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 9:6). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:8) offers a slightly different interpretation.</i><br>If the <i>scroll</i> is in the possession of the woman under whose <i>get</i> the signatures of the witnesses do not appear, the <i>get</i> is not acceptable unless it is given to her in the presence of witnesses [who observe the transfer].<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the fact that the names of the fathers of the witnesses are located under the woman's <i>get</i> does not cause it to be considered a <i>get</i> with false signatures. (See Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:8.)</i>
[The following rules apply if a scribe] writes two <i>gittin</i>, one above the other, and the witnesses sign between the two <i>gittin</i> - their signatures thus being below the first <i>get</i> and above the second <i>get</i>. If the scroll is in the possession of the woman under whose <i>get</i> the signatures appear, her divorce is valid, and the woman above whose <i>get</i> the signatures appear is not divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">These laws apply even when there are no witnesses who observed the transfer of the scroll. If there are witnesses who observed the transfer of the scroll to the second woman, even the second <i>get</i> is acceptable.<br>If the scroll is in the possession of the woman above whose <i>get</i> the witnesses sign, both the <i>gittin</i> are unacceptable unless there are witnesses who observed their transfer.</i><br>If the witnesses signed at the top of a <i>get</i>, at its side or on its overside, it is not a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:4) states that the <i>get</i> is <i>pasul</i>, unacceptable. That term implies that it is the Rabbis who disqualified the use of such a <i>get</i>, while according to Scriptural law it is acceptable.</i> If it was given to [the woman] in the presence of witnesses, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that the witnesses did not sign in the proper place does not make the <i>get</i> invalid.</i>
If two <i>gittin</i> [were written in an arc,] with the beginning of one <i>get</i> facing the beginning of the other <i>get</i>, and the signature of the witnesses is located between the tops of both <i>gittin</i>, both are void. If they are transferred in the presence of witnesses, both are acceptable.
[The following rules apply if the scribe did not complete the text of the <i>get</i> in one column,] left a portion unwritten and wrote it at the top of a second column. If the witnesses signed below, at the end of the second column, it is acceptable, provided it is obvious that the scroll was not cut off, and it was the scribe's intent to complete the <i>get</i> in the second column.<br>If, however, this is not apparent, the status of the divorce is in doubt, even if the <i>get</i> was given in the presence of witnesses. [The rationale is that] perhaps there were two <i>gittin</i>, and the portion below the <i>get</i> in the first column and a portion above the <i>get</i> in the second column were cut off.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">And thus a portion of the <i>get</i> that the woman receives would not have been written for her sake. For this reason, even if the get was given in the presence of witnesses who observed the transfer, it is not acceptable unless the witnesses testify that the <i>get</i> was originally written in two columns (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
If, after writing the <i>get</i> and completing it, [the scribe] wrote: "Give regards to so and so," "Greetings to you, so and so, my friend," or the like, and afterwards the witnesses signed beneath the greeting, the status of the divorce is doubtful,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:3) uses the term <i>pasul</i>, "unacceptable," meaning that the <i>get</i> was disqualified by Rabbinic decree alone. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 130:4.)</i> despite the fact that the woman is in possession of the <i>get</i>. [The rationale is] perhaps the witnesses signed only for the greeting [without paying attention to the <i>get</i>].<br>If, however, the scribe wrote: "And give regards to so and so," "And greetings to you, so and so, my friend," or the like, the <i>get</i> is acceptable. Since [the greeting] accompanies the <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The word "and" (in Hebrew, the letter <i>vav</i>) establishes a connection between the two, and the greeting is not considered to be a separate entity.</i> [we presume that the witnesses] signed with both these thoughts in mind.<br>If [the <i>get</i>] was given to her in the presence of witnesses, it is acceptable in either instance.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In the first instance, even if we were to assume that the intent of the witnesses was to sign on the greeting, that does not disqualify the <i>get</i>. It is acceptable when given in the presence of witnesses.</i>

Chapter 5

The Torah's expression, [Deuteronomy  24:1]: "He will... place it in her hand," need not be interpreted only [according to its strict literal meaning], that the <i>get</i> must be placed in her hand. Regardless of whether the <i>get</i> is placed in her hand, her bosom or her courtyard, or is given to her agent whom she charged that his hand would be as her hand, the same law applies.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Numbers 21:26, which uses the word <i>miyado</i> - literally, "from his hand" - as meaning "from his possession." A similar usage is frequent within the Torah and within Talmudic sources.<br>It must, however, be emphasized that the accepted practice is to place the <i>get</i> in the woman's hand and not to remain content with depositing it in her courtyard. (See Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:14.)</i><br>The same laws apply to a courtyard that she acquired, one that she rented or one that is lent to her. They are all considered to be her property, and once the <i>get</i> reaches her property, the divorce is effective.
[The following laws apply when a man] throws a <i>get</i> for his wife into her courtyard. If she is standing next to her courtyard, the divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to a courtyard, a point of clarification is necessary. In general, the benefit from a woman's property belongs to her husband, and whatever is acquired by her property belongs to him. In this instance, however, we say that the acquisition of the bill of the divorce and the right to acquire the articles placed in her property become hers simultaneously.</i> If she is not standing next to her courtyard, the divorce is not effective until she stands next to her courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording implies, however, that when the woman returns to her courtyard, the divorce is effective. Rav Meir HaLevi, as quoted by the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139) differs and maintains that the woman must be present at the time the <i>get</i> is placed in her courtyard. If she is not present at that time, Rav Meir HaLevi maintains that the divorce is not effective unless the husband waits and tells the woman: "This is your <i>get</i>," when she picks it up, or the husband must pick it up and give it to her again.<br>The Rashba submits a third opinion. He maintains that the status of the divorce is doubtful, for perhaps the woman desired the divorce. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:2-3 and commentaries.)</i><br>[These principles apply] even when the <i>get</i> will be guarded in the courtyard in which [it is placed]. [The rationale is that] divorce is considered to be a liability [and not an advantage] for [a woman]. And a liability may not be invoked against [a person] outside his [or her] presence.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">When considering something which is to a person's advantage - e.g., the acquisition of an ownerless object - a person may perform this act on behalf of a colleague without his knowledge, for our Sages postulated: "one may act to a person's advantage outside his presence." The rationale is that the person would probably desire to acquire the object. When, however, a liability is considered, this principle does not apply. Nevertheless, if a woman explicitly states that a <i>get</i> should be placed in her courtyard, the divorce is effective even when she is not standing next to it (<i>Beit Yosef, Even HaEzer</i> 139).</i>
[These rules apply when a <i>get</i> is given in the following situation. A woman] is standing on her roof, and [her husband] is standing in his courtyard that is located below it. He throws a <i>get</i> upwards to her.<br>Once the <i>get</i> reaches the space of the guardrail [to her roof] or comes within three handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In many different halachic contexts, three handbreadths of empty space is considered to be a continuation of the nearest significant halachic entity. This principle is referred to by the name <i>l'vud</i>.</i> of the roof [when her roof does not have a guardrail], the divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since it will ultimately come to rest within her property, it is considered as though it has already come to rest.<br>Accordingly, from this time onward, even if the husband changes his mind and desires to nullify the divorce before the <i>get</i> reaches the ground, he no longer has that option. The divorce is effective and the marriage bond has been broken. If a second man consecrates her at this time, she is considered to be his wife (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> [This applies provided the <i>get</i> ultimately] comes to rest on her roof.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Most other authorities differ with the Rambam on this point and maintain that as soon as the <i>get</i> enters the woman's possession, the divorce is effective, even though afterwards it is consumed by fire or its writing is wiped away by water, provided the fire or the water was not there before the husband threw the <i>get</i>. [Significantly, in his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 8:3), the Rambam does not mention the provision that ultimately the <i>get</i> must come to rest.] Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:4-5) mentions the Rambam's view, it is the approach of the other authorities that is favored.</i><br>If, however, [the writing of the <i>get</i>] is erased or [the <i>get</i>] is consumed by fire before it reaches her, the divorce is not effective. [This applies] even if the <i>get</i> is erased or consumed by fire after it passes the barriers of the woman's property or after it reaches within three handbreadths of her roof. [The rationale is that] since the <i>get</i> will never come to rest [in a complete state], it is void.
[Different rules apply when] the roof belongs to the husband and he stands on it, while his wife stands below in a courtyard that belongs to her, and he throws her a <i>get</i>. Once the <i>get</i> passes the boundaries of his property and enters the boundaries of her property, where she is standing, the divorce is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., immediately, even if the <i>get</i> does not reach the woman's property. The rationale is that as long as the conditions mentioned in the following halachah are met, it may be assumed that the laws of gravity will prevail, and the <i>get</i> will come to rest in the woman's property. Therefore, even if it is destroyed by a fluke occurrence beforehand, the divorce is effective (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 139:6).</i>
If, however, he throws a get into a fire located on the woman's property and it is consumed by the flames, or into water and it is erased or lost, the <i>get</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since he initially threw the <i>get</i> into the fire, it is considered as though it will never come to rest and will never enter the woman's possession in a complete state.</i> If, however, it enters her property (and comes to rest),<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The phrase set off by parentheses is found in the standard printed texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, but is lacking in many authentic manuscripts and early printings. Eliminating the phrase would imply that there is a difference between the situation described in Halachah 4 and that described in Halachah 3.<br>This indeed is the <i>Maggid Mishneh's</i> contention. As he explains, in Halachah 3 the husband is throwing the <i>get</i> against the natural gravitational pattern that prevails within the world, while in Halachah 4 the <i>get</i> is being thrown in a manner that conforms with the pattern of gravity. Therefore, as long as the <i>get</i> was not thrown into fire or water, the divorce is binding as soon as the <i>get</i> enters the woman's domain.</i> and afterwards fire comes and consumes it, the divorce is effective.
When the husband tosses the <i>get</i> on top of a reed or a spear implanted in her domain,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries on <i>Gittin</i> 79a explain that this refers to an instance where the top of the reed or the spear is higher than the walls of the courtyard.</i> the divorce is not effective until [the <i>get</i>] comes to rest in a place that is protected by her.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">For the wind may blow it outside the courtyard.</i><br>[The following rule applies when] there are two courtyards, the inner courtyard belongs to the woman, the outer courtyard belongs to the man, and the walls of the outer courtyard are taller than those of the inner courtyard. If [the husband] throws the <i>get</i> into the space [of the inner courtyard, above its walls, but lower than the walls] of the outer courtyard, the divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>get</i> will be protected by the walls of the outer courtyard even within the inner courtyard, if it is thrown toward the inner courtyard the divorce is effective after it enters the space of the outer courtyard (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:9).</i> [The rationale is] the inner [courtyard] is protected by the walls of the outer [courtyard]. This concept does not apply with regard to containers.
What is implied?<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">There are two differences between courtyards and containers located in the husband's domain: a) With regard to the courtyard, it is sufficient for the <i>get</i> to enter the space of the husband's courtyard. With regard to a container, by contrast, this is not sufficient.<br>b) Even when a <i>get</i> comes to rest within a container, the divorce is not effective, except in the specific instance mentioned by the Rambam.<br>The rationale for both these differences is that the walls of a courtyard are made primarily for protecting the articles contained within, and in the above instance the walls of the outer courtyard also protect the articles within the inner courtyard. In contrast, the function of the wall of a container is to hold the object it contains (Rashi, <i>Gittin</i> 79b).</i> There are two containers, located one inside the other [and both are located in the husband's domain].<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The bracketed addition is made on the basis of the gloss of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>. There are other commentaries that maintain that the containers are located in the woman's domain or in a neutral domain. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 139:13 and the gloss of Rabbi Akiva Eiger on those comments.)</i> The inner container belongs to her and the outer container belongs to him. If [the husband] throws the <i>get</i> to [his wife] toward the containers, the divorce is not effective<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The fundamental principle involved here is that a person cannot acquire an object by virtue of its being placed in a container belonging to him, if that container is located in a domain belonging to the giver, unless the giver is not concerned with the presence of the recipient's container. (See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 4:1.)<br>It must be emphasized that this concept is not accepted by Rabbenu Asher and the <i>Tur</i>. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Choshen Mishpat</i> 200:3 and commentaries.)</i> - even when the <i>get</i> reaches the space of the inner container - until it comes to rest on the side of the inner container.<br>[Moreover,] the above applies only when [the woman's container] is lying on its side and does not have a bottom.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">When a container is lying on its side and it does not have a bottom, it will not serve a functional purpose. Therefore, the husband will not be concerned that it is placed in his property. As such, if the <i>get</i> comes to rest on it, the divorce is effective.</i> If it has a bottom, even if [the <i>get</i>] comes to rest on that bottom, the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the woman's container will serve a functional purpose. Therefore, it is likely that the husband will object to its presence on his property.<br>It must be emphasized that <i>Tosafot,</i> the Ra'avad, Rabbenu Nissim and others interpret the passage in <i>Gittin, loc. cit.,</i> differently from the Rambam. It is, however, the Rambam's view that is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:9).</i> For when a container belonging to a woman is located in the domain of the husband, the woman may not acquire a <i>get</i> by means of it unless the husband is not concerned about the place it occupies.
When [a husband] throws a <i>get</i> to his wife while she is located in his house or in his courtyard, the divorce is not effective until the <i>get</i> reaches the woman's hand or a container that belongs to her, to whose presence within his domain the husband does not object - e.g., a small bottle or basket or the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Gittin</i> 77a) explains that a man must assume that a woman will bring such articles with her when she enters a domain. Therefore, the husband's acceptance of the woman's presence also implies a willingness to allow her to bring in such articles.</i><br>Similarly, if the <i>get</i> reaches a couch belonging to her<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the couch belongs to the husband, the divorce is not effective unless the <i>get</i> is given to the woman, even when the woman is sitting on the couch.</i> on which she is sitting that is ten handbreadths high, the divorce is effective. For [the couch] is considered to be a separate domain,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since the couch is ten handbreadths (80 centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, 96 centimeters according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>) high, it is considered to be a separate entity. (See a parallel concept in <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 3:9.)</i> and the husband does not object to the place taken by its legs.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, which explains that in other situations - e.g., a couch belonging to a purchaser that is in the domain of a seller - this concept would not necessarily apply, and the transfer would not be considered effective. (Note <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 139:15.)</i>
[The following rules apply when] the husband lends his wife a place in his courtyard [for the purpose of receiving her <i>get</i>], without defining its borders.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">If he specified a place, the <i>get</i> would have to be deposited in that place.</i> If he throws her the <i>get</i> and it reaches within four cubits of where she is standing, the divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For we assume that the place where the woman is standing is the place that she was granted.</i><br>When [in the above instance, the <i>get</i>] rolled away [from the place where the woman is standing,] and fell on a beam or on a rock further removed from her,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the interpretation of the Rambam's ruling offered by Rabbenu Nissim. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> questions the Rambam's rulings, interpreting his approach as implying that if the place where the <i>get</i> comes to rest is within four cubits of the woman, it would appear that the divorce should be effective even if any of these factors applies. And if the place is further removed, the divorce is never effective. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:11) rules according to other authorities, who interpret this passage according to the interpretation offered by Rabbenu Nissim. The interpretation of the Rambam's words offered by the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> is mentioned as a minority opinion.</i> [the following rules apply]. If the place where [the <i>get</i>] fell is not four cubits by four cubits, nor is it ten [handbreadths] high, nor does it have a separate name of its own, it is not considered to be a distinct entity, and it is as if [the <i>get</i>] and [the woman] were in the same place. [Therefore, the divorce is effective.]<br>If the place is characterized by any of these three factors, it is considered to be a distinct entity. [Since] the husband lent the woman one place, but not two places, the divorce is not effective until the <i>get</i> reaches her hand.
If [the husband] throws the <i>get</i> to her [while she is in her domain], and [the <i>get</i>] passes through the domain and falls outside her domain [the divorce is not effective].
