Even if the <i>get</i> passes within three [handbreadths] of the ground,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the principle of <i>l'vud</i>, three handbreadths is an extension of any given entity. Nevertheless, since, as happened, the <i>get</i> did not come to rest within the woman's domain, the fact that it passed through it close to the ground is not significant. This same principle is cited in several other contexts - e.g., <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 4:9. See also <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 13:16.</i> the divorce is not effective<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139) maintains that, as appears from <i>Bava Metzia</i> 12a, the matter is the subject of an unresolved Talmudic debate. Hence, the status of the divorce is a matter of doubt, and although the woman may not consider herself to be divorced, she may not continue living with her first husband, and if she was consecrated by another person, he is required to divorce her before she may marry anyone else.</i> until [the <i>get</i>] comes to rest in her domain.
[The following rule applies when a woman] is standing on her roof, [her husband] throws her [a <i>get</i>], and [instead of reaching her,] it falls on a roof [belonging to another person].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the gloss of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>. The Ra'avad and others interpret <i>Gittin</i> 79b, the source for this halachah, as referring to two roofs belonging to the husband, and explain that it teaches a concept similar to that reflected by Halachah 9. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:12) mentions the Rambam's view, it favors the other interpretation.</i> If the woman can stretch out her hand and take it, the divorce is effective.<br>[The rationale is that] although the domains are divided above as they are divided below, [and thus the <i>get</i> is located on a domain that does not belong to the woman, this is not significant,] for people are not concerned with [a neighbor's making use of] their property in such a manner.
[The following rules apply if a woman holds] her hand at an incline, [her husband] throws a <i>get</i> to her hand, and it falls to the earth. If it falls within four cubits of where she is standing and comes to rest there, the divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In certain circumstances, our Sages ordained although a person is standing in property that does not belong to him, the four cubits around him [or her] are considered to be his courtyard. And placing an article within that space is considered to be placing it in his domain. (See <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 4:9.) Therefore, just as if the <i>get</i> in a woman's courtyard had come to rest in the woman's courtyard, the divorce would be effective, so too it is effective if it comes to rest within four cubits of her.<br>If the <i>get</i> does not come to rest within four cubits of the woman, one might say that the outcome is dependent on the difference of opinion between the Rambam and the <i>Tur</i> mentioned in the notes on Halachah 10. In this instance, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:15) follows the opinion of the <i>Tur</i> and rules that the status of the divorce is doubtful.</i><br>When, [however, the <i>get</i>] falls into the sea or into a fire, the divorce is not effective, if she is standing next to the water or the fire [when the <i>get</i> is thrown to her]. [The rationale is] that, at the outset, it would be destroyed as it fell.
[The following rules apply when a husband] throws a <i>get</i> to [his wife] in the public domain or in a domain that does not belong to either of them. If [the <i>get</i>] is "close to him," the divorce is not effective. If [the <i>get</i>] is partially "close to him," and partially "close to her," the status of the divorce is in doubt unless it is definitely close to her. If [the <i>get</i>] is close enough to her that she can bend down and pick it up, the divorce is unacceptable [according to Rabbinic decree].<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, if the woman erred and remarried she is not forced to divorce her second husband. If, however, she remarried when the status of the divorce is in doubt, she may not remain married to her second husband.</i> [Only when] the <i>get</i> reaches her hand may she remarry on this basis <i>a priori</i>.
What is meant by "close to him"? That he could protect the <i>get</i> while she could not. If both are able to protect [the <i>get</i>], or both are unable to protect it, it is considered to be partially "close to him," and partially "close to her."<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam does not mention an instance where the woman can protect the <i>get</i> but the husband cannot, and yet it is not close enough to her for her to bend down and pick it up. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the Rambam would maintain that in such an instance the status of the divorce is doubtful.<br>Although with regard to <i>kiddushin</i>, in such an instance the Rambam rules that a marriage bond is established (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:22), the laws are more stringent with regard to divorce. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 139:22 states that most other authorities would differ and would agree that according to Scriptural law, the divorce is effective in such an instance; it is disqualified only by virtue of Rabbinic decree.</i>
[If a husband] comes to a place first and stands there, and then [his wife] comes and stands opposite him, the divorce is not effective if he throws her [a <i>get</i>] and [it falls] within his four cubits,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Whenever an area becomes considered to be the "four cubits" belonging to one individual, another person may not acquire an article in that area until the first person departs (<i>Gittin</i> 78a).</i> even if she can bend down and pick it up.<br>If she comes to the place first and stands, and then he comes and stands opposite her and throws it to her, even when [the <i>get</i>] is partially "close to him" and partially "close to her," the <i>get</i> is [merely] deemed unacceptable [by virtue of Rabbinic decree] because it is within her four cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to Scriptural law the divorce is effective, because the four cubits in which the woman is standing become her exclusive property.</i> [Only when] the <i>get</i> reaches her hand [is the divorce effective <i>a priori</i>].
[The following rules apply when a husband] throws a <i>get</i> that is tied with a string into [his wife's] hand and he remains holding the other end of the string. If he can pull [the <i>get</i> from her hand] and bring it back to him,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 138:2) interprets the intent as being that the knot tying the string to the <i>get</i> must be attached in a manner strong enough to allow the <i>get</i> to be pulled back to the husband. If it is tied loosely, the divorce is effective. If, however, it is the woman who prevents the husband from drawing back the <i>get</i> by clasping it within her hand, the divorce is not effective. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> does, however, mention another view, which rules the divorce acceptable in such an instance.</i> the divorce is not effective<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 78b also derives this concept from the description of a divorce in Deuteronomy 24:3: "And he will write a bill of divorce for her, place it in her hand...." As long as the husband can retake possession of the bill of divorce, he has not severed his connection with the woman.</i> until he snaps the string.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the husband does snap the string, the Rambam rules the divorce to be effective. Other commentaries differ, explaining that since the giving of the <i>get</i> was not carried out in the proper manner, the <i>get</i> must be given again for the divorce to be acceptable.</i> If he cannot pull [the <i>get</i> from her], the divorce is effective.
[The following rules apply when a husband] gives [a woman's] <i>get</i> to a servant belonging to her. If he is awake, he is bound and she is guarding him, the divorce is effective. It is considered to be as if [the <i>get</i>] were placed in a courtyard belonging to her and she is standing at its side. If [the servant] is not bound, the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since the servant has an independent will, he is considered to be a courtyard that is not guarded by the woman herself. Also, the conception of a domain as equivalent to a person's hand applies only when that domain remains still and not when, as in the case of a servant, it moves.<br>Based on a difference in the version of <i>Gittin</i> 78a, the source for this halachah, the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139) and others differ with the Rambam and maintain that it is necessary for the servant to be both bound and asleep for the divorce to be effective. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 139:16) quotes both opinions, the Rambam's view appears to be favored.</i><br>If [the husband] places [the <i>get</i>] in the servant's hand while he is sleeping, and [the woman] is guarding him, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable [by virtue of Rabbinic decree].<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since the servant was not bound, the Sages disqualified the divorce, lest one give a <i>get</i> to a servant while awake and unbound.</i> If [the servant] is bound, the divorce is acceptable.
[The following rules apply if a husband] writes a <i>get</i>, places it in the hand of a servant belonging to him and writes a deed for the woman, giving her the servant as a present. If the servant is bound, the divorce is effective, for when she acquires the servant she also acquires the <i>get</i>. If the servant is unbound and awake, [the woman] acquires the servant; the divorce, however, is not effective until the <i>get</i> reaches her hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording implies that once the servant gives her the <i>get</i>, however, the divorce is effective. It is not necessary for the husband to take the <i>get</i> from the servant and give it to her. As mentioned by the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 139:29, this point is not accepted by all authorities.</i><br>Similarly, if [a man] placed a <i>get</i> in a courtyard belonging to him and sold or gave the courtyard to [his wife], the divorce is effective once she acquires the courtyard by virtue of the transfer of a deed or money, or by manifesting her ownership over it.

Chapter 6

An agent who is appointed by a woman to receive her <i>get</i> from her husband is called a receiving agent (<i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>). When the <i>get</i> reaches this agent's hand, the divorce is completed,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">And from that time onward, the husband cannot recant.</i> as if it has reached the hands of the woman herself.<br>[The agent] must be appointed in the presence of two witnesses, and two witnesses must be present when the <i>get</i> is conveyed to the agent. Even if the second pair of witnesses is the same as the first pair, or one of them is from the first pair, they are acceptable as witnesses.
When does the above<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">That is, when does the requirement for witnesses apply?</i> apply? When the <i>get</i> is lost or torn. If, however, the <i>get</i> is in the possession of the <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>, there is no need for witnesses. This applies whether the <i>get</i> was given by the husband in private, or it was given in the presence of witnesses. The presence of the <i>get</i> in the possession of the agent is equivalent to its presence in the possession of the woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 16.</i> Nevertheless, <i>a priori</i>, the <i>get</i> should be given only in the presence of witnesses who observe its transfer, as is the case with regard to [a <i>get</i> given to] the woman herself.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 15.</i>
A husband may not appoint an agent to receive a <i>get</i> for his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A divorce is considered to be undesirable and detrimental for a woman. Therefore, an agent cannot be appointed for her without her consent. For an activity that is detrimental to a person cannot be performed on his or her behalf without his will (<i>Gittin</i> 62b).</i> He may, however, appoint an agent to deliver a <i>get</i> to his wife. Such an agent is referred to as a delivery agent (<i>sh'liach holachah</i>).
Similarly, a woman may appoint an agent to fetch her <i>get</i> for her from her husband. Such an agent is referred to as an agent who fetches (<i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>). Neither a <i>sh'liach holachah</i> nor a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i> need [be appointed in the presence of] witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:15, where the Rambam states: "For the only purpose witnesses would serve with regard to the agency... is to make known the truth of the matter."<br>According to the Rambam, the distinction between these agents and a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> appointed by the woman is that through the acceptance of the <i>get</i> the marriage bonds are annulled, and "no matters involving forbidden sexual relations [are established if] fewer than two witnesses are present (<i>Yevamot</i> 88a)." Since the <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> is concerned only with receiving the <i>get</i>, his agency involves nothing else but the actual divorce. Hence, witnesses must be present to acknowledge the appointment of this agent. In contrast, the agency of a <i>sh'liach holachah</i> and a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i> also involves transporting the <i>get</i>, an activity that is merely a preparatory step for the divorce. Hence, witnesses are not required for his appointment.<br>It must be emphasized that the Ra'avad and others offer a far more straightforward rationale clarifying the distinction between these agents. A <i>sh'liach holachah</i> and a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i> each carry the <i>get</i> with them. Hence, they need no further proof of their agency. A <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>, by contrast, does not have possession of the <i>get</i> before the divorce. Hence, his position must be strengthened by having his appointment observed by witnesses.<br>In practice, it has already become customary for the appointment of a <i>sh'liach holachah</i> to be certified by a written document. There are opinions that differ and require that the appointment of a <i>sh'liach holachah</i> also be observed by witnesses. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:11, 30, 40.)</i>
A woman is not divorced through the medium of a <i>get</i> sent by her husband or brought by a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i> [whom she appointed] until the <i>get</i> reaches her hand.<br>Whenever the term agent is used with regard to a <i>get</i> without any further explanation, the intent is a <i>sh'liach holachah</i> or a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>.
Anyone is acceptable to act as an agent with regard to a divorce, whether as a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>, a <i>sh'liach holachah</i> or a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements are intended to negate the opinion of his teacher, Rabbi Yosef Migash, who maintains that a servant may serve as a <i>sh'liach holachah</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:31) quotes both views, but appears to favor the Rambam's view.</i> with the exception of five individuals: a gentile, a servant, a deaf mute, a mentally incompetent individual and a minor.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 3, Halachah 15, and <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:17.</i> If one of these individuals brings or receives [a <i>get</i>], the divorce is not effective.
Even women, relatives<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:33) states that <i>a priori</i>, it is proper that the agent not share any family ties with the husband, his wife, or the judges executing the divorce.</i> and individuals who are disqualified [from serving as witnesses] because of the violation of Rabbinic law are acceptable to serve as agents for a divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">It would appear that even if the signatures of the witnesses have not been verified, and the acceptability of a <i>get</i> depends on the agents' statements, these individuals may serve as agents.</i> Individuals who are disqualified because of the violation of Scriptural law, by contrast, are not acceptable [to serve as agents] to deliver a <i>get</i>. If they bring it, the divorce is unacceptable.<br>When does the above<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">That the divorce is unacceptable, but not void.</i> apply? When the signatures on the <i>get</i> have been verified.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">As can be seen in the various manuscript copies of the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 2:6), the Rambam changed his opinion regarding the acceptance of these individuals as agents when the veracity of the signature on a <i>get</i> has been established. The standard text of the Commentary on the Mishnah states that if the witnesses' signatures have been verified, the <i>get</i> is acceptable unless the person worships false gods or violates the Sabbath in public. This is also the view of other authorities and is the ruling favored by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:33).</i> If, however, we must rely only on the words of an individual who is disqualified because of the violation of Scriptural law, the divorce is void entirely.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See also Chapter 12, Halachah 17. Based on the objections of the Ra'avad, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> modifies the Rambam's ruling slightly, explaining that the intent is that the woman may not remarry, and not that the <i>get</i> is void. If, however, she does remarry, her second husband is also required to divorce her.<br>The <i>get</i> she received from her first husband is not void, because it is possible that witnesses will come and verify the signatures on the <i>get</i>. In such an event, the divorce will be effective retroactively. Nevertheless, since those signatures have not been verified as of yet, her second husband must divorce her.<br>Rav David Arameah explains that the Rambam's intent is that the <i>get</i> is still viable, but it should be given to another agent to effect the divorce. Note the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and others, who question the source for the Rambam's ruling.</i>
If the agent was a minor when he was given the <i>get</i>, and he attained majority when he brought it [to the woman], [or he was a] deaf mute and gained the ability to hear and speak, [or he was] mentally incompetent and gained competence, [or he was] a gentile and converted, or a servant and was freed, the divorce is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In all these instances, since the agent was not fit to serve in that capacity at the time the agency was delegated to him, it is as if the agency had never been conveyed upon him.</i><br>If, however, the husband gives [an agent] a <i>get</i> while [the agent] is able to hear and speak, [or the agent then] becomes a deaf mute and afterwards regains his ability to hear and speak, or [the agent] was mentally competent, he lost his competence and then regained it when he brought it to the woman, the <i>get</i> is acceptable, for at the outset and at the conclusion, [the agent] was of sound mind.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that, in the interim, he was unfit to serve as an agent is of no significance. Note the <i>Or Sameach</i>, who on this basis questions what the law would be if the husband negates the agency and then desires to reestablish it. Must he reappoint the agent or not?</i>
When a woman appoints an agent in the presence of witnesses and tells him: "Take my <i>get</i> and keep it for me in your possession," the person is a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>. It is as if she told him: "Receive my <i>get</i> for me."<br>A woman may appoint a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> to receive her <i>get</i> for her from an agent appointed by her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 63b states that our Sages entertained a hypothesis that this involves a disregard for the honor of the husband, and therefore the divorce should be void, but they did not accept this theory.</i><br>A girl below the age of majority may not appoint a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>. Although she may acquire property by virtue of her courtyard in the same manner as an adult woman<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">And thus, if a <i>get</i> were placed in her courtyard, the divorce would be acceptable. Moreover, the effectiveness of a courtyard in the acceptance of a <i>get</i> is associated with the concept of agency. (See <i>Bava Metzia</i> 10b and the gloss of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> on Chapter 5, Halachah 2.)</i> [she does not have the privilege of appointing an agent]. The rationale is that [the appointment of] a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> requires witnesses, and witnesses may not testify with regard to a minor, because she is not of complete mental competence.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad disagrees with the Rambam and maintains that the rationale is that a minor is never empowered to appoint an agent. And thus, a woman below majority may not appoint a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i> either. It must be noted that in <i>Hilchot Sh'luchin V'Shutafin</i> 2:2, the Rambam indeed states that in no instance may a minor appoint an agent. See the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> and Rav Kapach, who emphasize that a special law applies with regard to an agent to receive a <i>get</i>, for he is considered to be the extension of the woman's hand. Note the Rambam's wording in Chapter 1, Halachah 1.</i>
[The following laws apply] when a woman has appointed a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> and her husband told him: "I do not want you to receive the <i>get</i> for her. Instead, here is her <i>get</i>. Bring it to her." The husband has this prerogative, and the person becomes a <i>sh'liach holachah</i>, rather than a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the divorce does not become final when the agent receives the <i>get</i>. It is only when the <i>get</i> reaches the woman's hand that the divorce takes effect.</i><br>If, however, the husband tells [the woman's agent]: "Receive the <i>get</i> for her," "Here it is," or "Acquire it for her," he has not revoked the agency [with which] the <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> [was charged]. But if [the husband] tells [the agent]: "Bring it to her," he has revoked the agency [with which] the <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> [was charged] and has made him the agent of the husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the husband's statements imply that he is unwilling to give the agent the <i>get</i> in his original capacity, but that he is willing to charge him with a different agency - bringing the woman the <i>get</i> as agent of the husband.<br>This ruling is not accepted by all authorities. The Rashba and Rabbenu Nissim differ, maintaining that in such an instance, the status of the divorce is in doubt. The Rambam's opinion is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 140:6), while the other views are cited by the Ramah.</i> Similarly, if the husband said: "Bring it and give it to her," he has revoked the agency [with which] the <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> [was charged].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Although with regard to the transfer of a present the Rambam maintains that the expression "Give it to him" is equivalent to saying "Acquire it on his behalf" (<i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 4:4), because the husband said "Bring it to her" it is clear that he desired the agent to serve in the capacity of a <i>sh'liach holachah</i>.</i>
[In the following situation, although the <i>get</i> reaches the woman's hand, the divorce is not effective because of the confusion in the delegation of agency.] An agent appointed by a woman came to receive her <i>get</i> from her husband. The agent told the husband: "I am a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>."<br>The husband responded: "Bring the <i>get</i> [in the capacity] in which she appointed you"<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 140:8) explains that the phrase "in the capacity in which she appointed you" is of primary significance here. If the husband does not add that phrase and says merely: "Bring the <i>get</i> to her," he is appointing the agent to be a <i>sh'liach holachah</i>, and by taking the <i>get</i> the agent is accepting this appointment. Therefore, if the agent delivers the <i>get</i> to the woman, the divorce is effective.</i> - i.e., he did not revoke his agency. Instead, it is as if he had said: "Whether she appointed you to be a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> or a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>, you remain in that capacity."<br>The agent brought [the woman] the <i>get</i>, but she told him: "I did not appoint you to be a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>, but rather to be a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>." Even if the agent gives her the <i>get</i>, the divorce is not effective, for in speaking to the husband, the agent revoked the agency that he was granted. It is as if he had told him: "I was never appointed a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i> on her behalf."
[A different ruling applies in the following instance.] The agent told the husband, "I am a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>," and the husband told him, "Bring [the <i>get</i> in the capacity] in which she appointed you."<br>The agent brought [the woman] the <i>get</i>, but she told him: "I appointed you to be a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>." When the <i>get</i> is delivered to the woman, the divorce is effective, for he did not revoke the agency that he was granted. He merely reduced [her dependence on him]. For she appointed him [as an agent] to receive [the <i>get</i>], and he said: "I will merely bring it."<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">By serving as a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>, the agent accepts far more responsibility and difficulty than by serving as a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>. If he served as a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>, the divorce would be completed after he received the <i>get</i>. As a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>, the agent must trouble himself to bring the <i>get</i> to the woman. As such, we assume that since the woman was willing to accept the agent as a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>, she is certainly willing to accept him as a <i>sh'liach hava'ah</i>.<br>Note the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> and Rabbenu Nissim, who explain that it is possible that the husband relied on the words of the agent, or it is possible that he was concerned with the words of his wife. Indeed, with regard to questions of commercial law, there is an unresolved question in <i>Bava Metzia</i> 76a regarding this matter. Following this rationale, even if the <i>get</i> never reached the woman, as long as it reached the agent the status of the divorce is in doubt. This ruling is also cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 140:9).</i>
[The following rules apply when] a husband sends a <i>get</i> to his wife, and when the agent attempts to give it to her, she refuses to take it and tells him in the presence of witnesses, "Hold the <i>get</i> in safekeeping,"<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">With this expression, the woman's intent is that the agent should begin acting as her agent and hold the <i>get</i> for her. For keeping the <i>get</i> as an entrusted article violates the instructions that her husband gave him (Rabbenu Nissim).</i> or "You are an agent to receive it for me." Until the <i>get</i> is given to the woman, the status of the divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The doubt is whether an agent can begin acting in the capacity of the woman's agent before he has completed executing the agency with which he was charged by the husband (<i>Gittin</i> 63b).</i> Once it reaches her possession, the divorce is definitely binding.
When an agent brings a <i>get</i>, he must give it to the woman in the presence of two [witnesses].<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 16.</i> These two [witnesses] must read [the <i>get</i>] and then have it given in their presence.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 19.</i> For the laws applying to the exchange between the agent and the woman are the same as those applying to her exchange with her husband, for the agent is taking his place.<br>Accordingly, if the agent gave [the <i>get</i>] to her without having it read by the witnesses who observed its transfer, and the woman took it and threw it into the ocean, the status of the divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 21.</i>
When an agent transgresses and gives [a woman her] <i>get</i> in private, he should take it back from her and give it to her in the presence of two [witnesses].<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam rules that after the fact, a <i>get</i> given in private is acceptable. There are, however, <i>geonim</i> who hold that such a <i>get</i> is unacceptable (Chapter 1, Halachah 16). Since the status of an agent is weaker than that of the husband himself, a second transfer of the <i>get</i> is required in this instance <i>a priori</i> (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> If he dies [and thus this is no longer possible], and the signatures of the witnesses on the <i>get</i> that the woman possesses have been verified, the divorce is acceptable.
Even when a <i>get</i> is delivered to the woman [for whom it is intended], it can be nullified [in the following instances]: The agent took the <i>get</i>, but the husband changed his mind before it reached the woman and told him or her: "The <i>get</i> that I sent you is void," he sent another agent to nullify [the <i>get</i>], or he told others, "The <i>get</i> that I sent to my wife is void."<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 26. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:59) rules that at the outset, a husband should not nullify a <i>get</i> unless either the agent he appointed or his wife is present. Otherwise, it is possible that the woman may receive the <i>get</i> - and marry another man - before receiving notice that her <i>get</i> was nullified.</i><br>When [a husband] nullifies the <i>get</i> in the presence of other people,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., people other than the agent. If he nullifies the <i>get</i> in the presence of the agent, no one else need be present.</i> there must be at least two people present.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Note the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:59), who cites an opinion that allows the husband to nullify the <i>get</i> by speaking to two people, one outside the presence of the other.</i> If the <i>get</i> has already been delivered to the woman or to a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i>, the husband can no longer nullify it.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For the divorce has already taken effect.</i> [This applies] even if he nullifies the <i>get</i> within a very brief time<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In this context, the words "a very brief time" are a loose translation. The Hebrew term <i>toch k'dei dibbur</i> has a very specific meaning: the amount of time necessary to say "<i>Shalom alecha, rabbi umori</i>."</i> [after appointing the agent];<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that divorcing a wife is a very serious matter, and a man would not make such a decision unless he were resolute. Therefore, his change of mind is not considered to be a clarification of his original position (as in certain other instances - e.g., a retraction of a business commitment), but rather an entirely new decision. (Note the parallels to <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 7:22, <i>Hilchot Avodah Zarah</i> 2:9 and <i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 2:17.)</i> since [the <i>get</i>] was nullified after being delivered to the woman, to [her] <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> or to her courtyard, it is not void, and the divorce is effective.
Although [a husband] was seeking to appoint an agent to nullify [a <i>get</i>], or he was seeking two individuals so that he could nullify [the <i>get</i>] in their presence, and during the time he was searching for these individuals the <i>get</i> reached the woman's hand, and afterwards he nullified it, it is not void.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The fundamental principle involved is that Torah law depends on a person's acts and statements, not his feelings. Although it is obvious that the husband desired to nullify the <i>get</i>, since he was not able to take binding action the divorce is valid. (See also Halachah 25.)</i> Despite the fact that he was trying to nullify it before it reached her hand [the divorce is effective].
If [the husband] told ten men: "Write a <i>get</i> and give it to my wife,"<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachah 27.</i> he may nullify [the <i>get</i>] in the presence of one of the ten, although the others are not present. And he may nullify it in the presence of two other people [who were not involved originally].<br>If he sent the <i>get</i> via two [agents], he may negate one of the agents although the other is not present. Even when there are ten agents, when he negates [the agency] in the presence of one of them, the <i>get</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with Rambam and maintains that although a person may nullify the agency of one of the two (or ten), doing so does not nullify the agency of the other(s), and if they give the woman the <i>get</i>, the divorce is effective. Although the Rambam's opinion is also mentioned, it is that of the Ra'avad that is favored by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 149:61).</i>
Similarly, when a person tells two [witnesses],<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 134:1) states that even when the husband makes statements to two witnesses individually, the objection is binding.</i> "The <i>get</i> that I [am intending to] write for my wife is nullified," although he has a <i>get</i> written afterwards and gives it to her in the presence of two other [witnesses], the divorce is void. This is referred to as lodging an objection with regard to a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i>, Chapter 6, the Rambam explains the details of lodging an objection with regard to a sale. There he explains that when a person is compelled to make a sale against his will, he can nullify the transaction by issuing an objection stating that he was compelled to make this sale. With regard to an objection to a <i>get</i>, other authorities (see <i>Tur</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i>, Chapter 134) also mention the concept of compulsion. The Rambam, however, omits mention of the subject entirely.<br>As reflected in Halachah 27, our Sages considered lodging an objection to a <i>get</i> in a very negative light.</i><br>Similarly, if [the husband] were to tell two [witnesses]: "Any <i>get</i> that so and so will write for me is nullified," "Any <i>get</i> that I will have written in the court of so and so is nullified," or "Any <i>get</i> that I will have written in the next twenty years is nullified," the <i>get</i> is nullified.<br>Similarly, if he told two [witnesses]: "Any <i>get</i> that I write for so and so, my wife, is nullified. And any statements that I make to nullify this objection are nullified," the <i>get</i> is void, although he had it written and given to her, despite the fact that he nullified his objection before having the <i>get</i> written.
How can the latter situation be corrected?<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The situation mentioned by the Rambam in the previous halachah is not merely a theoretical question. In many instances, a husband who was compelled to divorce his wife would seek to have the <i>get</i> nullified so that their marriage could continue. Similarly, apostates and men whose feelings toward their wives have soured have sought to nullify their divorces in order to cause their wives difficulties.</i> The witnesses should tell [the husband] before the composition of the <i>get</i>: "Affirm in our presence that any statements that you have made that when verified would cause [this] <i>get</i> to be nullified, are themselves nullified."<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Many of the other commentaries differ with the Rambam concerning this point. Some - e.g., the Rashba - are more stringent, for they maintain that the husband can word his objection to nullify even such a statement. They explain that to insure that the <i>get</i> is not nullified, the husband must also disqualify the testimony of any witness who heard his objections.<br>Others - e.g., Rabbenu Asher - are more lenient and maintain that all that is necessary is for the husband to say that all objections to the <i>get</i> are nullified. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 134:3) rules according to the Rashba. The Ramah maintains that Rabbenu Asher's ruling is halachically acceptable; however, he counsels following the Rashba's ruling to eliminate all doubts.</i> [The husband] must answer "Yes."<br>Afterwards, he should instruct them to write the <i>get</i>, sign it and give it to his wife. We do not let [the husband] leave until the <i>get</i> is delivered [to his wife], lest he go out and nullify [the <i>get</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Despite the statements that he had made previously, if the husband nullified the <i>get</i> afterwards, his statements would be effective and the divorce void (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i><br>Neither a person who lodges an objection [to a <i>get</i>], nor one who seeks to nullify such an objection needs [to have his statements affirmed by] an act of contract.
When a person has sent a <i>get</i> via an agent and [later] nullifies [the giving of] the <i>get</i>, he may divorce the woman with [this <i>get</i>] whenever he desires. He did not nullify the <i>get</i> as a <i>get</i>; he merely nullified the agency.<br>Therefore, if the <i>get</i> was in the possession of the husband and he nullified it - e.g., he said: "This <i>get</i> is nullified" - he may never use it for a divorce. It is like a broken shard, and if it is used for a divorce, the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">There are authorities who differ with the Rambam on this point, based on their version of <i>Gittin</i> 32b. According to these authorities, since the <i>get</i> was written according to law, it is always able to be used for a divorce and can never be nullified by the husband. In consideration of the opinion of these authorities, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:66), when quoting this law, rules that the status of the divorce is in doubt.</i><br>Similarly, if the <i>get</i> was entrusted to the agent, but the husband made an explicit statement, saying: "The <i>get</i> that I sent is nullified [and may not] serve as a <i>get</i>," he may never use it to effect a divorce.
Which wording can be used to nullify a <i>get</i>? [The husband] says: "It is nullified," "I cannot abide by it," "May this <i>get</i> not be effective," "[May it] not permit [her]," "[May it] not release [her], "[May it] not send [her] forth," "[May it] not divorce [her]," "May it be a shard," "May it be like a shard," or "Behold it is like a shard" - If he used any of these expressions or a similar one, he has negated it.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The point is that these expressions all indicate that the husband no longer desires to use this <i>get</i> to divorce his wife.</i>
If, however, [the husband] says: "This <i>get</i> is not a <i>get</i>," "It is unacceptable," "It is not effective," "It does not permit [her]," "It does not release [her], "It does not send [her] forth," "It does not divorce [her]," "It is a shard," his statements are not effective. This wording does not [indicate his desire to] nullify [the <i>get</i>]. Instead, they are statements of fact, and in this instance, statements of incorrect fact.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">For all the statements quoted above are false. The <i>get</i> is effective; it is the husband who no longer desires to use it.</i> It is as if someone said that a forbidden entity were permitted or that an impure object were pure.
If he says, "This <i>get</i> is nullified," [it is possible that] the implication is the use of the past tense, as in the phrase [Song of Songs 5:6]: "He turned away and was gone." Thus, there is a question concerning the matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam implies that the question is whether the word לטב implies a future tense - i.e., that his intent is to nullify the <i>get</i> - in which instance the divorce would not be effective. Or whether it implies the past tense, in which case, as in the previous halachah, the husband would be giving us incorrect information.<br>Rashi offers a slightly different interpretation of the passage in <i>Gittin</i> 32b that serves as the source for this halachah. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:63) quotes both opinions.</i> Therefore, if [a woman] has been divorced with this <i>get</i> [after such statements were made], the status of the divorce is in doubt.
[As reflected in the following instance, an explicit statement must be made to nullify the <i>get</i>: A man] sent a <i>get</i> to his wife [via an agent]. The agent returned to him and said: "I could not find her," or "She did not want to receive it." Although the husband answered: "Blessed be He who is good and does good," or made other statements that imply that he no longer wants to divorce her, and that he is happy that the <i>get</i> was not delivered, the <i>get</i> is not nullified. Instead, he may give it to her and the divorce will be effective. [For the <i>get</i> to be nullified,] he must explicitly say "Do not give it to her," or he should explicitly nullify it [using one of the above expressions].
A person who sent a <i>get</i> to his wife [via an agent] and then nullified [the <i>get</i>] in the presence of two other people,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the man nullifies the <i>get</i> in the presence of the agent or in the presence of his wife, there is no difficulty.</i> and similarly, a person who issued an objection [nullifying] a <i>get</i>, should be given stripes for rebelliousness, because he makes it possible for illegitimate children to be conceived.<br>Since a <i>get</i> reached [the woman], [it is possible that] she will marry on this basis. [Only] afterwards, when the witnesses in whose presence the husband nullified the <i>get</i> or issued an objection before the <i>get</i> was written appear [will she realize the difficulty]. Thus, a child [conceived in her second marriage] will be illegitimate.
When an agent brings a <i>get</i> and gives it to a woman, we do not suspect that the husband nullified it. Instead, he should give it to the woman under the presumption that it is acceptable, and the woman may marry on this basis. If it is discovered afterwards that [the husband] negated it, [the woman] must leave [her second husband], and any children conceived are illegitimate.<br>Similarly, when [a husband] has a <i>get</i> written and gives it to his wife, we do not suspect that perhaps he lodged an objection regarding this <i>get</i>. Instead, we operate under the conception that the <i>get</i> is acceptable, and the woman is allowed to marry on this basis.
Similarly, when an agent brings a <i>get</i> [that was sent by] a man who was sick or elderly, he may give it to the woman under the presumption that the husband is still alive. If, however, the husband was in his death throes when [the agent left], even when he gives the <i>get</i> to the woman the status of the divorce is in doubt. For the majority of those in their death throes will die, and a <i>get</i> given after death is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The primary reason for giving such a <i>get</i> - for the marriage is terminated with the husband's death regardless - is to free a childless woman from the obligations of <i>yibbum</i> and <i>chalitzah</i>. If the <i>get</i> was given before the husband's death, these rites are not necessary. Otherwise, they are. With the current advances in record keeping and communication, it is usually possible to eliminate the doubt mentioned in this halachah.</i><br>Similarly, when a city is surrounded by an army<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:69) states that this law applies only when a city is surrounded by the army of the ruling authority. If it is surrounded by an army from another country, we no longer operate under the presumption that the husband is alive.</i> and held under siege, a ship is in distress at sea,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 3:4), the Rambam defines a ship in distress as follows: "One that faces a tempest-torn sea that threatens to sink it. The sailors are unable to control its path as they desire.... Nevertheless, all the oars and the other instruments remain intact."</i> or a person is taken out to be judged [with regard to a capital case],<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This applies regardless of whether the person is being tried by a Jewish court or a gentile court.</i> we presume that any person in such a situation is alive. If a person in such a situation has sent a <i>get</i> [to his wife] via an agent, he may give it to her, and the presumption will be that the divorce is effective.
When, by contrast, a city has been conquered by an attacking army and [its wall] broken, a ship is lost at sea,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam defines this as referring to a ship whose oars, steering mechanism and other navigational tools have been broken, and it is totally at the mercy of the waters.<br>The laws that apply when a ship has sunk are described in Chapter 13, Halachah 16.</i> a person is being taken out to be executed by a gentile court,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The implication is that if the person was sentenced to death by a Jewish court, we presume that the sentence was carried out. (See <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 9:2.) In contrast, there is a possibility that a gentile court will accept a bribe.</i> or he is being dragged by a beast of prey, swept away by a river or an avalanche has fallen upon him, the stringencies applying to both the living and the dead, are followed.<br>If [a person in one of these situations gave] a <i>get</i> to an agent, the agent should not give it to the husband's wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Lest the husband have died already.</i> If, however, he gives it to her, the status of the divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For we do not know which came first, the husband's death or the giving of the <i>get</i>.</i> If it is known that the husband died before the <i>get</i> reached his wife, the divorce is void.
When a husband sends his wife a <i>get</i>, he is obligated to provide her with support and fulfill the other stipulations of the marriage contract, until the <i>get</i> reaches [his wife] or a <i>sh'liach kabbalah</i> she has appointed.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Although the husband desires to divorce her, she remains his wife until the <i>get</i> reaches her. Therefore, the husband remains obligated by the marriage contract.</i>

Chapter 7

[The following rules apply when] an agent brings a <i>get</i> from one place to another in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is singled out because Jewish courts frequently held sessions there, and it was feasible to verify the signatures of the witnesses to the <i>get</i>. In contrast, the courts in the diaspora held sessions less frequently, and verifying the signature of witnesses was a far more formidable task. Therefore, different laws apply, as stated in Halachah 5. In the present age, the laws applying to sending <i>gittin</i> in the diaspora are also followed in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:1.)</i> Although [the agent] did not witness the writing of the <i>get</i> and does not know who the witnesses are, but rather the <i>get</i> was given to him by the husband, who instructed him to give it to his wife, [the agent] may give [the woman the <i>get</i>] in the presence of witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">More particularly, according to the Rambam, only one witness is necessary, because the agent himself may serve as the second witness.</i> Although the identity of the witnesses [who signed the <i>get</i>] is unknown to us, [the woman] is considered divorced, and she may remarry on this basis.
If the husband came and protested, saying: "I never divorced her. The <i>get</i> she was given is a forgery," the signatures [of the witnesses] should be verified.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If the signatures of the witnesses can be verified, the husband's protests are ignored. Once the witnesses sign the <i>get</i>, it is considered as if they have testified in court, and the husband's statements are of no avail.</i> If this is impossible, and the witnesses are not known at all, she must leave [her second husband], and [any children born to them] are considered illegitimate,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 142:5, which quotes a difference of opinion among the commentaries whether or not the Rambam maintains that the husband's protest causes the <i>get</i> to be considered void. The consequence of this difference of opinion is the status of the children born out of a second marriage. Are they definitely considered to be illegitimate, or is this a point of doubt?</i> for [we assume that] she has not been divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam considers the <i>get</i> to be unacceptable because the fact that the husband protests the authenticity of the <i>get</i> and the woman is unable to verify the signatures causes us to question the matter. Since the woman was regarded as married, her status cannot be changed until she receives a <i>get</i> that is not open to doubt.<br>The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141) explains that although we assume that the woman has not been divorced, we do not know this with absolutely certainty. Hence, if she is consecrated by another man after receiving this <i>get</i>, she must be divorced by this second husband. Similarly, there are other authorities who maintain that the status of the divorce is doubtful.</i> If the <i>get</i> has been lost, the status of the divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For in this instance there is no way to verify the signature of the witnesses.</i><br>For this reason, women who we presume hate each other are not trusted to bring a <i>get</i> to one another in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. [We suspect that] it might be a forgery, because one desires that the other remarry and be forbidden to her husband[s].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the woman remarries, she will be committing adultery, and hence, she will be forbidden to remain married to her first husband. Nor will she be permitted to remain married to her second husband. Since they were involved in an adulterous relationship, they are forbidden to marry.</i>
These are the women who we presume hate each other:<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., just as a mother-in-law may not bring a <i>get</i> for her daughter-in-law, a daughter-in-law may not bring a <i>get</i> for her mother-in-law.</i> A mother-in-law, the daughter of a mother-in-law, another woman married to the same man - this applies even if this woman has since remarried<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Despite the fact that this woman has been divorced and remarried, we assume that she still harbors bad feelings towards the woman with whom she shared her previous husband and might desire to create difficulties for her.</i> - her <i>yevamah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a woman who is married to her husband's brother. If the woman's husband died without children, her husband's brother would be obligated to marry her to fulfill the mitzvah of <i>yibbum</i>. Thus, the two women could ultimately become married to the same man.</i> - this applies even if she was her sister<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Although in this instance the mitzvah of <i>yibbum</i> would not apply because the two sisters are forbidden to marry the same man, our Sages did not differentiate when imposing this restriction.</i> - and her husband's daughter [from another marriage]. All other women are acceptable [to act in this capacity].
When a man brings a <i>get</i> from one place to another place in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and becomes sick or is prevented from fulfilling his agency because of factors beyond his control, he may send the <i>get</i> via a second agent.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, there is nothing preventing the agent from carrying out his agency, he may not appoint another agent. For we assume that a husband would object to the substitution (<i>Gittin</i> 29a).</i> Similarly, if the second agent becomes sick, he may send it via another agent. Indeed, even 100 agents [may be appointed in this manner].<br>There is no need for the agent to appoint the second agent in the presence of witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the notes on Chapter 6, Halachah 4, there are opinions that differ and require the appointment of an agent to be observed by witnesses. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:11) favors the Rambam's view with regard to the appointment of the agent himself, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>:40) follows the other opinions with regard to the agent's appointment of a second agent. In practice, the present custom is to have the appointment witnessed.</i> The final agent to whom the <i>get</i> is given should give it to the woman in the presence of two witnesses, and the divorce is effective. [This applies] even when the first agent dies [before the divorce takes effect].<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For the husband, and not the previous agent, is the source of empowerment for the final agent.</i>
[The following rules apply when] an agent brings a <i>get</i> from one place to another in the diaspora, from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> to the diaspora or from the diaspora to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. If the agent was present at the time of the composition and signature of the <i>get</i>, he should say, in the presence of two witnesses, "It was written in my presence and it was signed in my presence."<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, the verification of the signature of witnesses on a legal document requires two witnesses. In this instance, however, an exception was made, and the statements of one individual are accepted.<br>The rationale for this exception can be explained as follows: According to Scriptural law, there is no need for the signatures of the witnesses to a legal document to be verified. This requirement was instituted by our Sages. And in order to prevent a woman from having difficulties in obtaining a divorce, they relaxed that requirement and accepted the statements of the agent (<i>Gittin</i> 3a).<br>The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:7) quotes opinions that rule that the phrase "It was written and signed in my presence" must be recited in its Hebrew original, <i>B'fanai nichtav uv'fanai nechtam</i>; a translation is not sufficient.</i><br>Afterwards, it should be given in her presence, and the divorce takes effect, even though the identity of the witnesses is not known to us. Even if the witnesses' names resemble the names of gentiles, we do not suspect [that there is any difficulty].<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we do not suspect that the witnesses were gentiles and that the <i>get</i> is void. For it has been customary for Jews living in the diaspora to adopt gentile names (<i>Tosafot, Gittin</i> 11b).<br>If, however, the probability is small that a Jew would use such a name, e.g., Nickolas or Patrick, there are authorities who are more stringent. See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 130:30.</i>
If [afterwards,] the husband came and protested [that the <i>get</i> is a forgery], his words are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Once the statements of the agent are accepted by the court, they are given the same weight as if they had been made by two witnesses. Therefore, the protest of the husband is of no consequence (<i>Gittin</i> 9a). If, however, the husband brings two witnesses who testify that the <i>get</i> is a forgery, their word - and not that of the agent - is accepted (Halachah 9).</i> Therefore, even women who we presume hate each other are relied upon to bring a <i>get</i> in such a situation and state that it was written and signed in their presence.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Our suspicion is that these women would forge a <i>get</i> to cause the wife to be forbidden to remained married to both husbands, and that the forgery will be easily discovered, because her first husband will protest the legitimacy of the <i>get</i>. When the agent herself must say "The get was written and signed in my presence," the first husband's protest will not be accepted, and the woman will be allowed to remain married to her second husband. As such, the forgery will not achieve its desired result.</i>
Similarly, when an agent who brings a <i>get</i> in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> says: "It was written and signed in my presence," although he is not required to do so, if the husband comes and protests [that the <i>get</i> was forged], [the husband's] words are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages accepted the statements of the agent as equivalent to those of two witnesses even when he was not required to have made them (<i>Gittin</i> 6b). Note the comments of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, who questions whether this also applies with regard to questions of monetary law - e.g., the payment of the woman's marriage contract.</i><br>[With regard to a <i>get</i> sent to or from the diaspora,] if the agent was not present at the time of the writing or signing of the <i>get</i>, it may not be given to the woman unless the signatures [of the witnesses] were verified.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The woman might think that the divorce is effective upon receipt of the <i>get</i> and may remarry, without being aware of the difficulties involved.</i> The agent himself may serve as [one of] the three [judges] who verify the signatures of the witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">More particularly, in this matter the agent can serve in three capacities: 1) as the agent to give the <i>get</i>, 2) as a witness to verify the signatures of the witnesses, and 3) as a judge declaring that the signatures have been verified. This is possible because, as explained above, the verification of witnesses is a requirement of Rabbinic law. In matters of Scriptural law, a witness may not serve as a judge. (See also Halachah 19.)</i><br>If [the signatures] were not verified, and the <i>get</i> was given to the woman, the divorce is unacceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., disqualified by the Rabbis. It is not, however, void according to Scriptural law (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). According to the general principles mentioned by the Rambam (Chapter 10, Halachah 2), if the woman remarries in such an instance, she is not obligated to leave her second husband. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142) quotes opinions that differ and require her to leave her second husband, even if her first husband does not protest. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:1) follows the Rambam's ruling.</i> until [the signatures] are verified. If the husband comes and protests, and the signatures are not verified, we consider her not to be divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">See the notes on Halachah 1.</i> If the <i>get</i> has been lost, the status of the divorce is in doubt.
Why did [our Sages] require [the agent] to say: "It was written and signed in my presence" in the diaspora? So that the woman would not have to have [the signatures] verified if the husband comes and protests. [Our Sages did not desire that she be faced with such a predicament,] because witnesses are often not available to verify signatures [on a document that has been taken] from one place to another in the diaspora.
When a husband brought explicit proof<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., two witnesses who testify that the <i>get</i> is a forgery.</i> that a <i>get</i> concerning which [an agent] said: "It was written and signed in my presence" is a forgery, [the <i>get</i>] is void. For [our Sages] accepted the statements of one person only in the face of a protest raised by a husband that was not accompanied by substantial proof. When, however, this person's statements are contradicted by witnesses, [his statements] are not accepted.
The rivers in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and the islands in the Mediterranean Sea that are within the boundaries of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 1:7, the northern boundary of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> was the Amanon Mountains, a range in Northern Lebanon, far above the position of contemporary Beirut. The southern boundary was the Nile river. All islands within the imaginary line drawn from one point on the Mediterranean to the other are considered to be part of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i> are bound by the rules pertaining to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. [The islands] that are outside these boundaries are bound by the rules pertaining to the diaspora. The boundaries of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> are described in <i>Hilchot Terumot</i>.<br>With regard to <i>gittin</i>, [the rules pertaining to] <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> also pertain to Babylonia.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In the Talmudic era, Babylonia was a center of Torah study and there would be students traveling from all the cities to and from the central yeshivot in Sura, Neharde'a and Pumbedita. Hence, it was possible to find witnesses to verify the authenticity of the signatures on a <i>get</i> (<i>Gittin</i> 6a).</i>
When a <i>get</i> was written in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and signed in the diaspora, [the agent] is required to say: "It was written and signed in my presence."<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Regardless of whether the <i>get</i> was given in another place in the diaspora or in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, the signature of the witnesses cannot be easily verified, and hence the agent is required to make these statements.</i> If it was written in the diaspora and signed in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, [the agent] is not required to say: "It was written and signed in my presence."<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>get</i> is given in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, the signatures of the witnesses can be verified, and hence the agent is not required to make these statements.</i>
When a portion of the <i>get</i> is written in [the agent's] presence, and all the signatures were executed in his presence, he may say: "It was written and signed in my presence,"<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The fundamental element to be observed by the agent is the signature of the <i>get</i> by the witnesses. For as long as the scribe wrote a portion of the <i>get lishmah</i>, for the sake of the man and the woman getting divorced, it is sufficient (Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Gittin</i> 2:1).<br>Note the <i>Yadav Shel Moshe</i>, who questions why the Rambam concerns himself with the issue of <i>lishmah</i>. On the difference of opinion between Rabbah and Ravva regarding the reason for the agent having to say: "It was written and signed in my presence," the Rambam follows the opinion of Ravva, who states that the problem is not <i>lishmah</i>, but rather the verification of the signatures of the witnesses.</i> provided it was the initial portion - even one line<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Gittin</i> 15a, interprets this as referring to the line that contains the man's name, the woman's name and the date. <i>Tosafot</i> interprets it as referring to the first line of the <i>get</i>. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 142:28 explains that the Rambam accepts neither of these opinions, but means any given line of the <i>get</i>.</i> - [that was written in his presence].<br>[Moreover,] even if he merely heard the noise [made by the scribe's] pen [writing], and the witnesses signed in his presence, he may say: "It was written and signed in my presence." Similarly, even if the scribe went out to the marketplace and then returned and completed the <i>get</i>, [the agent] does not fear that another [man] found [the scribe] and told him [to write a <i>get</i> for him], and he [completed] writing the <i>get</i> for another person.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If that were the case, the <i>get</i> would not be written <i>lishmah</i>.</i> Instead, [the agent] may say: "It was written and signed in my presence."
If [the agent] said: "It was written in my presence," but did not say, "It was signed in my presence," [if he said,] "It was signed in my presence," but did not say, "It was written in my presence," or [if he said,] "The entire [<i>get</i>] was written in my presence, and one witness signed [in my presence], but not the other witness," even if he himself was the other witness,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For the confirmation of the <i>get</i> must follow the directives outlined by our Sages in their entirety or rely on the verification of the signatures of the witnesses (<i>Tosafot, Gittin</i> 15b).</i> the signature of the witnesses must be verified.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since the decree of our Sages was not fulfilled, the only alternative is to verify the signatures of the witnesses.</i> Only then, may [the <i>get</i>] be given to her.<br>If he and another person testify with regard to [the signature of] the second witness, who did not sign in his presence,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the agent says that the <i>get</i> was written, and one signed in his presence, and he and another person verify the signature of the second witness.</i> [the <i>get</i>] is acceptable and may be given to her. Needless to say, if two other [witnesses] testify with regard to [the signature of] the second witness, [the <i>get</i>] is acceptable.
When two agents bring a <i>get</i> from the diaspora, they may give it to [the woman], causing the divorce to be effective, even though it was not written and signed in their presence. Since the husband gave it to them to give to his wife, his protests regarding this <i>get</i> are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142) differs with this ruling. He agrees that in contrast to a <i>get</i> brought by one agent, a <i>get</i> brought by two agents may be given to the woman without their saying, "It was written and signed in our presence." Nevertheless, the <i>Tur</i> maintains that if the husband protests the authenticity of the <i>get</i>, the signatures of the witnesses must be verified.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:18) follows the Rambam's ruling, while the Ramah quotes that of the <i>Tur</i>.</i> Although [the signatures of the witnesses] have not been verified, the agents he [appointed] serve as witnesses [to its authenticity].<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies only when the agents are able to serve as witnesses in court. If, however, they are related to the husband or the wife, or would be disqualified for other reasons, they are required to say, "It was written and signed in our presence."</i> For if these witnesses said, "She was divorced in our presence," the divorce would be effective, even though there is no <i>get</i> present.
When does the above apply? When the <i>get</i> is in the possession of both of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Gittin</i> 16b) appears to imply that both agents must be physically holding the <i>get</i> together. The Rashba differs [and this opinion is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:18)] and states all that is necessary is for the agents to state that they were both appointed by the husband and given the <i>get</i>. The Rashba extends this leniency and states that even if only one agent was appointed by the husband, if another person witnessed his appointment and traveled with him, it is not necessary for the agent to say "It was written and signed in my presence."</i> If, however, the <i>get</i> is not in the possession of both of them, they must say: "It was written and signed in our presence."<br>Accordingly, if one agent says, "It was written in my presence," and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above, according to the interpretation accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, we are not speaking about a second agent, but another person who accompanied the agent.</i> says, "It was signed in my presence," or they both say, "It was written in our presence," and one<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This is referring to a third individual, and not one of the agents.</i> says: "It was signed in my presence," since it is not in the possession of both of them, it should not be given to the woman unless the authenticity of the signatures has been verified.
If one person said: "It was written in my presence,"<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the statement, "It was written in my presence," is unnecessary. Since two witnesses testify that they observed the signing of the <i>get</i>, they have verified the signatures of the witnesses. The Rambam mentions this instance, only because he is quoting the wording of the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 2:1). And the Mishnah mentions this only as a literary device, so that the wording in its different clauses are parallel.</i> and two people say: "It was signed in our presence," [the <i>get</i>] is acceptable. [This applies] even when they are not in possession [of the <i>get</i>], because the signatures [of the witnesses] have been verified.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the two individuals who speak with regard to the signing of the <i>get</i> are not agents, their statements verify the authenticity of the signatures, making it a valid legal document.</i>
[The following rules apply when] an agent brings a <i>get</i> in the diaspora and gives it to the woman in private, or gives it to her in the presence of two witnesses, but does not tell her: "It was written and signed in my presence." Even if she has married, he should take the <i>get</i> back from her and then give it to her in the presence of two witnesses and say: "It was written and signed in my presence." If he does not take it back from her, the divorce is unacceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This wording implies that if the woman remarried after this divorce, she may remain married to her second husband. Rabbenu Asher and others rule more stringently, and maintain that in such a situation, the woman must be divorced by her second husband.</i> until the signatures [of the witnesses] are verified.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Even after the signatures of the witnesses have been verified, the <i>get</i> should be taken from the woman and returned to her in the presence of witnesses, as stated in Chapter 6, Halachah 15.</i>
If [the agent] gave a woman a <i>get</i> and did not suffice to say: "It was written and signed in my presence," before he lost the power of speech, the signatures [of the witnesses] should be verified.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's wording, it appears that it is not sufficient for the witness to write these words out. Although a person who cannot speak and who witnessed the death of a woman's husband is allowed to write out his testimony (Chapter 13, Halachah 28), there is no alternative in that situation. In these circumstances, there is an alternative: to have the signatures of the witnesses verified (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> Afterwards, he may give it to her.
A blind person may not bring a <i>get</i> from the diaspora, because he is unable to say: "It was written and signed in my presence." Accordingly, if [the <i>get</i>] was written and signed in his presence while he possessed the power of sight, and he became blind afterwards, he may say in the presence of three [men]: "It was written and signed in my presence," and give [the <i>get</i>] to her.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 23a states that when an agent is blind, he must be able to recognize the woman by the sound of her voice. It is not sufficient for others to identify her for him. When an agent can see, however, he need not know the woman's identity and may rely on the statements of others (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:11).</i><br>Similarly, when a woman brings a <i>get</i> from the diaspora, she must say: "It was written and signed in my presence," in the presence of three [men]. The provision to make these statements in the presence of two men applies only when the agent is acceptable to serve as a witness.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Edut</i> 9:12 states that a blind person may not serve as a witness, and <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 2:9 states that such a person may not serve as a judge. <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 9:2 states that a woman may not serve in these capacities. Similarly, if the agent is related to the man or the woman, he is incapable of serving as a judge and three witnesses are necessary (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:4).</i> In such an instance, he joins the other two and serves as one of the three who verify the <i>get</i>, based on his statements.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 5 and 7 and notes.</i> For a witness may serve as a judge in matters of Rabbinic law.
When an agent becomes ill or is prevented from carrying out his agency by forces beyond his control, he should approach a Jewish court<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">It is sufficient for him to appear before two judges, because he can serve as the third judge himself (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 142:9).</i> and tell them: "This <i>get</i> was written and signed in my presence."<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As opposed to the situation described in Halachah 4, the agent cannot appoint a second agent himself, because he is required to state in a court of law that the <i>get</i> was written and signed in his presence.</i> [The court,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The wording of the Rambam might be understood as implying that the agency is entrusted to the second agent by the court. This, however, is an incorrect inference, for it is the first agent who appoints the subsequent agent. This appointment must, however, be performed in a court to ensure that everything necessary to validate the <i>get</i> is performed. (See Rivash, Responsum 318.)</i> then, appoints] another agent and sends [the <i>get</i> to the woman] with him.<br>The second agent does not have to say: "It was written and signed in my presence." Instead, he says: "I am an agent of the court," and then he gives [the <i>get</i> to the woman] in the presence of witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:9) quotes the Rivash as ruling that, if sending a second <i>get</i> would be difficult, it is acceptable for the second agent to bring the <i>get</i>, even though he was not appointed in court, provided he brings a writ of authorization from the first agent.</i>
If the second agent becomes ill or is prevented from carrying out his agency by forces beyond his control, he should appoint another agent in the presence of the court. [Indeed,] even 100 [agents can be appointed in this manner].<br>[Before transferring the <i>get</i>,] the final agent says: "I am an agent of the court." He should then give the woman [the <i>get</i>]. [The <i>get</i> can be given] even if the first agent died.<br>Why must [the appointment be made] in court? Because [the first agent] must say: "It was written and signed in my presence."<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The reason the appointment of the latter agents must be made in a court is that the signatures of the witnesses have not been verified.
