By stating that his appointment was made in a court of law, every agent assures the court that the correct process has been followed regarding this <i>get</i>.</i> If, however, the signatures [of the witnesses] were verified, although one agent gives it to another in private - even if 100 agents are appointed in this way - the <i>get</i> is acceptable when it reaches the woman.<br>Although the husband did not explicitly tell the agent: "Send [the <i>get</i>] via another person if you are hindered by forces beyond your control," the agent may [appoint another agent] if he becomes ill or is hindered by forces beyond his control.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, there are no factors preventing the agent from discharging his agency, he may not appoint another agent unless the husband explicitly gave him the authority to do so (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
Whenever a person appoints an agent, the agent does not have to be present in court with him. Even if the agent is not present, he may tell [the court]: "So and so is my agent."<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The Rivash (Responsum 53) differs with this ruling and maintains that if the second agent does not hear the first agent say: "It was written and signed in my presence," he must be given a writ of appointment. Otherwise, he may not serve in this capacity. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:10) quotes the Rambam's ruling - and indeed expands upon it, allowing the agent to give the court the authority to appoint any agent they desire. The Ramah quotes the ruling of the Rivash.</i> Similarly, an agent may appoint an agent when the latter is not present. [Indeed,] even 100 [agents can be appointed in this manner].
A husband [may appoint] his wife [as an agent to transport her <i>get</i> to another place]. After having it written and signed in her presence, he gives her the <i>get</i> and tells her: "You are an agent to bring it to the court of so and so. They will appoint an agent, who will give you this <i>get</i> and thus effect the divorce."<br>Should the woman say: "It was written and signed in my presence," [when delivering the <i>get</i> to the court], her statements are accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">She must, however, make these statements for the divorce to be effective. Our Sages made this requirement in order not to differentiate between one agency and another (Rashba, commenting on <i>Gittin</i> 24a).</i> [The court] should take the <i>get</i> from her and give it to the agent so that he can give it to her as the husband instructed.
When does the above apply? When the husband [explicitly] made this stipulation. But if this stipulation is not made, but instead the husband [merely] gave her the <i>get</i> and it is in her possession, she is not required to make any statements [to the court]. We assume that she is divorced, for she is in possession of a <i>get</i> that is written according to law and that is signed by witnesses.<br>Although we do not recognize the signatures of the witnesses, and [their authenticity] has not been verified, we do not suspect that [the woman] forged them, for she would not cause her own ruin.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">It is likely that a forgery would ultimately be discovered, and she would then be forbidden to remain married to her second husband, nor would she be permitted to return to her first husband.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> draws a connection between this halachah and Chapter 12, Halachah 2, which states that the statements of a woman who produces a <i>get</i> are accepted, even though the authenticity of the signatures of the witnesses to the <i>get</i> has not been verified. The Ra'avad in his gloss on <i>Hilchot Avadim</i> 6:7 differs (see also his gloss to Chapter 12, Halachah 2). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:13-14) quotes the opinions of both the Rambam and the Ra'avad. See the Rivash (Responsum 385).</i><br>Moreover, it is considered as if the witnesses who signed the <i>get</i> had testified in court, until a protest is raised. Therefore, we assume that the <i>get</i> was written according to law and [grant the woman permission to] marry, as we assume that a <i>get</i> brought by an agent [in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>] is acceptable until the husband raises a protest or until he brings proof that it is a forgery or that it is void.<br>For if we had suspicions concerning these matters and the like, we would have to suspect that perhaps when a husband gave a <i>get</i> in our presence, he nullified it before he gave it; perhaps the witnesses who sign it are unacceptable, causing it to be regarded as a forgery; or perhaps it was not written for the sake [of the man or the woman being divorced]. Just as we do not entertain suspicions concerning these factors and assume that the <i>get</i> [is acceptable] until it is discovered that it is void, so too, we do not entertain suspicions concerning an agent or a woman when a <i>get</i> is in their possession. [The rationale is] that the laws governing prohibitions differ from those governing financial transactions.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to financial transactions, a person's statements are not accepted if he has an interest in the matter, unless he substantiates them with proof. Therefore, if a person gives a servant a deed of release that states that he has been freed and has been granted his master's possessions, his word is accepted with regard to his freedom. With regard to the possessions, however, he is required to substantiate his statements by verifying the authenticity of the witnesses to the deed (<i>Hilchot Avadim</i> 7:2).</i>

Chapter 8

When [a man] divorces [his wife] according to a conditional arrangement, and the condition is fulfilled, the divorce is effective. If the condition is not fulfilled, the divorce is not effective. All the laws governing conditional agreements have been explained in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i>, Chapter 6.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2 of that chapter, which explains that there are four rules that govern all conditional agreements:<br>a) the stipulation must be twofold [with both a positive and negative statement];<br>b) the positive aspect must be stated before the negative aspect;<br>c) the stipulation should be mentioned before the completion of the action that one desires to make conditional;<br>d) the stipulation must be something with which it is possible to comply.<br>If one of these rules was not kept when a conditional agreement was made, the stipulation is nullified; it is as if there were no condition at all. Note the details and explanations of these rules there.</i><br>There it is explained that when [a man] divorces [his wife] according to a conditional arrangement, and the condition is fulfilled, the divorce is effective from the time the condition is fulfilled, and not from the time the <i>get</i> is given to her.<br>As such, a husband may nullify a <i>get</i>, add to his conditions or add a further condition, until the original condition is fulfilled although [the woman] has already been given the <i>get</i>.<br>If the husband dies [before the condition is fulfilled],<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is relevant if the couple is childless, and the woman is obligated to fulfill either the rite of <i>yibbum</i> or of <i>chalitzah</i>.</i> or the <i>get</i> is lost or consumed by fire before the condition is fulfilled, the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to the first clause, a <i>get</i> can be effective only when the husband granting the divorce is still alive. If he dies, the marriage bonds are already rent by his death. With regard to the latter clause, since the <i>get</i> takes effect only when the condition is fulfilled, the <i>get</i> itself must be intact at that time. If it is not, there is no medium through which the divorce takes effect (see <i>Gittin</i> 74a).</i> <i>A priori</i>, [the woman] should not remarry until the condition is fulfilled.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Lest the husband die or the <i>get</i> be destroyed, and thus the divorce be nullified.</i> If, however, she has already married, she need not leave [her second husband],<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling is clarified by his statements in Chapter 9, Halachah 5, in which he states that a conditional divorce begins when the <i>get</i> is given and is not concluded until the condition is fulfilled. This conception has aroused objections among the commentators, who maintain that until the condition is fulfilled, the woman is like any other married woman. She cannot be consecrated, and marital relations with her are considered adulterous. Although in his <i>Beit Yosef</i>, Rav Yosef Karo supports the more stringent views, in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:2), he appears to follow the Rambam's opinion (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 143:2).</i> unless she is no longer able to fulfill [the condition].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., her husband made a condition that she give an article to his father, and his father dies.</i> [For since] the condition is nullified [the divorce is void.]<br>There it is explained that if [the husband] tells [his wife]: "You are divorced from the present time - or from today - on the following condition," or "You are divorced on the condition<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the husband uses this expression rather than saying: "If you do such and such, the divorce is effective. If you do not do such and such, the divorce is not effective." The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 144:3) and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 144:4) mention opinions that do not equate "on the condition that" with "from now onwards."</i> that this and this be done," when the condition is fulfilled the divorce becomes effective retroactively from the time the <i>get</i> reaches the woman.<br>Therefore, once the <i>get</i> reaches the woman's possession, [the husband] can no longer nullify the <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:2) quotes opinions that maintain that even when a condition is stated using the wording "on the condition that," the husband has the right to nullify the <i>get</i> until the condition is fulfilled.</i> add to his conditions or add a further condition. If the <i>get</i> is lost or consumed by fire, or even if the husband dies before the condition is fulfilled, she should fulfill the condition after his death, and the divorce is considered effective from the time the <i>get</i> was given to her.<br><i>A priori</i>, [the woman] may remarry even before the condition is fulfilled.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) does not accept this ruling and declares that the woman should not remarry until the condition is fulfilled. There is, however, one exception: a condition that requires the woman to refrain from performing a particular activity before the divorce takes effect.</i> We do not suspect that she will not fulfill the condition,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., because of the severe consequences this will bring upon her, we do not suspect that she will fail to do what is necessary to maintain the legitimacy of her second marriage.</i> because it was stated using the wording "From this time" or "On the condition that...."
Whenever [a man] divorces [his wife] conditionally - whether he states, "from this time onward," or makes [a repeated statement of the condition, as required] - he should not enter into privacy with his wife throughout the entire time the condition remains unfulfilled.<br>Instead, [all his dealings with her should be] in the presence of [at least one] witness. Even a servant or a maid-servant can serve this purpose, with the exception of her [private] maid-servant or her young son. For she is not embarrassed to enter into marital relations in the presence of these people.<br>It is well known that if [the husband] enters into privacy [with his wife] in the presence of two witnesses at the same time, the status of the divorce is doubtful, even if the condition was fulfilled. [We suspect] that he engaged in marital relations [with her],<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">For entering into privacy with a woman in the presence of witnesses is considered equivalent to actual relations. (See Chapter 10, Halachah 18.)</i> and nullified the <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachah 19, which explains that when there is a possibility that a man has had marital relations with his divorcee, we assume that he consecrated her again, rather than conducting these relations in a licentious manner. (See also Chapter 9, Halachah 25.)</i> as will be explained in these laws.
How should a man divorce [his wife] conditionally? He should not say: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife on this condition," or "Write a <i>get</i> and give it to her on this condition." Needless to say, he should not write in the <i>get</i>: "So and so divorces so and so on the [following] condition."<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This is unacceptable, as explained in the following halachah.</i><br>What procedure should instead be followed? He should instruct the scribe to write [a <i>get</i>] and the witnesses to sign. They should write an acceptable <i>get</i> that does not mention any condition at all. Afterwards, he should give her the <i>get</i> and tell her: "This is your <i>get</i>..." or "Behold you are divorced on the following condition." Or he should tell [the witnesses] or his agent: "Give her this <i>get</i> on the following condition."<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 147:1, which states that in a situation of difficulty in which the husband cannot wait until the <i>get</i> is written, we rely on more lenient views that differ with the Rambam. According to these views, the husband may instruct the scribe and the witnesses to write a <i>get</i> in the usual manner and to give it to the agent and before it is written, instruct the agent to give it to the woman conditionally.</i>
If [a husband] had a condition<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a condition that is acceptable (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 147:2).</i> written in the <i>get</i> after the essential portion of the <i>get</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the man's name, the woman's name, the date and the sentence: "Behold, you are permitted [to marry] any man," as stated in Chapter 3, Halachah 17.</i> was written, the <i>get</i> is acceptable, regardless of whether [the condition] was written before the signature of the witnesses or after their signature.<br>If, however, [the condition] was written before the essential portion of the <i>get</i> - even if he used the wording, "on the following condition"<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., using acceptable wording, as opposed to the wording described in the following halachah.</i> - the status of the divorce is doubtful, for the husband retains rights in the essential matter of the <i>get</i>. Similarly, if he verbally stated a condition before the essential portion of the <i>get</i> was written, the status of the divorce is doubtful.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling is questioned by the commentaries. <i>Gittin</i> 84b states that such a <i>get</i> is <i>pasul</i>, "unacceptable." Unlike the Rambam, for whom this term has an explicit meaning (see Chapter 10, Halachah 20), in the Talmud the interpretation of the term is somewhat ambiguous. In one of his responsa, the Rambam explains that since - as mentioned in the previous halachah - the difficulty concerns a point of Torah law, and yet the Talmud did not rule the <i>get</i> to be void, he maintains that the status of the divorce is in doubt.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam interprets the ruling as dependent on the principle of <i>bererah</i>, that retroactively, when the condition is fulfilled, it becomes apparent that the <i>get</i> did sever the connection between the husband and the wife. Since our Sages did not determine whether or not the principle of <i>bererah</i> applies with regard to questions of Torah law, the status of the divorce is in doubt.<br>Other authorities differ and maintain that if the condition was stated properly before the essential portion of the <i>get</i> was written and fulfilled, the <i>get</i> is acceptable. If the condition was written in the <i>get</i> itself, <i>Tosafot</i>, the Ramban and the Ra'avad maintain that the <i>get</i> is acceptable, while Rabbenu Nissim states that such a <i>get</i> is deemed unacceptable by Rabbinic decree.<br>At the outset, all the authorities maintain that the rules stated by the Rambam should be followed. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 147:2) also quotes the Rambam's ruling that the status of the divorce is in doubt. In a case of hardship, however, the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 147:2 states that the more lenient views can be relied upon.</i>
[These rules apply when a man] divorces his wife and [makes the following conditions] whether verbally or in writing, after the essential portion [of the <i>get</i>] has been written,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Needless to say, according to the Rambam, when any of the following conditions is binding, it invalidates the <i>get</i> if it was made or stated before the essential portion of the <i>get</i> was written. The difference between these conditions and those stated in the previous halachah is that those conditions can be fulfilled through a specific activity, and once they are fulfilled the marriage bond is severed entirely. The conditions mentioned in this halachah, by contrast, remain binding throughout the woman's life. Thus, her connection to her husband is never severed entirely.<br>The authorities which differ with the Rambam in the previous halachah, also differ in this instance, and maintain that if this condition is written in the <i>get</i> itself, the <i>get</i> is acceptable according to Scriptural law, but disqualified by Rabbinic decree. See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 137:3.</i>. If he tells her: "Behold, you are permitted [to marry] any man aside from so and so," or "...with the exception of so and so," [the ruling would depend on the identity of the individual specified].<br>If that individual is a gentile, a servant or a person with whom the woman is forbidden to engage in marital relations - e.g., her father, her brother, his father or his brother - the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the prohibitions involved, there is no possibility of the woman's establishing a marriage bond with these individuals (see <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:14-15), and therefore, her husband's statements are of no consequence.</i> If, however, the individual is one who could consecrate her [as a wife], the <i>get</i> is void,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Note, however, Chapter 10, Halachah 1. Rabbenu Nissim and other authorities rule more leniently and maintain that such a <i>get</i> is acceptable according to Scriptural law and was deemed unacceptable only by virtue of Rabbinic decree.</i> even if there is a negative prohibition [that is not punishable by death - neither at the hand of God nor the court - involved in such relations]<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In such instances, even though marital relations violate a prohibition, a marriage bond can be established (<i>Hilchot Ishut, loc. cit.</i>).</i> or that individual is a minor.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For ultimately, the minor will attain majority. Rabbenu Chanan'el differs and states that with regard to a minor, the status of the divorce is doubtful. The difference of opinion is dependent on the text of <i>Gittin</i> 85b, of which two versions exist.</i> [The rationale is that by making such a condition,] the husband retained certain rights with regard to the divorce, and [the bond between the couple] is not [entirely] severed.<br>If [the individual mentioned] is the husband of the wife's sister, the status of the divorce is in doubt. For although at present relations between the wife and that man are forbidden, if her sister dies, she will become permitted to marry him.
The status of the divorce is in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In all the cases to follow, the divorce does not totally sever the relationship between the husband and the wife. Nevertheless, our Sages were not certain that the rights retained by the husband were sufficient to render the divorce void (<i>Gittin</i> 85a, <i>Perishah, Even HaEzer</i> 137).</i> when [a husband divorces his wife and] tells her: "Behold, you are permitted [to marry] any man, aside from a man who will be born in the future, who does not exist at present," "...aside from promiscuous relationships" - i.e., it is as if he said: "Behold, you are permitted to marry any man, but with regard to promiscuous relations, it is as if you remain forbidden like a married woman."<br>[This ruling also applies if he tells her:] "You are permitted to any man who will engage in relations with you in the ordinary manner, but with regard to anal intercourse, you remain prohibited," "You are permitted [to marry] any man, aside from one who consecrates you with a legal document" - i.e, the woman may be consecrated by [a gift of] money or via sexual relations, but if one consecrates her with a legal document, she is forbidden like a married woman - or "You are permitted [to marry] any man, with the exception of [the right to] nullify your vows" - i.e., [I] retain none of the rights of the marriage relationship except [the right to] nullify your vows; in that regard, it is as if you remained my wife.<br>[Similarly, if the husband desires that the woman be considered as if she were married with regard to her right] to partake of <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the husband is a priest, and while married his wife may partake of <i>terumah</i>, although she is the daughter of an Israelite.</i> or with regard to his right to inherit her property if she dies [in his lifetime, the status of the divorce is in doubt].
[If a husband] tells [his wife]: "You are permitted [to marry] any man aside from Reuven and Shimon," and then tells her: "You are permitted [to marry] Reuven or Shimon," the divorce is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this refers to an instance where the husband takes the <i>get</i> back from the woman and gives it to her again, making the latter statement. This conception is also reflected in the ruling of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 137:1).<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 137) differs with the Rambam and maintains that in this instance the status of the divorce is in doubt. We suspect that perhaps the intent of his latter statement was to permit her to Reuven and Shimon, but to forbid her to everyone else. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 137:4) accepts the <i>Tur's</i> ruling. All authorities agree that if the husband says: "You are also permitted [to marry] Reuven or Shimon," the <i>get</i> is acceptable.</i> For he has nullified his condition, permitting his wife [to marry] the men [concerning whom] the condition was made.
[If, after telling his wife that she is permitted to marry any man aside from Reuven and Shimon, the husband] tells her: "You are permitted [to marry] Reuven," the divorce is void, because he has not nullified the condition [preventing her from marrying] Shimon.<br>If he tells her: "You are permitted to [marry] Shimon," or "You are even permitted to [marry] Shimon," the status of the divorce is in doubt. We suspect that perhaps he permitted her only to Shimon, and she is still forbidden to Reuven. By saying "even to Shimon," he meant "to everyone, even to Shimon." [Nevertheless, the divorce is not void, for] perhaps he permitted her to everyone, and by saying "even to Shimon," he meant "to Shimon and also to Reuven." [This is plausible,] for Shimon was mentioned after Reuven in his condition.
If [a husband] makes a condition, saying; "Today you are not my wife, but tomorrow you will be my wife," the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher and the Rashba differ and maintain that in such an instance, the divorce is effective. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 137:5) follows the view of Rabbenu Nissim, who maintains that the status of the divorce is in doubt.</i> Although the connection between the couple is severed on the day [of the <i>get</i>, it is renewed afterwards]. For this reason, in the wording of the <i>get</i>, is written the phrase: "from the present day onward."
[If a husband tells his wife:] "This is your <i>get</i> on the condition that you never drink wine for the rest of your life," the divorce is not effective, for [the relationship between them] has not been severed.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">There is a factor, the prohibition against drinking wine, that always maintains the connection between them.</i><br>If the condition states "for as long as I live" or "for as long as so and so lives," the divorce is effective, for the relationship between the couple is severed. When that person dies, [the woman] will no longer have any connection with [her previous husband].
[If a husband tells his wife:] "This is your <i>get</i> on the condition that you do not go to your father's house for 30 days, the divorce is effective. "...That you never go to your father's house," the divorce is not effective, for [the relationship between them] has not been severed.<br>[Similarly, if a husband] tells [his wife:] "This is your <i>get</i> on the condition that you never eat meat," or "...that you never drink wine," the divorce is not effective, for [the relationship between them] has not been severed. "...[that you not eat meat or drink wine] for 30 days, the divorce is effective.
[If a husband tells his wife:] "This is your <i>get</i> on the condition that you never marry so and so," the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Rashba differs with this ruling, explaining that since the other person may die, this can be compared to an instance where the husband makes a condition that his wife not drink wine during the duration of another person's life. Just as that condition is considered to be having a limit, so too, should this condition be considered to be limited.<br>In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo defends the Rambam's ruling, explaining that in the instance at hand, the woman will never be able to marry the man specified in the condition. As such, the condition is not considered limited. In his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:22), he quotes both opinions and states that the stringencies required by both views should be observed.</i> To what can this be compared? to his telling her: "This is your <i>get</i> on the condition that you never drink wine," "...that you never go to your father's home," or "[that you not go to your father's home] for the rest of your life."<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the condition is one that binds a woman to her husband for her entire life.</i><br>If, however, he tells her: "[This is your <i>get</i>] on the condition that you do not marry so and so for fifty years,"<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> quotes a responsum of the Rashbatz that states that the intent is that the marriage will be forbidden for a period longer than the woman is expected to live. Thus, though the husband has in effect prohibited the woman from marrying the other man forever, since this is not explicitly stated, the condition is considered to be limited in scope.</i> the divorce is effective; she may not marry the person during the time specified.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam, it appears that the woman is free to marry another man immediately, for we assume that she will abide by the condition, rather than place herself in a compromising situation. There are, however, other opinions that state that she is not allowed to remarry until the person specified dies. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:16) quotes both opinions without appearing to favor either one.</i> If she marries him, the divorce is nullified retroactively.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is considered as if the original marriage bond had never been severed. Any children born to her between the divorce and its nullification are deemed illegitimate.</i><br>If [the woman] engages in promiscuous sexual relations with the man specified, any child born to her is legitimate, and the divorce remains acceptable. For [the husband's] condition mentioned only marriage.
[If a husband tells his wife:] "This is your <i>get</i> on the condition that you marry so and so," if she marries him [the divorce is effective], as it would be if other conditions were involved. Nevertheless, our Sages said that she should not marry either the man specified or any other man.<br>She should not marry the man specified, lest the people say: "They are giving their wives as presents to each other."<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This would compromise the entire image of Jewish divorce. (See Rashi, <i>Gittin</i> 84a.)</i> Nor should she marry anyone else, for the <i>get</i> is effective only when the condition is fulfilled.<br>If she transgresses and marries the man specified, she need not be divorced. If she marries another person before she marries the man specified, the <i>get</i> is nullified, and she must leave her second husband. Any child she bears [the second husband] is illegitimate. He must, however, divorce her with a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The requirement for the woman's second husband to divorce her is a Rabbinic decree, instituted lest people see a woman who appears married leave her husband without a <i>get</i>. According to Scriptural law, a <i>get</i> is not necessary (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>The Ra'avad and the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143) suggest that she can have the legitimacy of the children of her second husband restored retroactively by marrying the person specified in the first <i>get</i> after being divorced by her second husband. In this way, the condition will have been kept.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> justifies the Rambam's ruling, explaining that the husband's intent was that the woman should marry the man specified before marrying anyone else. (The commentaries cite a parallel to this ruling in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 11:10.) The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:15) quotes the Rambam's view, while the Ramah follows that of the Ra'avad and the <i>Tur</i>.</i>
[If a husband tells his wife:] "Behold, this is your <i>get</i>, but the paper belongs to me," the divorce is not effective, for [the relationship between them] has not been severed.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband maintains possession of the paper on which the <i>get</i> is written, the couple's marriage relationship has not been totally severed. Rashi (<i>Gittin</i> 75b) offers a different interpretation of this ruling.</i> "...On the condition that you give me the paper," the divorce is effective, [provided] she gives [him the paper].<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">When she receives the <i>get</i>, the paper belongs to her. Afterwards, she willingly gives it back. This follows the principle that a present given on condition that it be returned is considered to be a present (Rashi, <i>Gittin</i> 20b).</i>
If he engraved a <i>get</i> on a plate of gold and gave it to her, telling her: "This is [both] your <i>get</i> and [the money due you by virtue of] your <i>ketubah</i>, [the divorce is] acceptable and she is considered to have received the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 20b explains that the fact that the same article serves two purposes - effecting the divorce and providing remuneration for the woman's marriage contract - does not prevent it from being effective.</i> provided the plate is worth the value of her <i>ketubah</i>. If it is not, he should compensate [for the difference].
[The following rules apply when a man] divorces [his wife] conditionally, including a stipulation that causes the divorce to be considered void - e.g., that she never eat meat or drink wine for the rest of her life, or that she be free to marry any man with the exception of a particular individual, or that he mentioned other conditions before the essential portion of the <i>get</i> was written: If the condition was written in the <i>get</i>, and he rubbed it out and gave it to her, the status of the divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>get</i> was written in an unacceptable manner, there is reason to maintain that the <i>get</i> can never be acceptable. This applies when the unacceptable condition was written before the essential portion of the <i>get</i>.<br>The wording used by the Rambam is, however, questionable. The Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 9:1) and the gloss of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> on this halachah use the word <i>pasul</i> "unacceptable," rather than "the status of the divorce is in doubt" to describe such a <i>get</i>. Perhaps here, the Rambam uses the term "the status of the divorce is in doubt," because, as cited above from the Rambam's responsa, according to the Rambam, the question in these situations is whether the <i>get</i> is written for the sake of severing the marriage bond. And this is a question of Scriptural law (Rav Kapach).</i> If the condition was stated verbally,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that this refers to a condition stated after the essential portion of the <i>get</i> was written. If such a condition was stated before the essential portion was written, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable, because it was not written with the intent of severing the marriage relationship. See Halachah 4 and notes.</i> he should take the <i>get</i> from her and give it to her again without mentioning a condition, or mention a condition that is acceptable.
What is implied? [A man] gave a <i>get</i> to his wife and told her, "Behold, you are divorced with this [<i>get</i>] and are free [to marry] any man aside from so and so."<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages decreed that unless the <i>get</i> is returned, it not effective, even if the condition was nullified. Since the original giving of such a <i>get</i> has a permanent effect - the woman is forbidden to marry a priest (or to remain married to her husband, if he is a priest), as stated in Chapter 10, Halachah 1 - the only way such a condition can be nullified is by returning the <i>get</i> to the husband, and having him give it again without any condition.<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 147:6 states that this law applies only with regard to a condition that prevents her from marrying a particular man that was stated after the essential portion of the <i>get</i> was written. When a condition that nullifies the <i>get</i> was written before the essential portion of the <i>get</i> was written, the <i>get</i> is void and cannot be rectified. When a stipulation such as "on the condition that you do not drink wine for the rest of your life" was made after the essential portion of the <i>get</i> was written, there is no need to take the <i>get</i> back from the woman. It is sufficient to nullify the stipulation.</i> If he took [the <i>get</i>] from her and gave it to her and said: "Behold you are free [to marry] any man," or "This is your <i>get</i>," the divorce is effective. The same applies in other similar situations.
When [a man] gives a <i>get</i> to his wife on the condition that she give him two hundred <i>zuz</i>, and afterwards establishes a second condition in the presence of witnesses that she serve his father for two years, his latter statement did not nullify his original one.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that this refers to an instance where the condition was stated in a twofold statement: "If you do such and such, the <i>get</i> is effective, but if you do not do such and such, the <i>get</i> is not effective." In such instances, since the divorce is not effective until the condition is fulfilled, the husband may alter the condition. When, however, a <i>get</i> is given "on condition that... ," the divorce is retroactively effective from the time the <i>get</i> was given. Hence, the condition cannot be changed.<br>Rashi (<i>Gittin</i> 76a) offers a different interpretation of this passage. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:10) follows the Rambam's interpretation, while the Ramah follows that of Rashi.</i> Instead, it is as if he told her: "Fulfill one of the two conditions." If she desires, she may serve his father, or if she desires, she may give [him the money]. One of the witnesses to the first condition cannot join with one of the witnesses to the second condition [to testify that the <i>get</i> was given on a conditional basis].<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For each of the conditions is considered to be a separate matter.</i><br>If, however, [the husband] made a condition that the woman must give him 200 <i>zuz</i>, and afterwards made a condition in the presence of two [witnesses] that she must give him 300 <i>zuz</i>, the condition of 200 has been nullified, and she must give [him] 300 <i>zuz</i>. The same principle applies in all similar situations.
If [a husband] makes a condition that his wife must perform a specific activity without stating a duration of time, it is considered as if he stated that she should perform this activity for one day. For he did not specify any amount of time.<br>What is implied? If he told her: "This is your <i>get</i> on condition that you perform work for me," "...on condition that you serve my father," or "...on condition that you nurse my son," if she performs work for him or serves his father for one day or nurses his son for one day during the time when a child [usually] nurses - i.e., within his first 24 months [of life] - the <i>get</i> is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling reflects an interesting halachic judgment on the Rambam's part. <i>Gittin</i> 75b explains that the ruling cited by the Rambam was authored by Rav Ashi. Although the Gemara poses questions on Rav Ashi's view that are left unresolved, since his position was not refuted, according to the Rambam, the halachah follows his view.<br>The <i>Tur</i> follows a different view and maintains that the woman must serve her husband's father for the duration of the father's life and nurse the son for the entire two-year period. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:8) follows the Rambam's view, while the Ramah cites that of the <i>Tur</i>.</i>If the son or the father dies before she nurses him or serves him, the <i>get</i> is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For the condition has not been fulfilled.</i>
If [the husband] tells her: "[This is your <i>get</i>] on condition that you nurse my son..." or "...serve my father for two years," [for the divorce to be effective] she must complete the time specified. If the son or the father dies within this period, or the father says: "I don't want you to serve me," the <i>get</i> is not effective, for the condition has not been fulfilled.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143) differs and states that since the woman did not prevent the condition from being fulfilled, the <i>get</i> is effective. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:8) does not accept this view. The Ramah states that the more stringent view should be followed.</i> The same principles apply in all similar situations.
[The following rules apply when a husband] tells [his wife]: "[This is your <i>get</i>] on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i> within 30 days." If she gives him [the money] within 30 days with his consent, the divorce is effective. If she gives [it] after 30 days, the divorce is not effective. If she gives it to him against his will, and he refuses to accept it, the divorce is unacceptable until he willingly accepts it.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion among our Sages regarding whether a person who forces a colleague to accept something is considered to have given it to him or not. Although the view that he is considered to have given the object is favored, the Rabbis deemed the divorce unacceptable in consideration of the other view (<i>Gittin</i> 74a, 75a).<br>More specifically, it appears that the Rambam maintains that according to Scriptural law, the <i>get</i> is effective, and it is disqualified merely by virtue of Rabbinic decree, while Rabbenu Asher and the Rashba maintain that, according to Scriptural law, there is a doubt regarding the issue (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 143:7).</i><br>If he tells her within the 30 days, "I am willing to forego [the money on your behalf," the divorce is not effective, because the condition was not fulfilled.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected in Halachah 23, a husband can nullify a condition that he makes to a <i>get</i>. In this instance, however, he is not nullifying the condition, but rather trying to have it satisfied in a way other than that which he originally intended. This is not acceptable. Originally, he made the condition to annoy the woman, and since she did not suffer this worrying, the divorce is not effective unless he nullifies the condition entirely (<i>Gittin</i> 74b; <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> If he dies within the 30 days, since the 30 days are completed without her giving [him the money], the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, if the husband dies without children, she is required to perform either the rites of <i>yibbum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i>.</i>
If he tells her, "[This is your <i>get</i>] on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>," without specifying a time period, and he dies before she gives [him the money], she cannot give [the sum] to his heirs; the condition was that it be given to him. [In this instance, however,] the <i>get</i> is not nullified [by the husband's death], since a definite time period was not specified.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">When a specific time period is mentioned and the condition is not fulfilled in that period, the divorce is nullified. In this instance, however, since a specific time is not mentioned, the Rambam maintains that the divorce is never nullified.<br>Rav Kapach explains that the Rambam's approach is based on his conception (see Halachah 1 of this chapter, and Chapter 9, Halachot 1 and 5) that when a <i>get</i> is given on a conditional basis, the <i>get</i> takes effect immediately, but it is not completed until the condition is fulfilled. Therefore, in this instance, since the condition can never be fulfilled, the divorce is not completed, and <i>chalitzah</i> is necessary if the husband dies childless. Nevertheless, <i>yibbum</i> cannot be performed, for the divorce has already begun.<br>Other commentaries offer different interpretations of the Rambam's views. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143) rejects the Rambam's ruling and maintains that the woman may also perform the rite of <i>yibbum</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:3) follows the Rambam's view.</i> Therefore, even if the <i>get</i> was lost or ripped before he died, [the woman] should not marry another man until she performs the rite of <i>chalitzah</i>.
[If the husband tells her:] "This is your <i>get</i> on condition that you give me this particular utensil," or "...this particular garment," and that utensil or garment is lost or stolen, even if she gives him 1000 <i>zuz</i> in compensation for [the desired object],<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143) differs and explains that the husband has the option of accepting the money in lieu of the object originally mentioned. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:6) mentions both views, it appears to favor that of the Rambam.</i> the divorce is not effective until she gives him the specified utensil or garment, or until he nullifies the condition.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he tells the woman, "This is your <i>get</i>; there are no conditions attached." He does not have to take the <i>get</i> back from the woman.</i>
[The following rules apply when a woman] is divorced conditionally, and another man consecrates her before the condition is fulfilled. If the condition is fulfilled [afterwards], she is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 146:5 states that this applies when the husband gave the <i>get</i> "on the condition that...," for in such an instance the divorce is effective retroactively from the time the <i>get</i> was given. (With regard to other conditions, see the notes on Halachah 1.)</i> If, however, the condition is not fulfilled and the <i>get</i> is nullified,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., the condition was limited to a specific time span, as in Halachah 22, or the condition was that she would not perform a specific act and she did perform it. If, however, the condition involves an act that she is capable of performing, we suspect that she has fulfilled the condition (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Shulchan Aruch</i> and Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 146:5).</i> she does not require a <i>get</i> from the second husband,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's decision and maintains that since it was possible for the woman to have fulfilled the condition, we suspect that perhaps she did in fact fulfill it. Therefore, she is forbidden to remain married to her first husband. It is the Rambam's view that is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>:4).</i> for [in this situation] she cannot be consecrated. If, however, she marries [a second husband], the condition is not fulfilled, and the <i>get</i> is nullified, she must also be divorced by the second [husband], as explained above.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 13.</i>

Chapter 9

[When a man] divorces his wife and [asserts that the divorce] will not take effect until a specific time passes, the divorce takes effect when that time arrives. Thus, this resembles [a <i>get</i> given on] a conditional basis, and yet it is not a conditional <i>get</i>.<br>It resembles [a <i>get</i> given on] a conditional basis because the divorce takes effect when the specified time arrives [as in the case of a conditional <i>get</i>, which is completed when the condition is fulfilled]. Nevertheless, it is not considered a conditional <i>get</i> because a person who divorces [his wife] conditionally has already performed the divorce, and in this instance, the man has not divorced her until the time arrives.<br>For this reason, a person who gives a conditional <i>get</i> must restate the condition [in both a positive and negative form], while this person does not have to restate his words. Nor must he comply with the other requirements of conditional agreements that we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See the notes on Chapter 8, Halachah 1.</i>.
What is implied? [When a husband] tells his wife: "This is your <i>get</i>, but the divorce does not take effect until 30 days pass," she is not divorced until after the passage of 30 days. If her husband dies, or the <i>get</i> is lost or consumed by fire in the interim, she is not divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband dies she is not divorced, because a divorce must take effect before death. If the <i>get</i> is destroyed, she is not divorced because it is the <i>get</i> that serves as the medium of divorce, and at the time when the <i>get</i> must take effect, it no longer exists.</i>
[When the woman] went and placed [the <i>get</i>] in [a place] at the side of the public domain,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The public domain was a marketplace with pillars on either side. At the side of the pillars, there was space that though ownerless, could be used to acquire property, because it was not common for people to walk in that space. (See <i>Hilchot Gezelah Va'Avedah</i> 17:9.)</i> and after 30 days passed it was stolen or lost, the divorce is effective. Since the <i>get</i> existed on the day when it took effect, and it was set aside in a designated place that is not public domain [it can serve as the medium for the divorce].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and others contrast the Rambam's ruling here, that the divorce is not retroactively effective from the time the husband gave the <i>get</i>, to his ruling in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 2:9. That source states that a sale can be effective when made with the stipulation that it take effect after 30 days, only when the stipulation is that it take effect retroactively. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> differentiates between the two instances, explaining that a sale requires an act of contract to effect a transfer, and that act took place when the sale was originally made, not 30 days afterwards. In contrast, with regard to a <i>get</i>, the fundamental aspect of the divorce is effected by the writing of the <i>get</i>.</i> [The governing principle is that the area at] the sides of the public domain is not the same as the public domain itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Had the <i>get</i> been placed by the woman in the public domain itself, it would not be effective even if it existed when the time specified by the husband arrived. Since it left the woman's domain entirely, the giving of the <i>get</i> originally performed by the husband is no longer considered significant. And, as stated at the beginning of these laws, one of the requirements of a divorce is that the <i>get</i> be given to the woman by her husband.</i>
Similarly, if the husband makes the divorce dependent on an action,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in this instance as well, there is a resemblance to the laws of a conditional divorce. Nevertheless, since different wording is used, the laws vary.</i> the same rules that govern a <i>get</i> that takes effect after a specific time apply. For example, [a husband] said to [his] wife: "This is your <i>get</i>, but the divorce does not take effect until you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>." After [the woman] gives [the money], the divorce takes effect.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Moreover, the action specified by the husband may be something that does not require anything to be done by his wife. For example, he may tell her that the divorce will take effect after the first rainfall (Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Kiddushin</i> 3:3).</i><br>In this instance [as well], there is no need to restate the condition [in both a positive and negative form], nor to comply with the other requirements of conditional agreements that we have explained. For this person has not divorced [his wife] conditionally - he has not yet divorced her at all. Instead, he made the divorce dependent on a specific action, and after [that action is completed], the divorce will take effect.
What is the difference between a conditional divorce and a divorce that will take effect only after a fixed time, or a divorce dependent on an action? With regard to a conditional divorce, the divorce is a fact; it is, however, not completed until the condition is fulfilled. Therefore, when the condition is fulfilled, the divorce takes effect as long as the <i>get</i> exists, even if it is not in the woman's possession. When the condition is fulfilled, there is no need for [the woman] to pick up [the <i>get</i>] or take possession, for it was given to her for the purpose of divorce. If she married before the condition was fulfilled, she need not leave [her second husband], as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 8, Halachah 1. See the notes on that halachah.</i><br>With regard to a divorce that will take effect only after a fixed time or a divorce dependent on an action, by contrast, she was not given the <i>get</i> for the [immediate] purpose of divorce. Instead, it is considered to be an entrusted object until the specified time arrives or she performs the action. Therefore, it is necessary that the <i>get</i> be in her possession at that time, that she take [possession of it], or that it be in a place that she designated for it even though it is not in her domain, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 3. If the <i>get</i> is not in the woman's possession at this time, the divorce is not effective according to the Rambam. Nevertheless, other authorities have a different conception of these laws, and according to them, the status of the divorce would be doubtful (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Beit Shmuel</i> 146:3).</i> Only then, is she divorced by virtue [of this <i>get</i>].<br>If she marries before the specified time arrives, or before she performs the action on which the divorce is dependent, she must leave her second husband,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For their relations are considered adulterous.</i> and any child [born of their union] is considered illegitimate. For she is a married woman, and the divorce is not [yet] effective.
When [a man] gives his wife a <i>get</i> and tells her: "If you do not give me 200 <i>zuz</i>, this <i>get</i> is not effective," or "...you are not divorced," he is not considered to have performed a divorce at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">For he did not preface his statements by saying: "This is your <i>get</i>," or "You are divorced...." Hence, he is not considered to have given the <i>get</i> for the sake of divorce. Although such an intent could be deduced from his words, since his statements were not explicit, they are of no consequence. Indeed, making such a statement is less effective than not saying anything at all (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Kiryat Melech Rav</i>).</i> For the <i>get</i> was not given on a conditional basis, nor was it made dependent on an action. The same [ruling applies] in all similar situations.
When a person would like to give a conditional divorce, with the condition being that the divorce not take effect until a particular time, he should express the concept in a conditional statement, making the condition dependent on his departure or return at a specific time.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he desires that the <i>get</i> not take effect until a specific time, but does not want the restrictions mentioned in the previous halachah to apply. This is not merely a theoretical situation. Such <i>gittin</i> were given frequently in previous generations when a man undertook an overseas journey and wished to spare his wife from having to remain unmarried if he was killed, kidnapped by pirates or in other ways prevented from returning against his will.</i><br>What is implied? He tells her: "If I do not return between the present time and 30 days, this <i>get</i> is effective. If I do return within 30 days, this <i>get</i> is not effective." He [then] gives her the <i>get</i>. Alternatively, he should tell her: "This is your <i>get</i> on condition that I do not return to this country within 30 days." The same [ruling applies] in all similar situations.
When a person divorces [his wife] on the condition that he not return to this country within 30 days, and he set out to return within 30 days, but became ill or was prevented [from returning by a flooding] river and did not return until after 30 days, the <i>get</i> is effective. Even if he protests, "I am being held back by forces beyond my control" [the <i>get</i> is effective].<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 30a cites an example of a man who divorced his wife on condition that he not return within 30 days. A river washed away the bridge leading to his city. Although he stood on the river banks and called to his townsmen: "See, I have returned," our Sages ruled that he is not considered to have returned, and they considered the divorce binding.</i><br>[The rationale is that the claim,] "I was prevented by forces beyond my control," is not accepted with regard to <i>gittin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">According to Scriptural law, a person who is prevented from fulfilling a condition by forces beyond his control is not considered as having failed to fulfill it. Nevertheless, our Sages ruled that the laws of divorce are an exception to this principle, because modest women might presume that their husbands were prevented from fulfilling their condition by forces beyond their control and never remarry (<i>Ketubot</i> 2b).<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes the opinion of <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Ketubot</i> 2b) and others, who state that this principle applies only with regard to events beyond mortal control that one could possibly foresee - e.g., illness or flood. It does not apply with regard to events beyond a person's control that are totally unlikely - e.g., earthquakes. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> cites statements by the Rambam in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i>, Chapter 19, which indicate that he would also accept this restriction.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh's</i> premise is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 144:1). It appears that the determination of what is an event beyond a person's control that is totally unlikely is dependent on the sociological conditions of the age. For example, the Ramah rules that being held as a captive is not a totally unlikely occurrence.</i> even if the husband makes it evident that he does not desire that the divorce take effect.
[The following rules apply when a man gives a] divorce on the condition<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As will be explained, this law applies when the husband stated "on condition that..." when giving the <i>get</i>.</i> that it become effective if 30 days pass without [his seeing his wife's] face. If he repeatedly came [and appeared before her], but never entered into privacy with her, and then 30 days [passed without his seeing her], the <i>get</i> is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since ultimately the condition was fulfilled.</i> Although he came [and appeared before her] within 30 days [after the <i>get</i> was given], since he did not enter into privacy with her,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If he entered into privacy with her, the <i>get</i> would be nullified. See Chapter 3, Halachah 5, Chapter 8, Halachah 2.</i> [once the condition was fulfilled,] the <i>get</i> is effective.<br>When does the above apply? When he made the condition and said: "I accept her word with regard to [whether or] not I appeased her."<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In such an instance, if the woman were to say that she never agreed to her husband's spending more than 30 days away from her, the <i>get</i> would be effective after the condition is fulfilled.</i> If, however, he did not accept her word, we suspect that perhaps he placated her when he came and visited her, she was willing to forego [her desire to be with him], and he nullified the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The husband gave his wife the <i>get</i> because he was going away on a journey and knew that she would not desire to be alone. If he was able to appease her and she was willing to remain alone for 30 days, he is able to nullify the <i>get</i>. Usually, once a <i>get</i> is given "on condition that...," it cannot be nullified once it has been given. Nevertheless, in this instance, since the condition was made for the sake of the woman, if she is willing to forego seeing her husband, the husband may nullify the <i>get</i> (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> For this reason, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable [even] after 30 days [pass without the husband seeing her].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to Scriptural law, since the condition was fulfilled, and there is no evidence that the couple resolved their differences, the <i>get</i> is binding. Our Sages, nevertheless, disqualified the <i>get</i> for the reasons stated above.</i><br>Similarly, when a man tells a woman: "This is your <i>get</i> and [it becomes effective] after twelve months pass," if he lives in the same city as she does, we suspect that he appeased her, unless he said: "I accept her word that I did not appease her."
A similar [principle applies with regard to] all conditions that are dependent on her will, and the <i>get</i> would be nullified if she was willing to forego these conditions in favor of her husband. We suspect that perhaps he appeased her,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the divorce is unacceptable.</i> unless he said: "I accept her word...."<br>When does the above apply? When the woman is divorced after <i>nisu'in</i>, for he is familiar with her.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">And we assume that because of their familiarity, he knows how to appease her, and she is more willing to accept his overtures.</i> When, however, [a husband] divorces [his wife] after [merely] consecrating her, we do not suspect that he appeased her.
[When a man gives his wife a <i>get</i> and tells her:] "This is your <i>get</i> [and it is effective] from the present time onward if I do not return within twelve months," we do not suspect that perhaps he returned in secrecy, for people do not generally come in secrecy. [Therefore,] if the time period he specified passes without his coming, the divorce is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's decision is based on his interpretation of <i>Gittin</i> 76b. The Ra'avad interprets that passage more stringently and requires the husband to have accepted his wife's statements as to whether or not he came as binding. Otherwise, we suspect that he came in secrecy. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 144:7) follows the Rambam's view, but states that <i>a priori</i>, it is customary to include the condition mentioned by the Ra'avad.</i><br>If he dies within the twelve months, although the divorce [will become] effective, for there is no way that he can come, if she would have been obligated to perform the rite of <i>yibbum</i>, she should not marry until the twelve months pass and the condition is fulfilled.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If she desires to marry within the twelve months, she must undergo the rite of <i>chalitzah</i> first.</i>
When a healthy person [divorces his wife] on the condition that the <i>get</i> take effect if he dies, and when a sick person [divorces his wife] on the condition that the <i>get</i> take effect if he dies from the illness afflicting him,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 145:8) quotes the Rashba, who states that even if a sick person does not specify that he die from the illness afflicting him, we assume that this is his intent.</i> their statements are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the advantage of such a <i>get</i> would be to free his wife of the obligation of <i>yibbum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i>. Similarly, the subsequent halachot refer to <i>gittin</i> given for this purpose.</i> The expression "if I die" implies [that it takes effect] after his death. [Alternatively,] the intent is that [it take effect] retroactively [if he dies]. [Because of the doubt involved,] if [a man] uses the expression "If I die...," it is considered as if he said "after my death." [The <i>get</i> is therefore void, because] there is no concept of divorce after a person's death.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's ruling. He maintains that the date of the <i>get</i> indicates that the husband desires that the <i>get</i> take effect retroactively. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 145:1) quotes the Rambam's view, but mentions a minority opinion that takes the Ra'avad's view into consideration.</i>
If, [however, the husband] tells her: "This is your <i>get</i>. If I die, [it is effective retroactively] from the present time," or "...If I die, [it is effective retroactively] from the present day,"<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> quote Rabbenu Tam as stating that it is preferable for the person to say: "from the present time," rather than "from the present day," lest he die before the conclusion of the day. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 145:2) quotes this suggestion.</i> the <i>get</i> is valid. When he dies, [his wife] is divorced.
[If a husband] says: "This is your <i>get</i>. If I die, [it is effective retroactively] from the present time - or from the present day - after my death," when [the husband] dies, the status of his wife's divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, if childless, she must perform the rite of <i>chalitzah</i> before marrying, and may not perform the rite of <i>yibbum</i>. As stated in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 18:25, such a woman is not entitled to receive support from her husband's estate after his death.</i> [We suspect that] perhaps, after saying "from the present time," he changed his mind and [did not desire that the <i>get</i> become effective] from the present time, and instead take effect after his death. And there is no concept of divorce after a person's death.
[If a husband] says: "This is your <i>get</i>. [It becomes effective] when the sun emerges from its shield," it is not effective if he dies that night.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For the <i>get</i> does not become effective until the morning. Since he died beforehand, a <i>get</i> cannot be effective after death.</i> [If he gave the <i>get</i>] on the condition that the sun shine and he dies that night, the <i>get</i> is effective, for when the sun shines [on the following day], the condition that he established becomes fulfilled.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For a <i>get</i> given "on the condition that" becomes effective retroactively from the time it was originally given, once the condition is fulfilled.</i><br>If [the husband gives a <i>get</i> and] establishes a condition: "If the sun shines, the <i>get</i> is effective. But if it does not shine, it is not effective," [the <i>get</i>] is not effective if he dies at night. For the condition was not fulfilled until after he died,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>get</i> given according to the rules of conditional agreements is not effective until the condition is fulfilled.</i> and there is no concept of divorce after a person's death.
When a person who is mortally ill has a <i>get</i> written for his wife and divorces her and then recovers, he cannot retract the divorce. A divorce [performed by a person in such a state] is not [governed by] the same laws as a present he gives.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 8:14, when a person who is mortally ill gives away all of his property as a present and then recovers, the present is automatically retracted, for we assume that his intent was solely to distribute his property after his death.</i> [The rationale for this distinction is] that if he were given the prerogative of retracting, the divorce would appear to take effect after death, in the same way that a present he gives is not legally transferred until after death.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the two laws are in direct contradiction. A present given by a dying man does not take effect until after he dies, and a divorce that he grants cannot take effect after he dies.</i>
[If a husband says:] "This is your <i>get</i>. [It is effective retroactively] from the present day if I die from this illness," and his house collapses upon him, he is bit by a snake, devoured by a lion or dies because of other similar causes, the divorce is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For the condition that he stated was not fulfilled (<i>Gittin</i> 73a).</i>
[If, by contrast,] he tells her: "...if I do not arise from this sickness," and his house collapses upon him, he is bit by a snake or devoured by a lion, the status of the divorce is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">On the one hand, he did not arise from his sickbed. On the other hand, there is reason to assume that his intent was that he is giving the <i>get</i> on the condition that he die from the illness afflicting him, and this was not the case (<i>Gittin</i> 73a).</i><br>[The following rules apply when a husband tells his wife:] "Here is your <i>get</i>. [It is effective retroactively] from the present day if I die from this illness," and then rises [from his sickbed], walks in the marketplace,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if he walks in the marketplace without any support. Since he did not regain his health entirely, it is necessary to determine whether or not he died from the first illness. Note the contrast to <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 8:26.</i> and afterwards falls ill and dies. We make an assessment. If he died from the first illness, the divorce is effective. If not, it is not effective. If he progresses from one illness to another illness,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 145:14, who states that, according to the Rambam, for the divorce to be effective the man must still be afflicted with the first illness when he dies. Note the contrast to <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 8:27.</i> and does not arise [and walk] in the marketplace, the divorce is effective. There is no need to make an assessment.
With regard to all the conditions [mentioned above], during the days between the giving of the <i>get</i> and his death [with its consequent] fulfillment of the condition, she is considered to be a divorced woman with regard to all matters,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he has none of the privileges of a husband - e.g., he is not entitled to her earnings, he may not nullify her vows, and if he is a priest, he may not become impure because of her in the event of her death. With regard to paying for the woman's support, most authorities maintain that the husband is liable. There is no explicit statement from the Rambam regarding this issue.</i> provided he does not enter into privacy with her, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In such an instance, we suspect that the couple may have engaged in marital relations, and that the husband nullified the <i>get</i> beforehand.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a sick person desires to divorce his wife conditionally, so that if he dies she will not be obligated to perform the rite of <i>yibbum</i>, but if he recovers, the divorce will not be effective; he does not desire that the <i>get</i> take effect [retroactively] from the time he gives it, lest the matter be overly disturbing for him.<br>He should write the following in the <i>get</i> after writing the essential portion; alternatively, he should make these statements when he gives [his wife] the <i>get</i>: "If I do not die, this <i>get</i> will not be effective. If I die, this <i>get</i> will be effective. If I do not die, this <i>get</i> will not be effective." In this manner, the conditional factor is repeated. The positive factor is stated before the negative factor,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the requirement is not that the positive factor be stated first, but that it be stated before the negative factor. This is accomplished by restating the condition that would negate the <i>get</i>.</i> and the person's opening remarks do not speak of misfortune.<br>[If a <i>get</i> is given with such a condition,] the divorce will take effect when [the husband] dies,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> questions the Rambam's ruling, noting that from his previous decisions, it appears that unless the man says that the <i>get</i> will take effect retroactively <i>me'achshav</i>, "from this time onward," the <i>get</i> is not effective, because it does not take effect until after the man's death. Apparently, the Rambam maintains that the restatement of the condition resolves that difficulty, but this concept is not accepted by many other authorities. When stating this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 145:5) quotes the Rambam's wording, but adds the word <i>me'achshav</i>.</i> provided that the <i>get</i> reaches the woman before his death.
When a husband tells a colleague:<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the colleague was not appointed by the wife to serve as an agent to receive the <i>get</i>. The husband appointed him as her agent, because he felt that she would not desire to marry her <i>yavam</i>. According to Torah law, one may perform an action on a person's behalf without his knowledge if it is to his advantage - e.g., one may take possession of an ownerless object on behalf of a colleague without asking him. Hence, it is possible to assume that the agent can act on behalf of the woman without her knowledge (<i>Yevamot</i> 118b).</i> "Acquire this <i>get</i> on behalf of my wife, so that she will not be required to fulfill the rite of <i>yibbum</i>," and gives him the <i>get</i>, but the husband dies before the <i>get</i> reaches [the woman], the status of the divorce is in doubt. [Although there are times when a woman would be willing to marry the <i>yavam</i>,] it is to the advantage of most women not to be required to fulfill the rite of <i>yibbum</i>. Therefore, even though the <i>get</i> did not reach the woman, since another person acquired it on her behalf, the status of the divorce is in doubt.
When [a man] tells witnesses: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife after twelve months," or "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife and give it to her after twelve months," they should write [the <i>get</i>] and give it to her after the time he specified. If they write it within the time he specified,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Because by writing it within this time, they deviated from the instructions with which they were charged (<i>Gittin</i> 76b).</i> it is void, even if they do not give it to the woman until afterwards.<br>If they write it after the time he specified, but [the husband] dies before they give it to her, it is void. If it is not known whether he died before the <i>get</i> was given, or the <i>get</i> was given before he died, the status of the divorce is in doubt.
[If a husband] instructs [agents]: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife and give it to her after the Sabbatical cycle," they must write it within the first year after the conclusion of the Sabbatical cycle.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although the wording of the Hebrew original could possibly be interpreted otherwise, the commentaries agree that the Rambam's intent is that the <i>get</i> may be written immediately after the time specified and should not be written after a prolonged period has elapsed from that time.</i> If he told them: "...after a year," they must write it during the first month of the coming year.<br>If he told them: "...after a month," they must write it during the first week of the coming month. If he told them: "...after the Sabbath," they must write it before the end of Tuesday. If he told them: "...before the Sabbath," they must write it between Wednesday and the end of Friday.
If they delayed and did not write and give the <i>get</i> at the time he specified - e.g., he told them "after a month," and they wrote the <i>get</i> and gave it to her after two weeks had passed in the second month, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>get</i> is unacceptable because the husband's words also imply a restriction, that the <i>get</i> should be written directly afterwards and not after a prolonged period. Indeed, for this reason, Rabbenu Nissim [and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 144:6)] rule that in such a situation, the status of the divorce is in doubt. In defense of the Rambam's ruling, the commentaries explain that even though the husband's intent may have been that the <i>get</i> be given immediately, since the wording he used could be interpreted to mean after a prolonged period, the <i>get</i> is acceptable according to Scriptural law.</i>
If [the husband] entered into privacy with [his wife] after he told [agents] to write [a <i>get</i>], sign it and give it to her, they should not write it. One can make an [obvious] inference. If a <i>get</i> that he gave her is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is referring to his statements in Chapter 8, Halachah 2. There, however, he uses the expression, "the status of the <i>get</i> is in doubt."</i> when he enters into privacy with her, lest they have engaged in marital relations, how much more so should a <i>get</i> that was not yet written [be left unwritten].
