If [the agents] wrote the <i>get</i> and gave it to her after he entered into privacy with her, the <i>get</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries question the Rambam's decision, explaining that although there is a possibility that the couple engaged in marital relations and the husband consecrated her at that time (see Chapter 10, Halachah 18), it is also possible that this did not take place. Indeed, in Chapter 3, Halachah 5, with reference to a <i>get</i> given by a husband after he entered into privacy with his wife, the Rambam rules that after the fact, the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<br>[It is possible to differentiate between the two instances, because in that halachah, the husband himself gave the woman the <i>get</i>. This is a greater indication that he did not nullify it (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). Even after that explanation, however, the Rambam's ruling in this halachah raises questions.]<br>When referring to this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 149:7) accepts the ruling that at the outset, the witnesses should not have such a <i>get</i> written, but follows the ruling of the Ramban and the Rashba, who maintain that if they had the <i>get</i> written and gave it to the woman, the status of the divorce is in doubt.</i>
[If a man] tells ten people: "Write a <i>get</i> for my wife," one of them should write it on behalf of all of them. [If he tells them:] "All of you, write it," one should write it in the presence of all of them.<br>[If he tells them:] "Bring this <i>get</i> to my wife," one of them should bring it to her on behalf of all of them. [If he tells them:] "All of you, bring this <i>get</i> to my wife," one should bring it [to her] in the presence of all of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This is the ruling according to Scriptural law. See, however, Halachah 28.</i>
[If a man] tells ten people: "Write a <i>get</i>, sign it and give it to my wife," one of them should write it, two should sign it, and one should give it to her. It is acceptable even if one person writes it, he serves as one of the two witnesses who signs it, and he serves as the agent who gives it to her.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">There are many authorities who object to this ruling, maintaining that the scribe cannot sign as a witness to the <i>get</i>. In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo states that even the Rambam agrees that at the outset, a scribe should not sign as a witness. After the fact, however, the Rambam maintains that his signature does not disqualify the <i>get</i>. In his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:18), Rav Yosef Karo writes: "One should be careful not to have the scribe sign as a witness, because there are authorities who disqualify this."</i><br>If [the husband] tells them: "All of you sign it," they must all sign it. If the husband counted the people - whether he counted all of them or merely some of them<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., speaking to the people, he said: "One, two, three, four, write a <i>get</i>..." (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 120:18).</i> - and told them to sign it, it is considered as if he told all of them to sign it. The two who sign it at the outset act as witnesses,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam, it appears that not only must the signatures of the acceptable witnesses be first chronologically, but they must also appear first on the <i>get</i>. Other authorities do not make this requirement and maintain that it is only chronological precedence that is significant (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 120:14).</i> while the others [should sign it to complete] the stipulation [the husband] made.<br>Accordingly, if the remaining witnesses were unacceptable, or one signed it on the day the <i>get</i> was written and the others on subsequent days<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The two witnesses whose signatures are required must sign the <i>get</i> on the day that it was written (Chapter 1, Halachah 25).</i> - even several days after [the <i>get</i> was written],<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 120:9) states that they may sign the <i>get</i> to fulfill the condition even after it was given to the woman. The Ramah adds that this may be done even after she has remarried.</i> the <i>get</i> is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i> and subsequent Ashkenazic authorities differ and maintain that for the <i>get</i> to be valid, all the witnesses must be acceptable. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 120:10) mentions both views, but appears to favor that of the Rambam. The Ramah follows <i>Tosafot's</i> ruling.</i><br>If one of them died before signing it, the <i>get</i> is void.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Because the instructions that the husband gave concerning the <i>get</i> were not fulfilled.</i> If one of the first witnesses who signed it was unacceptable, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable, lest it be said that an unacceptable witness may sign other legal documents when many witnesses sign.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 5:6-7, which states that if persons who are unacceptable as witnesses signed a legal document with the intent of serving as witnesses, the document is disqualified, even if two acceptable witnesses signed it first. If, however, the persons who were unacceptable did not sign the document with the intent of serving as witnesses, the document is acceptable, even if the signature of the unacceptable person appears first.</i> The only reason [our Sages] accepted [such signatures] with regard to a <i>get</i> signed by many witnesses is that the witnesses who observe the transfer are of fundamental importance.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 1, Halachah 15. With regard to other legal documents, the witnesses who sign the contract are of paramount importance, and they must all therefore be acceptable. The witnesses who observe its transfer are less significant. But with regard to <i>gittin</i>, it is the witnesses who observe the transfer who are of primary importance. Therefore, if the initial two signatures to the <i>get</i> are acceptable, we are not concerned with those who sign subsequently (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Kessef Mishneh, Hilchot Edut</i>).</i>
Our Sages established the [following] rules with regard to a person who tells many people to write, sign, or bring a <i>get</i> for his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">These rules were instituted lest the the husband tell a group of people that they all should write, sign or bring a <i>get</i>, and they not appreciate that they were all charged with this responsibility. Lest some of them fail to discharge this responsibility and thus cause the <i>get</i> to be void, our Sages required that whenever many people are instructed to be involved in a <i>get</i>, they all must take full participatory roles.</i> With regard to writing, he should tell them: "Any one of you may write a <i>get</i> for my wife." Similarly, with regard to bringing [the <i>get</i>], [he should say]: "Any one of you may bring...." With regard to signing, he should tell them: "Any pair of you should sign this <i>get</i> and give it to my wife."
Why did our Sages state that the witnesses to a <i>get</i> should sign only in each other's presence?<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 24, based on <i>Gittin</i> 10b. If the witnesses do not sign in each other's presence, the <i>get</i> is unacceptable (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 130:13).</i> [This is] a decree, [instituted] lest a person tell many others: "All of you sign [as witnesses]." If it were possible for witnesses to sign outside the presence of the other witnesses, two witnesses might sign the <i>get</i>, and the woman might take it and think that it does not require any more witnesses, [when in fact] the condition that [the husband made] was not fulfilled.
If [a man] tells three people, including a father and a son, "Two of you should write a <i>get</i> for my wife, sign it and give it to her," the <i>get</i> is acceptable whether the son signs with the other person or the father signs with the other person.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The father and the son may not both sign as witnesses, because witnesses may not be related to each other.</i> [The son may serve in this capacity] because a person may appoint a son as an agent instead of his father.
When [a man] tells two [colleagues]: "Write [a <i>get</i>], sign it and give it to so and so to bring to my wife," or "...give it to [my] agent to bring to her," one of them should write it, and they should both sign it and give it to the agent. If they bring it to the woman themselves, the divorce is not effective, for they were not appointed as agents to effect the divorce.<br>What should they do [if in error they gave it to the woman]? They should take it back from her and give it to the agent, who should in turn give it back to the woman in their presence or in the presence of other [witnesses].<br>My teachers issued a ruling with regard to such a <i>get</i> that does not appear to be appropriate, because of a flaw that existed in the versions [of the Talmud] that they possessed.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is referring to Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi's interpretation of <i>Gittin</i> 63b. Significantly, the standard published texts of the Talmud available to us follow Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi's version. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:36) follows Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi's conception and rules that the status of the divorce is in doubt. (See the notes of the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 141:99, which explain the Rambam's interpretation.)</i>
[Our Sages ruled that] the status of a divorce is in doubt [in the following situation: A husband] tells a scribe: "Write me a <i>get</i> for my wife." [The scribe] wrote it and gave it to the husband, without this being observed by witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the <i>get</i> is not signed by witnesses.</i> The husband took it, gave it to an agent,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam (Chapter 6, Halachah 4), the appointment of such an agent need not be observed by witnesses.</i> and told him: "Give this <i>get</i> to my wife in the presence of witnesses." The agent [carried out the instructions] and gave it to [the woman] in the presence of witnesses.<br>[The rationale is] that since he is only a single witness, an agent's statements would not be accepted [as grounds] to permit a woman who was [previously] forbidden to marry, except for the fact [that his statements are supported by] the written statements of the witnesses who signed the <i>get</i>. [For the witnesses' statements] are considered as if they were testimony given in court until [the husband] lodges a protest, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 24. Note the question of the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 141:17, who asks why the Rambam distinguishes between this instance and an agent charged with consecrating a woman, whose word is accepted, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:15. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> explains that the fact that the <i>get</i> is not signed detracts from the agent's credibility. For although a <i>get</i> is acceptable when it has not been signed, this is not desirable. Hence, we are more suspicious of the possibility of forgery.</i> If there are two witnesses who observed the husband giving the <i>get</i> to the agent and instructing him to use it for the divorce, the divorce is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">As long as there are witnesses who observe the transfer of the <i>get</i>, the agent's actions are considered to be equivalent to those of the husband himself. Hence, just as a <i>get</i> given by the husband is acceptable after the fact if its transfer was observed by witnesses, even if the <i>get</i> was not signed, this same ruling applies when his agent gives the <i>get</i>.</i>
[When a husband] tells an agent: "Give this <i>get</i> to my wife in this and this place," the divorce is not effective if he gives it to her in another place.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">For the wording the husband used implies that he is stipulating that the <i>get</i> must be given in that place.</i> "...She is in this and this place," and the agent gave it to her in another place, [the <i>get</i>] is binding; he is merely pointing out the place.<br>Similarly, if he tells [the agent]: "Do not give it to her [anywhere] except in the house," and he gives it to her in the loft; "Do not give it to her except with your right hand," and he gives it to her with his left; "Give it to her on this and this day," and he gives it to her before that date; <sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">From the contrast with the following clause, the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 141:63 and others rule that, in this instance, the <i>get</i> is binding if it is given after that date. Note, however, the <i>Mishneh LaMelech</i> who differs and rules that the divorce is disqualified by the Sages.</i> the divorce is not effective. "Do not give it to her except on this and this day," the divorce is not effective if he gives it to the woman before or afterwards, for he indicated that he wanted the <i>get</i> to be given on that date. Similar rulings apply in all analogous situations.
Similarly, when a woman tells her agent: "Receive my <i>get</i> for me in this and this place," and he receives it for her in another place, the divorce is not effective. [When she tells him:] "Bring me my <i>get</i> in this and this place," the <i>get</i> is acceptable if he brings it to her in another place.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">By accepting the <i>get</i> from the agent in the place she receives it, the woman shows that she is not particular about where she receives it (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i> in his gloss on Chapter 6, Halachah 4). The Rambam's ruling indicates a slight textual difference from the popular version of <i>Gittin</i> 6:3, the source for this halachah. There is, however, no difference in principle between them.</i>
When [a man] tells an agent: "Bring this <i>get</i> to my wife," the agent may send the <i>get</i> with another person if he becomes ill or is otherwise prevented [from bringing it to her] by forces beyond his control.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the agent is not prevented from discharging his agency, he should not charge another person with that responsibility. (See Chapter 7, Halachah 4 and notes.)</i> [This applies] regardless of whether he told him: "Bring it [to her]," or "You bring it [to her]."<br>If, however, [the husband] told [the agent]: "Take the following article from her and give her this <i>get</i>," the agent should not send the <i>get</i> via another individual.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">For it is likely that the principal would not desire that anyone other than the person he designated be given his article to guard. Rashi (<i>Gittin</i> 29a) offers a different interpretation. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 141:65.)</i> If [the agent] did send [the <i>get</i>] via another individual, and the woman came out to greet the agent and she gave him the article, and afterwards he gave her the <i>get</i>, the divorce is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since the second agent did not deviate from the husband's instructions, the <i>get</i> is acceptable, despite the fact that a person other than the one appointed by the husband gave the <i>get</i> to the woman.<br>Rabbenu Asher rules that even if the second agent takes the article before giving the woman the <i>get</i>, the divorce is not effective. Although Rabbenu Asher's ruling is also mentioned, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:51) appears to follow the Rambam's decision.</i>
If the agent - i.e., [either] the first [or the second] agent - gave the woman the <i>get</i> first and then she gave him the article, the divorce is not effective. [The rationale is] that [the agent] violated the husband's instructions with regard to a matter which, in general, would cause people to object.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the husband was in effect saying, "Make sure you get the article before you give her the <i>get</i>." Since the agent did not fulfill these instructions, the divorce is not valid. See the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 141:66, who quotes opinions that state that if the husband's wording does not indicate that he definitely wants the agent to secure possession of the article before giving the <i>get</i>, the divorce is binding.</i> For the husband told him: "Take the article first and then give her the <i>get</i>," and he gave [the <i>get</i>] first and then took [the article].
[If the husband] told the agent: "Give her the <i>get</i> and take this and this article from her," [the agent] should not send the <i>get</i> via another person, for [the principal] would not desire that his article be entrusted to a person [other than the one he appointed].<br>If, however, the agent sent it with another person, the divorce is binding. [This applies] regardless of whether she gave the article before [receiving the <i>get</i>] or afterwards.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">For in this instance, there is no obvious advantage to the principal in having the agent give the <i>get</i> first.<br>As mentioned in the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, the Ramban differs and maintains that for the divorce to be binding the woman must give the agent the article first.</i>
[If the husband] gives an agent a <i>get</i> and tells him: "No one other than you should give it to her," the divorce is not effective, if the agent gives [the <i>get</i>] to another person who gives it to the woman.<br>Similarly, [if the husband] told the agent: "Do not give it to her yourself. Give it to so and so, and he will give it to her," the divorce is not effective if the first agent gives it to the woman. For he was not appointed as an agent to effect the divorce.
[A husband] gave [an agent] a <i>get</i> and told him: "Bring this <i>get</i> to my wife."<br>The agent told him: "I do not know who she is."<br>The husband replied: "Give it to so and so. He knows who she is."<br>The [first] agent has not been appointed as an agent to effect the divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam, if the first agent gives the woman the <i>get</i>, the divorce is not effective. As mentioned in the notes on Halachah 31, other opinions differ and maintain that in this instance, the status of the divorce is in doubt.</i> All he can do is give the <i>get</i> to the person whom the husband designated. That person is the agent appointed to effect the divorce. He must bring [the <i>get</i> to the woman] or send it via another agent if he becomes ill or is prevented from doing so by forces beyond his control.
When [a husband] gives an agent a <i>get</i> and tells him: "Do not give this <i>get</i> to my wife until after 30 days," [the agent] may send it to her via a second agent [whom he appoints] within the 30 days, if he becomes ill or is prevented from giving it to her by forces beyond his control.<br>[The rationale is that] even though at the time [he appoints the second agent] he is not charged with effecting the divorce, since he will function in that capacity after 30 days pass, he has the authority to appoint a second agent within the 30 days.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Needless to say, the second agent should not give the woman the <i>get</i> until the original 30 days have passed.<br>Rav Meir HaLevi states that we suspect that the first agent will not convey these instructions clearly to the second agent. Therefore, the first agent should entrust the <i>get</i> to the court, which will appoint a second agent after the 30 days have passed. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:37) mentions this view, it appears that the Rambam's view is favored.</i>
When does the above apply? When the husband was not in the same city as his wife, or he was divorcing her after consecration,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 10 and 11.</i>[but before the marriage bond has been consummated].<br>If, however, the marriage bond has been consummated, we suspect that perhaps the husband appeased her. Therefore, the agent should not appoint another agent within the 30 days unless the husband said: "I accept the word of my wife if she says that I did not appease her." After 30 days pass, [the agent] should give her the <i>get</i>. However, we suspect [that perhaps it was nullified], as explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 9.</i> unless [the husband] said, "I accept the word of my wife if she says that I did not appease her."

Chapter 10

Whenever in this text<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In the Talmud, by contrast, the terms <i>bateil</i> and <i>pasul</i> do not have these specific meanings. There are times when <i>pasul</i> is used to connote a <i>get</i> that is unacceptable according to Scriptural law, and <i>bateil</i> to refer to a <i>get</i> that is unacceptable according to Rabbinic law (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). (See also the responsum of the Rambam quoted by the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> in his gloss on Chapter 8, Halachah 4.)</i> we have used the terms "the <i>get</i> is void," or "the divorce is not effective," the intent is that the <i>get</i> is void according to Scriptural law. The woman is married in the full sense of the term. If she remarries, she must leave [her second husband]; any child she bears him is illegitimate.<br>If the husband [of a woman who receives such a <i>get</i>] is a priest, she is not forbidden to him as a divorced woman,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs and maintains that the priest should be forbidden to remain married to a woman he divorced with such a <i>get</i>, lest the impression be created that the prohibition against a priest's marrying a divorcee can be waived. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 150:3), however, accepts the Rambam's ruling.</i> with one exception: a person who divorces his wife and tells her: "You are divorced from me, but you are not permitted to marry anyone else."<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 8, Halachah 5.</i> Although such a divorce is not binding, according to Rabbinic law<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam considers the proof-text that he quotes as an <i>asmachta</i> - i.e., a support from the Torah invented by the Rabbis to uphold their decree. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> himself differs, noting that <i>Gittin</i> 82a appears to indicate that the prohibition is of Scriptural origin.<br><i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i> explains that the Rambam's conception can be resolved based on his statements in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (General Principle 2), that any law that is not explicitly stated in the Torah, but rather derived through the Thirteen Principles of Biblical exegesis, is considered to be Rabbinic in origin (<i>midivrei soferim</i>). This classification does not, however, in any way diminish the status of this practice, and it is as though it were explicitly stated in the Torah. (See the commentaries on <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 1:2.) Similarly, in this instance the prohibition is not explicit in the Torah, but rather derived through exegesis. Thus, it has the status of Scriptural law, but is considered to be Rabbinic in origin.<br>It must be emphasized that just as the woman is prohibited from remarrying a priest if divorced, she is also prohibited from remaining married to her husband, if he is a priest [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 150:3)].</i> such a woman is forbidden to marry a priest, as [implied by Leviticus 21:7]: "[They may not take] a woman divorced from her husband." Our Sages interpreted this to mean: "Even if she is divorced only from her husband and not permitted to marry any one else, she is forbidden to the priesthood. This is the "wisp of a <i>get</i>" that disqualifies [a woman] from [marrying a member of] the priesthood by Rabbinic decree.
Whenever, in this text, we have used the term "the <i>get</i> is unacceptable," the intent is that the <i>get</i> is unacceptable merely according to Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to Scriptural law, the divorce is binding. Our Sages, however, disqualified the divorce and forbade the woman from remarrying until a <i>get</i> is given that presents no halachic difficulties.</i> Thus, according to Scriptural law, the woman is forbidden to the priesthood.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, if her first husband is a priest, he may no longer become impure while caring for her burial (<i>Hilchot Eivel</i> 2:13).</i><br><i>A priori</i>, she should not remarry. If, however, she has remarried, she need not leave [her second husband].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since her divorce is effective according to Scriptural law, our Sages did not abrogate her second marriage.</i> Children born [from her second marriage] are legitimate. A second <i>get</i> that is acceptable should be given to her, while she remains married to her [second] husband.<br>Should it be impossible to have a second [<i>get</i>] composed, if her second husband is scrupulous and voluntarily divorces her this is praiseworthy, provided she has not borne [him] children. If, however, she has borne him children, he should not divorce her because of [our Sages'] disqualification of her <i>get</i>, lest this cause his sons' reputation to be sullied.
Whenever, in this text, we have used the term "the status of the divorce is in doubt," the woman should not remarry. If she has remarried, she must leave her second husband, and there is a doubt regarding the legimitacy of any children that she bears [her second husband], for her own status is in doubt, and she may be forbidden to engage in marital relations.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in these instances, our Sages were unsure whether or not the <i>get</i> is valid according to Scriptural law. Because of the doubt, the woman is not allowed to engage in marital relations with anyone other than her first husband until an acceptable <i>get</i> is given. The first husband can no longer annul her vows, nor does he have the right to inherit her property if she dies in his lifetime.</i><br>If [a man] divorces his wife with an unacceptable <i>get</i>, or the status of [their] divorce is in doubt, and he desires to remarry her, she is permitted to her husband. He does not have to renew the marriage,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's intent is somewhat questionable. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explain that even in this situation, the husband must consecrate his wife a second time.</i> recite the seven marriage blessings,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For the marriage blessings should not be recited if there is a doubt whether they are necessary. Even with regard to a <i>get</i> that was disqualified by Rabbinic law, since the woman should not remarry, the blessings should not be recited (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> or write her a new marriage contract,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The marriage contract states: "When you [may] marry another person, you may collect the sum mentioned within." Since such a woman is not fit to remarry, and she could not collect the money due her by virtue of her original marriage contract, that contract remains in effect (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> unless she has been divorced in a manner that is binding.
Our Sages ordained that whenever [a woman] who was given a <i>get</i> that is void remarries, she must be divorced by her second husband, lest people say: "A married woman has been allowed to remarry without [receiving] a <i>get</i>."<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Although her second marriage is void, and thus, according to Scriptural law there is no need for a <i>get</i>, our Sages established such a requirement because of the impression that might be created.</i> And she must receive a [second] <i>get</i> from her first husband, so that she be permitted to marry anyone else.<br>She is forbidden to remarry both her first and second husband forever.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">When a woman commits adultery, she is forbidden to engage in marital relations both with her husband and with the adulterer (even after being divorced from her husband). In this instance, the woman's second marriage is an adulterous relationship. Therefore, she may not remain married to either of her husbands.</i> [This applies] even if she entered into marital relations [with her second husband] without knowing of the prohibition involved. [This prohibition was instituted]<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the abovementioned prohibition stems from Scriptural law. It applies, however, only when the prohibition against adultery was willfully violated. Nevertheless, even when adultery was committed unwittingly, the woman is forbidden to both husbands: to her second husband, because her marriage to her first husband has not been terminated, and to her first husband, because of the Rabbinical decree mentioned by the Rambam.</i> lest people say: "The man remarried his divorcee after she was married [to another man]." If one of the two transgressed and remarried her, he should be compelled to send her away.
A similar law applies when a woman remarries [after] witnesses testified that her [first] husband died, and later her first husband returns. [This applies] regardless of whether her [first] husband was a mentally sound individual or a deaf-mute, or whether she remarried a mentally sound person or a deaf-mute, who cannot establish a marriage bond in a complete sense.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:9, a deaf-mute's mental capacity is considered insufficient for him to establish a marriage bond. Nevertheless, our Sages ordained that if a deaf-mute man or woman enters into a marriage relationship, that relationship is binding according to Rabbinic law, regardless of whether the mate is also a deaf-mute or is fully mentally competent.<br>Since a deaf-mute's marriage is not effective according to Torah law, there is room to suppose that the woman should be allowed to remarry her first husband, for the difference between a deaf-mute's marriage and an ordinary marriage is obvious. And conversely, one might assume that since a deaf-mute is not held responsible for his actions, if a woman's second husband were a deaf-mute, they would be allowed to remain married. For these reasons, it is necessary to state that the stringencies apply in these instances as well.</i> She must be divorced by both of them; both must give her a <i>get</i>, and she is forbidden to engage in marital relations with either of them again.
If [after witnesses testified that a woman's husband died or that she was given a <i>get</i>], she was consecrated [but the marriage bond was not yet consummated],<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since the woman did not enter into forbidden marital relations, there is no reason to penalize her (<i>Yevamot</i> 89a).</i> and then her first husband returned, or it was discovered that the <i>get</i> was void, she is permitted to her first husband. Nor must she be divorced by the second husband, for a marriage bond cannot be established with a woman who is forbidden because of a severe prohibition.<br>[In this instance, we do not require a divorce out of fear that] someone say: "A married woman is being released without a <i>get</i>." Since the marriage was not consummated, the person will rationalize: "The consecration was made on condition, and that condition was not fulfilled."
[The following laws apply when] a woman married, and afterwards it was discovered that her <i>get</i> was void, or [it had been reported that her first husband died,] and he returned. Neither her first nor her second husband is entitled to an ownerless object that she discovers<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The second husband is not entitled to the article, because he is not truly her husband. The first husband is not entitled to it because our Sages granted a husband rights to ownerless articles his wife discovers to prevent friction from arising in the home, and in this instance, since he is required to divorce her, there is no need to prevent friction (<i>Yevamot</i> 90b).</i> nor to the profits from her work.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A husband is granted the profits from his wife's work in return for providing for her sustenance. Since neither of the husbands is required to provide for her, neither is entitled to this benefit (<i>Ibid.</i>).</i> Neither can he annul her vows.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah granted a husband the right to annul his wife's vows so that she will remain attractive to him. In this instance, the husband should not be attracted to the wife (<i>Ibid.</i>).</i> None of the benefits that either of them received after she married [her second husband] should be expropriated from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages explained that the woman should have been entitled to be recompensed for these benefits. Nevertheless, they were withheld from her as a penalty for her actions. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:56) does rule that the benefits that her second husband received after her first husband returned (or after the <i>get</i> was discovered void) should be returned to her.</i> Neither of them is obligated to pay her the money due her by virtue of her marriage contract,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages instituted the requirement of a marriage contract so that a husband would be deterred by the financial burden he would have to bear, and would not consider divorce a light matter. In this instance, however, our Sages desire the divorce. Therefore, they did not impose a financial burden (<i>Yevamot</i> 89a).</i> nor to fulfill any of the stipulations of the marriage contract.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">It is only when a woman is entitled to the money due her by virtue of her marriage contract that the stipulations apply (<i>Ibid.</i>).</i><br>Neither of them is required to pay for her sustenance. If she took [money or property for this purpose] from either of them, she must return it. Neither of them is held responsible for any of the property she brought to the marriage that became worn out or was lost,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., she is judged as a woman who was divorced because of adultery, who is penalized, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 24:10. She is, however, entitled to take all her property that remains intact [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i> even <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Property for which the husband takes financial responsibility (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 16:1).</i><br>A child born to [the second husband] is illegitimate. If the first husband engages in relations with her before she is divorced by the second husband, a child born of this union is illegitimate by virtue of Rabbinic decree.<br>If the second [husband] divorced her and she received the money due her by virtue of her marriage contract, and afterwards her [first] husband came or it was discovered that the divorce was void, we do not expropriate the money she received for her sustenance or by virtue of her marriage contract.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For when she received the funds, they were not aware of the prohibition (Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Yevamot</i> 10:1).</i>
Similar laws apply when one of two brothers consecrated a woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected in the following halachot, based on <i>Yevamot</i> 94b, it appears that only when the woman has been merely consecrated is a <i>get</i> required from the second husband. For it is possible for an onlooker to think that perhaps the first <i>kiddushin</i> were given conditionally, and the marriage to the second brother is binding. Lest a misconception arise and one think that a married woman can be released without a <i>get</i>, the second husband is required to give a <i>get</i>.<br>If she was married to - not merely consecrated by - her first husband, a <i>get</i> is not required from the second husband, because when his brother returns, everyone will realize that his marriage to his brother's wife is not binding. Rabbenu Asher makes a further distinction and states that when the woman was married to - not merely consecrated by - the first brother, she and her first husband may continue living together as man and wife, despite the fact that she erred and performed the rite of <i>yibbum</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 159:4) follows the Rambam's ruling, while the Ramah follows that of Rabbenu Asher.</i> The brother who consecrated the woman departed, and afterwards it was reported that he died. The other brother performed the rite of <i>yibbum</i>, marrying his brother's wife, and then the brother returned.<br>The woman must be divorced by both husbands, receiving a <i>get</i> from both of them, and all the stringencies mentioned above apply to her. Similarly, if a man consecrated a woman, she journeyed to another country, the husband heard that she died and married her sister,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to marry two sisters in each other's lifetime. Once a man's wife dies, however, he may marry her sister. (See also <i>Hilchot Yibbum</i> 3:11.)</i> and afterwards it was discovered that she had not died: he must divorce both women, and all the stringencies mentioned above apply to both of them.
[Different rules apply, however, if a man] married a woman who journeyed to another country, he heard that she died, he married her sister, and then it was discovered that his [first] wife was alive. The sister does not require a <i>get</i>, and his [first] wife is permitted to him.<br>Similar laws apply with regard to other women forbidden by severe prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The intent is the Hebrew term <i>arayot</i>, a word with a specific meaning. As explained in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 1:5, this refers to a woman with whom sexual relations are forbidden and punishable by <i>karet</i>.</i> who were married under the assumption that the marriage was permitted,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., one was unaware of the family connection.</i> and it is discovered that the prohibition still applied. They do not require a divorce, for a marriage bond cannot be established with a woman forbidden by such a severe prohibition.
Why did they require that the sister of the woman whom the man consecrated be given a <i>get</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For, as mentioned, a marriage bond cannot be established with one of the <i>arayot</i>.<br>If the man married the first sister, then there is no possibility of such a misconception arising. A man will never marry a woman conditionally, for if the condition is not fulfilled, their marital relations are considered to be sinful. We assume that a person will not take that risk (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 15:27). (Note, however, <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 2:10 and the gloss of the Ra'avad.)</i> Lest people say that the [first] <i>kiddushin</i> were given conditionally, [the condition was not fulfilled,] and thus the law would allow marriage to her sister. Since the [first] woman's sister was divorced with a <i>get</i>, she - i.e., the man's first <i>arusah</i> - is forbidden [to him], lest people think that he married his divorcee's sister.
A scribe composed [a <i>get</i>], but erred and gave the <i>get</i> to the husband and the receipt [for the woman's marriage contract] to the woman, or the man and wife erred, and the husband took the <i>get</i> and the wife the receipt, and thus they thought they were divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The wording used by the Rambam here is somewhat confusing. For the scribe should give the <i>get</i> to the husband, who should give it to his wife. The intent appears to be, as in the source for this halachah, <i>Gittin</i> 80a, that the scribe gave the man the receipt and the woman the <i>get</i>, and they exchanged them. Thus, the woman was never given the <i>get</i> by her husband.</i><br>[The following rules apply] if afterwards the <i>get</i> is discovered in the possession of the man. If the woman has not remarried, she is considered as if she has not been divorced; it [appears to] have been revealed that the divorce did not ever take place. The husband should give her the <i>get</i> in the presence [of witnesses], and the divorce takes effect from the time the <i>get</i> is given.<br>If, however, she had [already] remarried, and afterwards the husband produces the <i>get</i>, claiming that she has not been divorced, for he is in possession of the <i>get</i> and it never reached her, we do not accept his claim. She is not forbidden to her [second] husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, we do not even require that, as a safeguard, the first husband give her the <i>get</i> while she is married to her second husband (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 151:2).<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> also states that this law applies even if the woman was merely consecrated by the second husband, but their marriage had not been consummated.</i> Instead, we operate under the presumption that she has been divorced, the <i>get</i> fell from her hand and was found by [her first husband], who desires to cause her to be forbidden to her second husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Gittin</i> 8:7), the Rambam explains that the husband is acting out of spite, seeking to prevent his divorcee from remarrying. Rashi (<i>Gittin</i> 80a) offers a different rationale.</i>
When a person divorces his wife because of unsavory reports,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 24:16.</i> or because she is indiscriminate with regard to vows, he is told: "Inform her that you are divorcing her to chastise her, and know that you will never be able to remarry her."<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This represents the Rambam's interpretation of <i>Gittin</i> 45b, 46b. Other halachic authorities view this situation slightly differently. The various views are listed in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 10:3).</i><br>Why is [a man forbidden] to remarry such a woman? This is a decree, [instituted] lest she marry another man, repent and live chastely with him. Her first [husband] may then say: "If I had known that this would be the case, I would not have divorced her." Thus, it resembles a divorce given on a condition that was not fulfilled,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is as if he gave her the <i>get</i> on the condition that she remain unchaste, and, because of her repentance, that condition was not fulfilled. Most authorities maintain that since this condition was not actually stated, such a complaint would not be heeded by the court. Nevertheless, since the matter might become a subject for gossip, her first husband is bound by these restrictions (<i>Tosafot, Gittin</i> 46a). From one of the Rambam's responsa, however, it appears that he maintains that the <i>get</i> would actually be void in such an instance.</i> in which case, retroactively, the <i>get</i> would be void. Therefore, we tell him: "Make a firm resolve to divorce her, knowing that you will never remarry her."<br>If, however, he transgresses and remarries her before she is consecrated to another man,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, she was consecrated by another man, even if she was divorced before their marriage was consummated, she may not remarry her first husband and must be divorced by him if they do remarry, as stated in Chapter 11, Halachah 12.</i> he need not divorce her.
Similarly, if [a man] divorces his wife because she is an <i>aylonit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">A woman who suffers from physical disorders and lacks female physical characteristics. She is unable to bear children. (See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 2:6, 15:7, 24:1.)</i> or because she always experiences menstrual bleeding in the midst of sexual relations,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 25:8-9.</i> he should never remarry her.<br>[This is a decree, instituted] lest she marry another man and have children if she was an <i>aylonit</i>, or her condition be healed if she suffered from menstrual bleeding. [Her first husband] may then say: "If I had known that this would be the case, I would not have divorced her." Thus, [it would appear] that her <i>get</i> is void and her children illegitimate. If, however, he transgresses and remarries her [before she is consecrated to another man], he need not divorce her.
When an agent brings a <i>get</i> from the diaspora and says, "It was written and signed in my presence" [to validate the signatures to the <i>get</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 5. If, however, a person brings a <i>get</i> from one place in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> to another, the permission granted to the woman to remarry does not depend on his statements. Therefore, he is permitted to marry her (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 12:1).</i> he should not marry her. We fear that perhaps he was attracted to her and therefore testified on her behalf.<br>[For this same reason,] when one witness testifies that a woman's husband died,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 15.</i> and she [is given license to] marry on the basis of his testimony, he should not marry her. Similarly, a sage who ruled that a woman is forbidden to her husband because of a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a woman took a vow that caused her to be forbidden to derive benefit from her husband, in which case he is required to divorce her. She asked a sage to annul the vow, but he could not find a halachic basis to do so (<i>Yevamot</i> 25b).</i> should not marry her.<br>Similarly, when a man is reputed to have had relations with a maid-servant, and afterwards she was granted her freedom, or when [a man is reputed to have had relations] with a gentile woman, and afterwards she converted, he should not marry her.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For this would substantiate the initial rumor and make the couple a subject of gossip (<i>Yevamot</i> 24b). On this basis, there are authorities who rule that if the rumor is substantiated - e.g., a couple are openly living together, or were married in a secular court - the woman should be encouraged to convert, and they should be married according to Jewish law.<br>Indeed, we find a responsum of the Rambam himself regarding a man who had an attractive female servant, and it was rumored that they had spent time together in private. The Rambam advises that the man be compelled to free the servant and either marry her or send her away. He acknowledges that doing so would violate the Rabbinic decree mentioned in this halachah, but states that because of the permissive state of morals prevalent in his society, there is no alternative.</i> Similarly, if a gentile or a servant had relations with a Jewish woman, and afterwards the gentile converted or the servant was freed, he should not marry this woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Yevamot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), it would appear that the reason for this restriction is that people should not say that the man converted in order to marry. This is undesirable. (See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 13:14.) With regard to this prohibition as well, it has become customary in the present time to show leniency.</i> In all these instances, if a couple transgress and marry,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">There are opinions that state that if the woman has merely been consecrated, but the marriage bond has not been consummated, the couple should be forced to separate. (See <i>Maggid Mishneh; Beit Shmuel</i> 12:2). There are other opinions, however, that maintain that the consecration is equivalent to the consummation of the marriage. Rav David Arameah cites <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 21:31 as an indication that the Rambam follows the latter view.</i> they should not be forced to separate<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes the Rashba as maintaining that a witness who brought a <i>get</i> and said "It was written and signed in my presence," should be forced to divorce the woman if he marries her. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 12:1) quotes the Rambam's ruling. See <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 12:1.</i>.
In all the above instances, if these men had wives and their wives died, or they divorced their wives at their wives' initiative,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 12:4) writes that if the man's wife was sick at the time that the woman became eligible for marriage, the prohibition remains in effect. For it possible that he was waiting for her to die.<br>The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 12:2 extends this stringency to include a man and wife whose marriage was known to be plagued by strife. Even if the divorce came at the woman's initiative, it is possible that the man expected it and had planned another alternative.</i> they are permitted to marry [the women mentioned above]; there is no need for hesitation.<br>Similarly if these women married other men and became widowed or were divorced, they are permitted to marry [the men mentioned above]; there is no need for hesitation.
All these women are permitted to marry [any relatives of the men involved]. [A woman may marry] the son of the witness who testified on her behalf, the son of the sage who declared that she was forbidden to her husband, the son of the man with whom she was reputed to have had relations, or any other relative of theirs. [The rationale for this leniency is that] a person will not commit a sin so that another person will benefit.<br>A woman is permitted to marry one of [the two witnesses] to her divorce or to her <i>mi'un</i>, or to marry one of the judges in whose presence she performed the rite of <i>chalitzah</i>. Our suspicions are aroused only when [the woman's license to marry depends on] the testimony of one witness.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">It is unlikely that two individuals will sin so that one may benefit (Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Yevamot</i> 2:12). The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i>, nevertheless, state that "a master of his soul" should avoid such a marriage.</i><br>A person should always shy away from serving as a witness to <i>mi'un</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Lest the woman later regret her decision (<i>Yevamot</i> 109b).</i> and make himself available [as a witness to enable] the rite of <i>chalitzah</i> [to be performed].
When a man divorces his wife and then engages in relations with her in the the presence of witnesses before she marries another man,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If the woman has remarried and divorced, however, the presumption stated does not apply, for it is forbidden for her first husband to remarry her (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 149:2).</i> we assume that since she was [originally] his wife, he remarried her and engaged in relations with the intent of consecrating her, and not as a licentious act.<br>[The above rule applies] regardless of whether they were divorced after marriage, or only after consecration. And this applies even if we saw that he paid her money.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Although this would make it appear more likely that the man was relating to her as a prostitute and not as a wife, it is not sufficient cause for us to negate our presumption (<i>Gittin</i> 73b).</i> For it is an accepted presumption that a person will not enter into sexual relations with his wife with a licentious intent, when he has the possibility of having these relations considered to be a mitzvah.<br>For this reason, this woman is definitely considered to be consecrated,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 149:3 interprets this to mean that if another person consecrates her afterwards, we consider it as if he had consecrated a married woman, and he is not required to divorce her.</i> and requires a second divorce [in order to marry another man].
[There is an extension to the above principle.] If a man entered into privacy with his divorcee in the presence of witnesses, the two witnesses observed [their conduct] simultaneously,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">It is not sufficient for one to have observed them in private at one time, and the other at a different time, for the two witnesses who observe the consecration of a woman must be together at that time.</i> and [the couple] had been married previously, we suspect that they engaged in sexual relations.<br>The witnesses to their entrance into privacy are thus considered to be witnesses to sexual relations. For a person who consecrates his wife via sexual relations need not engage in relations in the presence of witnesses. [All that] is necessary that [the couple] enter into privacy in the presence of witnesses and engage in relations in privacy, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:5; see also <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 14:16, which states that it is forbidden to engage in sexual relations in the presence of others.</i><br>Since [it is possible that the couple engaged in relations], the status of the woman is in doubt, because we suspect that she has been consecrated. Because of this suspicion, she requires a [second] <i>get</i>. If, however, the woman had merely been consecrated and was divorced [before she was married], we do not suspect [that they engaged in sexual relations], because they did not share such familiarity.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The previous halachah dealt with an instance in which relations were observed; hence, no distinction was made whether the couple was divorced before or after their first marriage was consummated. In this instance, they merely entered into privacy together. If, however, we know for a fact that a couple shared familiarity before the marriage bond was completed, a second <i>get</i> is required (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 149:2).</i>
Several of the <i>geonim</i> have ruled that any woman with whom a man engaged in sexual relations in the presence of witnesses requires a <i>get</i>, [the rationale for their ruling being that] a person will not carry out sexual relations with a licentious intent.<br>[Moreover,] they extended [the application of] this principle, until they decided to rule that when a man's maidservant bore him a son, we take the matter into account, and the man's wife is not [allowed to perform] the rite of <i>yibbum</i> [if he dies without children],<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">A son borne by a maidservant is not considered to have any connection to his father. These authorities, however, suspect that the woman was freed and then married, and that the child thus shares a connection with his father. According to this opinion, <i>chalitzah</i> is, nevertheless, required.</i> lest he have freed his maidservant<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For until the maidservant is freed, she cannot be married.</i> and afterwards engaged in relations with her [with the intent of consecrating her]. And there are those who ruled that we can definitely assume that he freed her [and consecrated her],<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">These authorities do not even require <i>chalitzah</i>.</i> for a man will not carry out sexual relations with a licentious intent.<br>I considered these opinions to be far from the paths of the Torah judgment, and it is not fit for one to rely on them. Our Sages made such statements only with regard to [a man's] wife whom he divorced, or to a person who consecrated a woman conditionally and then entered into sexual relations without clarifying his intent.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 7:23.</i> For in these instances the woman is the man's wife, and with regard to a man's wife we assume that he will not enter into sexual relations with a licentious intent unless he explicitly states that this is his intent, or that he is entering into these relations with a condition in mind.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz and others state that if the witnesses know that the woman is in the <i>niddah</i> state, we do not assume that the couple entered into relations for the sake of marriage. If they are prepared to violate the prohibition of <i>niddah</i>, we assume that they will have no hesitation to conduct relations outside marriage. [Some modern authorities consider taking this concept even further. Although a man and a woman claim that their intent is "marriage," if they violate the laws of <i>niddah</i>, their conception of marriage is not <i>kedat Moshe viYisrael</i>, "according to the faith of Moses and Israel," and Torah law does not require a divorce. See the responsum of the Rivash cited below.]</i><br>With regard to other women, however, [we do not follow this assumption]. Instead, whenever [a man enters into relations with] a wanton woman, we assume that he had a licentious intent,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam on this matter and maintains that if a man is in general known to be moral and observant, we assume that he will not enter into sexual relations with a licentious intent. This applies even if he entered into relations with a maidservant. If, however, the man has a reputation for wanton behavior, this assumption is not applied. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 149:5) follows the Rambam's view.<br>In the law that follows, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> explains that if a Jewish man and woman marry according to gentile law, we do not consider them to be man and wife according to Torah law. This ruling is based on a ruling of the Rivash (Responsum 6), which explains that a Jewish marriage must be "according to the faith of Moses and Israel," and a couple who marry according to gentile law, regardless of their desire to live together, are not establishing their union on this foundation.</i> unless he explicitly states that he intends to consecrate her. Needless to say, this applies with regard to a maidservant<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 4:6.</i> or to a gentile woman,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the Ra'avad accepts the Rambam's ruling, for it is impossible for a man to compel a woman to convert.</i> for our marriage laws do not apply with regard to them at all, and we have no suspicions [with regard to marriage] at all. A son born by them is assumed to be a gentile or a servant until it is definitively substantiated that his mother was freed or converted.
When it has been assumed that a woman is either married or consecrated, and a rumor is spread in [her] city that she has been divorced, [the rumor] is not given credence, and we continue to operate under the assumption [that there was no change in her status].<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, if her husband was a priest (who may not marry a divorced woman), he may remain married to her. We do not suspect that he divorced her and remarried her. (See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 17:20.)</i> [This applies] even if the majority, or even the entire city spread this rumor.<br>[Different rules apply, however, in the following instance.] A rumor was spread through the city that a woman was consecrated, and that rumor was reported in court, in which case we suspect that the woman was consecrated, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 9:22.</i> Afterwards, a rumor was spread that she was divorced from those <i>kiddushin</i>; we consider her to be divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and interprets <i>Gittin</i> 88b, the source for this halachah, as referring to an instance where there is one rumor that says that the woman was consecrated and then divorced. Although Rashi and others interpret the passage as the Rambam does, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 46:7) appears to merge both views into a single understanding.</i> [Since] she was forbidden on the basis of a rumor, she is permitted on the basis of a rumor.
[A man] should not marry a woman if he intends to divorce her.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">If the man informs a woman that he intends to divorce her, he may marry her for a brief amount of time (<i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 21:28; <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 119:1).</i> Nor should he maintain her as a wife<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">He did not intend to divorce her originally, made this decision after they married, but lives together with her as husband and wife with the thought of divorcing her in the future.</i> and live with her, if he intends to divorce her.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Children conceived during this time will have severely tainted personalities, as stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 21:12.</i><br>A man should not divorce his first wife unless he discovers an incident of sexual misconduct,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">There is somewhat of a difficulty with the Rambam's ruling. The opinion he cites is that of the School of Shammai, as mentioned at the conclusion of the tractate of <i>Gittin</i>. The School of Hillel differs and maintains that if a woman wrongs her husband, even with regard to as insignificant a matter as burning his meal, he may divorce her. Rabbi Akiva goes even further and maintains that even if a person finds a more attractive woman, he may divorce his wife.<br>Most authorities agree that in this, as in most other instances, the halachah follows the School of Hillel. Why then does the Rambam (and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> [<i>Even HaEzer</i> 119:3]) follow the opinion of the School of Shammai?<br>Among the resolutions offered is that the difference of opinion between the Schools of Shammai and Hillel applies only with regard to a second marriage. With regard to a first marriage, by contrast, even the School of Hillel accepts the School of Shammai's stringency (<i>Beit Yosef, Even HaEzer</i> 119).<br>Note also the statements of the Ramah, who rules that the prohibition applied only during the Talmudic era, when a woman could be divorced against her will. In the present era, when a woman must willingly accept a divorce, this prohibition does not apply.</i>, as [Deuteronomy 24:1] states: "When he finds evidence of sexual misconduct...." One should not hurry to divorce one's first wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gittin</i> 90b, which states that whenever a man divorces his first wife, the altar itself cries.<br>On the surface, the wording used by the Rambam is problematic. First he says that it is forbidden to divorce one's first wife, and then he states that one should not hurry, implying that one may, but that it is ethical not to be impulsive about the matter. The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 119:2 offers two resolutions: a) The prohibition exists only when, as was possible in the Talmudic era, the husband is divorcing his first wife against her will. If she is willing to accept the divorce, there is no prohibition; one should, however, proceed without haste. b) Even when one hears about improper sexual conduct, one should not be hasty about the matter. Instead, the incident should be investigated carefully to determine if in fact any wrongdoing occurred.</i> With regard to a second wife, by contrast, if one hates her, one may send her away.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Although divorce is undesirable, an unhealthy marriage is even more undesirable, and a marriage should be terminated if there are severe ill feelings between a couple.</i>
It is a mitzvah to divorce a woman who possesses unsavory character traits and does not conduct herself modestly<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The implication is both that she is continually contending with her husband and that she is immodest. In such an instance, it is a mitzvah to divorce her, even if she is a man's first bride (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 119:4).</i>, as is the practice of proper daughters of Israel, as [implied by Proverbs 22:10]: "Drive away the scoffer, and contention will depart."<br>When a woman has been divorced for loose moral conduct,<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 24:12.</i> it is not fitting for a proper man to marry her, lest people say: "This one sent away this wicked woman, and this one brought her home."
When a man's wife becomes a deaf-mute,<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a situation in which a woman married while fully capable and became a deaf-mute while married. Since a deaf-mute is considered to be mentally incapable, such a person is unable to establish a marriage bond that is binding according to Scriptural law. Our Rabbis made certain provisions for the marriage of such individuals, as explained in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:9. In this instance, however, since the woman was not a deaf-mute at the time of marriage, the marriage is binding according to Scriptural law.<br>The law teaches us that since according to Scriptural and Talmudic law, a woman can be divorced against her will, the fact that the woman is mentally incapable does not prevent her husband from divorcing her. Since a woman who is a deaf-mute is not totally incapable of caring for herself, our Sages did not make provisions for her, as they did for a woman who becomes mentally unsound.</i> he may divorce her, [by giving her] a <i>get</i>, and the divorce is effective. If, however, [his wife] loses control of her mental or emotional faculties,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This also refers to a situation in which the woman lost control of her faculties after marriage. If, however, she was mentally unstable at the outset, any marriage that she enters into is not binding at all (<i>Hilchot Ishut, loc. cit.</i>).</i> he may not divorce her until she regains stability.<br>This is a prescript ordained by our Sages so that she will not [be divorced and left] unattended, [and be violated] by immoral people, for she is unable to care for herself. Therefore, [her husband] should provide a place for her, provide her with food and drink from her own resources,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">He must, however, put at the woman's disposal the money due her by virtue of her marriage contract and the money due from her dowry (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 119:9).</i> and marry another woman. He is not obligated to provide her with her sustenance, garments and conjugal rights.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling does not apply in the Ashkenazic community, nor in other places where the custom is to follow the ban of Rabbenu Gershom not to divorce one's wife against her will. In these communities, the husband is required to provide for his wife's sustenance and garments from his own resources. The rationale is that since he is not able to divorce her - for she is not capable of consenting to the divorce - he remains liable to bear the financial burden of supporting her (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 119:9).<br>The husband is not, however, obligated to give her conjugal rights. The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 119) and the <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 119:12 state that in such a situation, the husband may be granted license to marry a second wife by 100 rabbis.</i> [For although our Sages compelled the husband to remain married to such a woman, they did not compel him to live with her as man and wife,] because a mentally sound individual cannot endure living with a mentally incapable person in one dwelling.<br>[The husband] is not obligated to provide her with medical treatment,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Even according to the law in Talmudic times, many differ with the Rambam's thesis and maintain that the husband is obligated for his wife's medical expenses.<br>The Ra'avad protests that in many instances, a person with mental disorders can be rehabilitated, and he maintains that the husband should be required to pay for such treatment. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 119:11 states that even the Rambam would agree to such an obligation.<br>In the Ashkenazic community today, just as a husband is required to supply his wife with her sustenance in such a situation, he is also held liable for all her medical expenses (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 119:6, <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 119:12).</i> nor to redeem her if she is taken captive. If he divorces her, the divorce is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">As the Ra'avad mentions, in an instance when a woman has lost mental competence to the extent that she cannot safeguard the <i>get</i> she was given, all authorities agree that the divorce is not binding. Even when she has that degree of mental competence, there are many authorities that maintain that the divorce is not effective. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 119:6) quotes the Rambam's view, as interpreted by the Ra'avad. The Ramah follows the more stringent views.</i> He should remove her from his home, and he is not obligated to care for her.

Chapter 11

One should not marry a girl below the age of majority.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even though a father has the right to consecrate his daughter before she reaches majority and arrange for her marriage, "it is not proper for him to act in this manner." Instead, our Sages enjoined that a person should not consecrate his daughter while she is a minor until she matures and says, "I would like [to marry] so and so" (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:19). Similarly, from the husband's point of view, he should not marry a girl until she is mature, lest she change her mind afterwards (<i>Tosafot, Kiddushin</i> 41a).</i> When a man marries an orphan girl below the age of majority, and [before she attains majority] she [decides that] she does not desire [to remain married to] this husband, she rejects [the marriage] and departs; a <i>get</i> is not required, because a consecration effected by a minor does not establish a marriage bond in the full sense, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages ordained that an orphan girl below the age of majority could be married, so that someone would care for her and protect her. With regard to such a marriage, the Rambam writes in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:8: "The consecration is not absolutely binding according to Scriptural law; it is merely a Rabbinic institution. [According to Scriptural law, the outcome] is tentative. If she continues living with her husband until she reaches the age of majority, the <i>kiddushin</i> are finalized, and she becomes a married woman in the complete sense of the term. There is no need for [her husband] to consecrate her again after she attains majority. If she does not want [to continue] living with him, she must perform <i>mi'un</i>; she then leaves [the relationship] without a divorce."</i> [The annulment of a marriage in this manner is referred to as <i>mi'un</i>.]<br>Similarly, when a girl below majority was married at her father's initiative,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the marriage is binding according to Scriptural law and cannot be annulled through <i>mi'un</i>.</i> but was then widowed or divorced while still a minor, she is considered to be an orphan, although her father is alive.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Once a girl is married, her father no longer has any authority over her, even though he is alive (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:12).</i> If she marries again while she is below the age of majority, she may annul her marriage through <i>mi'un</i>.
Although the marriage of a woman who is a deaf-mute is a Rabbinic institution like that of a minor, our Sages did not give her the right to annul it through <i>mi'un</i>, so that men would not refrain from marrying her.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In the instance of a minor, her right to annul the marriage lasts only until she reaches majority. With regard to a deaf-mute, by contrast, there would be no limit to this privilege. This would be regarded unfavorably by a husband (<i>Yevamot</i> 113a).</i>
A minor can annul her marriage via <i>mi'un</i> whether she has been merely consecrated or [even if] the marriage has been consummated. [She can exercise this privilege] in her husband's presence, or outside his presence. Just as she can annul her marriage to her husband, she can annul her relationship to a <i>yavam</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if her husband dies without children and she does not desire to marry the <i>yavam</i>, she can dissolve the marriage by <i>mi'un</i>.</i><br>Just as she can annul one marriage through <i>mi'un</i>, so too, she can annul a second marriage or a third marriage. Indeed, [she can exercise this privilege] any number of times.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Although a girl has the right to marry and dissolve her marriage as often as she desires, our Rabbis did not approve of such conduct and counselled that the Jewish court should arrange a marriage of a minor only when it does not appear likely that she will seek to dissolve the marriage (<i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i>).</i><br>As long as she is a minor, she has the right to annul her marriage through <i>mi'un</i>. When a minor does not exercise the right of <i>mi'un</i> and becomes consecrated to another man despite the fact that she was married, [the consecration is binding]. Becoming consecrated is [obviously] a rejection [- and thus an annulment - of her previous marriage].
Until when may a girl annul her marriage through <i>mi'un</i>? Throughout the entire time she is a minor, until she becomes a <i>na'arah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when she becomes twelve years old and manifests physical signs of maturity, as explained in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 2:3.</i> or until it is known that she becomes classified as an <i>aylonit</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A woman who does not manifest any female physical characteristics. If she shows clear signs of such a condition, she is placed in this category at age 20. If she does not show such signs, but also does not manifest signs of female physical maturity, she is not placed into this category until age 35 (<i>Ibid.</i>:4).</i><br>When does the above<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">That a girl can annul her marriage despite the fact that she has passed the age of twelve, when she has not manifested female physical characteristics.</i> apply? When her husband did not engage in marital relations with her after she became twelve years and one day old. If, however, the couple engaged in relations after she reached this age, since these relations consecrate her according to Scriptural law, as explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 1:2.</i> she no longer has the right to annul her marriage through <i>mi'un</i>.<br>Similarly, [when the woman reaches this age, we assume that she has lost her right to annul her marriage through <i>mi'un</i>]. She need not be checked for signs of physical maturity, for it is assumed that she has manifested them.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., our ordinary assumption is that a woman has manifested signs of maturity. Therefore, if a woman wants to annul the marriage after she reaches the age of twelve, she must undergo a physical inspection to show that she has not manifested signs of physical maturity. If no signs are discovered, and she has not engaged in relations with her husband after reaching the age of twelve, she may exercise the right of <i>mi'un</i>.</i>
If she has undergone a physical inspection, and no signs of maturity were discovered, but she engaged in marital relations after the age when she could have manifested signs of maturity, we suspect that pubic hairs had grown and later fell off. Because of the doubt, the marriage must be dissolved with a <i>get</i>.<br>If a woman [who engaged in marital relations with her husband after reaching the age of twelve attempts to] annul her marriage through <i>mi'un</i> after undergoing a physical examination,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In which no signs of physical maturity were discovered.</i> and then is consecrated by another person, [her second husband also] must dissolve their relationship with a <i>get</i>, because of the doubt involved.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since no signs of physical maturity were discovered, it is possible that the girl is still a minor and that the annulment of her first marriage - and thus her subsequent consecration - is acceptable. But it is also possible that, as mentioned in the previous halachah, pubic hairs grew and fell off, and that through engaging in marital relations she had been consecrated by her first husband.
