Because of the doubt involved, she must be divorced by both men.</i> If she married [the second husband], she must be divorced by both husbands and there is a doubt concerning the legitimacy of a child born to either of them [after her consecration to her second husband].<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is possible that her first marriage is binding, the legitimacy of a child fathered by her second husband is in doubt. And conversely, since it is possible that the marriage to her second husband is binding, there are also doubts concerning the legitimacy of a child fathered afterwards by the first husband.</i>
When a minor does not exercise her right to <i>mi'un</i> and attains majority, she no longer has this privilege. [This applies] even when she did not engage in marital relations with her husband after reaching the age of twelve years and one day. Since she has attained majority, it is a Rabbinic decree that a divorce is required [if the marriage must be dissolved].<br>[The rationale for this ruling is as follows:] The couple did not engage in marital relations after she reached the age of <i>na'arut</i>, in which instance it would be necessary to suspect that she manifested signs of physical maturity, and accordinly, there would be a doubt whether or not a marriage bond had been established. Nor did they engage in relations after she attained majority, in which instance she would become a married woman in all regards. Accordingly, the only reason she requires a <i>get</i> is the fact of her marriage as a minor, which is a Rabbinic institution.<br>Based on the above, if another man consecrated her after she attained majority, [when she had not engaged in marital relations with her first husband from the age of twelve onward,] the second man's consecration is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if she had not yet been divorced by her first husband. Since the second man's consecration has the power of Scriptural law, it takes priority.</i> As such, if her first husband divorces her, her second husband may consummate the marriage. If, however, her second husband divorces her, her first husband may not continue his marriage with her. [This is a decree, instituted] lest people say: "He remarried his divorcee after she was consecrated."<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Which is forbidden, as stated in Halachah 12.</i><br>If her second husband engaged in marital relations with her before her first husband divorced her, she must be divorced by both men. [This is a decree instituted] because [the situation] resembles an instance in which a woman heard that her husband died, she married, and then her first husband returned.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The resemblance is that the woman married a second husband before her marriage with the first was severed.</i> [The laws governing the two situations are not entirely analogous. In this instance,] a child fathered by the second husband is not illegitimate.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Because her marriage to her first husband is not binding according to Scriptural law.</i> But if her first husband engages in relations with her before her second husband divorces her, any child born is illegitimate.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For her second marriage is binding according to Scriptural law.</i>
In which instances must a minor perform the rite of <i>mi'un</i> [to nullify her marriage]? [Our Sages established the following guidelines.] If she was between six and ten [when she was consecrated], we investigate the extent of her sagacity.<br>If she knows to guard [the money given to her to effect] the <i>kiddushin,</i> appreciates that it was given for that purpose and will guard it differently from the way in which she would guard a nut, a date or the like, she must perform the rite of <i>mi'un</i> [to nullify her marriage].<br>If she does not know to guard [the money given to her to effect] the <i>kiddushin</i>, she need not perform the rite of <i>mi'un</i> [to nullify her marriage]. Instead, she returns to her mother's home as if she had never been consecrated. If she is less than six, even if she knows [how to guard the money given her,] she need not perform the rite of <i>mi'un</i>. If she is more than ten, even if she is very inept, she must perform the rite of <i>mi'un</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 155:2) quotes opinions that maintain that the above applies only when the girl's marriage was arranged by her brother or her mother. If she arranged the marriage herself, it is not binding, even according to Rabbinic law. The Ra'avad mentions a third opinion, which states that for a girl between the ages of six and ten, the marriage must be arranged by her family to be binding. After the age of ten, it is binding even if she arranged it herself.</i><br>Whenever a girl's brother, mother or relatives arranged for her marriage without telling her of the identity of the groom, she need not perform the rite of <i>mi'un</i> [to nullify her marriage].<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Yevamot</i> 13:2), which explains that this refers to an instance in which a girl's family members prepared her for marriage without informing her who her groom would be.</i>
What does the rite of <i>mi'un</i> entail? She tells two witnesses:<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Chanan'el and other authorities maintain that, <i>a priori</i>, three individuals should be present. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 155:4) mentions this opinion, the Rambam's view appears to be favored.</i> "I no longer desire my husband so and so," "I no longer desire to be consecrated [to the man] to whom my mother - or my brother - consecrated me," or the like.<br>[The above applies] even if the two individuals are guests dining in her husband's home and she is serving them. If she tells them, "I no longer desire my husband so and so," she has performed <i>mi'un</i>.
The two individuals in whose presence the minor performs the rite of <i>mi'un</i> should write the following for her: "On this and this day, _______________the daughter of _____________ rejected her husband." They sign the document and give it to her. This is the essential portion of a deed of <i>mi'un</i>.<br>A deed of <i>mi'un</i> does not resemble a bill of divorce, in which the giving of the bill effects the divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a deed of <i>mi'un</i> is merely a legal record. The act of <i>mi'un</i> - i.e., the girl's statement that she no longer desires to live with her husband - is what nullifies their marriage. In contrast, it is the transfer of the <i>get</i>, the bill of divorce, that causes the divorce to take effect.</i> It need not be written with the proper intent, nor must it be transferred, nor do any of the laws required for a bill of divorce apply with regard to it. The wording used for a <i>get</i> is not used for it, lest it appear to be a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 107b-108a relates that originally, the Sages would have a legal record of <i>mi'un</i> written using wording that somewhat resembled a bill of divorce. They saw, however, that this created the impression that the husband was forbidden to marry the girl's close relatives. Since this is not the case, as mentioned in Halachah 16, they altered the wording used for the legal record.</i> It is merely a legal record.
The two individuals before whom a girl makes a statement of <i>mi'un</i> must know the identity of the girl and her husband. Therefore, whoever sees [a girl] make a statement of <i>mi'un</i> [in the presence of two other people] and hears that statement, is entitled to write a legal record of this statement, even though he was not aware of [the girl's] identity beforehand.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Our additions are made on the basis of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 155:8). We assume that the other witnesses were aware of the law and would have objected to the girl's making such statements if they did not know her identity and that of her husband. (See a parallel in <i>Hilchot Yibbum VaChalitzah</i> 4:29. Note, however, the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 155:11 who differs.)</i><br>It has become customary for the Jewish people to write a legal record of a statement of <i>mi'un</i>, employing the following text.
A legal record of a statement of <i>mi'un</i>:<br>On this day of the week, and on this day of the month, in this year according to the following reckoning,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., from the creation or from the beginning of Alexander the Great's rule, as stated in Chapter 1, Halachah 27.</i> so and so, the daughter of so and so (her father's name) issued a protest in our presence, saying: "My mother or my brother misled me and had me married - or consecrated - to so and so, the son of so and so (his father's name) while I was a minor. I am now making a statement in your presence that I do not desire him, nor can I live with him." We have had so and so undergo a physical examination,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., she was checked by women on whom the court can rely, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 2:20.</i> and it has been established that she is still a minor. [Hence,] we have written and signed this [legal document] and have given it to her to serve as support and clear evidence.<br>So and so, the son of so and so (his father's name), a witness;<br>So and so, the son of so and so (his father's name), a witness.<br>
When a man divorces his wife, and she becomes consecrated to another man, she is forbidden to her first [husband], even though she has not engaged in marital relations [with her second husband]. If her first [husband] remarries her and engages in sexual relations with her,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Needless to say, the Rambam is not speaking about an instance in which the woman is still married to her second husband. That would be adultery, a sin punishable by execution. Rather, this applies even if the first husband remarries his wife after she is divorced by her second husband.<br>The husband is punished by lashes only if he both remarries her and engages in sexual relations with her. Neither act alone incurs that punishment (<i>Kiddushin</i> 78a).</i> he [transgresses a negative commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Commandment 356) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 580) count this as one of the Torah's 613 commandments.</i> and] is punished by lashes. He is forced to divorce her, as [implied by Deuteronomy 24:4]: "Her first husband, who sent her away, may not [return and remarry her]."
If she engaged in promiscuous relations with another man while she was divorced, she is permitted to remarry her husband, for it is written [Deuteronomy 24:2]: "And she departed from his home and went and became another man's [wife]." It is "becom[ing] another man's [wife]" - i.e., being consecrated - which causes her to become forbidden to remarry her [first] husband.
Included in this prohibition is that every woman who engaged in adulterous relations becomes forbidden to her husband. He is punished by lashes [for engaging in marital relations with her],<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, which cites an apparent contradiction between the Rambam's statements here and those in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 1:22 and <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i>, Chapter 19, from which it would appear that a man is not punished by lashes for engaging in relations with his wife in such a situation.</i>as [implied by the inclusion of the phrase] "after she has become tainted" in [Deuteronomy 24:4]: "And [an adulterous woman] has been tainted." There is one exception: the wife of an Israelite<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">But not a priest. (See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 18:27.)</i> who was raped.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Or who engaged in adulterous relations unwittingly. (See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 24:19.)</i><br>Therefore, whenever a woman becomes forbidden to her husband, because she violated his warning against entering into privacy with another man,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a <i>sotah</i>.</i> and he engages in relations with her, he is punished by stripes of rebellion.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Stripes of rebellion is the punishment given for the violation of a Rabbinic injunction. It refers to a less form of corporal punishment than lashes.<br>The husband is prohibited from engaging in relations with such a wife because it is possible that he will violate a prohibition of Scriptural law. Nevertheless, since it has not been definitely established that his wife committed adultery, he is not punished by lashes (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> If after divorcing his wife [for such reasons], the husband transgresses and remarries her, he must divorce her.
A deaf-mute may divorce his wife with signals, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 2, Halachah 17. This applies only when the person was a deaf-mute when he originally consecrated his wife.</i> [If such a woman] went and became consecrated to another deaf-mute [and is then divorced], she is forbidden to be remarried to her first husband. Needless to say, [this applies if her second husband] was a mentally competent individual.<br>If, however, a woman who had been married to a mentally competent individual and was divorced, married a deaf-mute and was divorced, she is permitted to remarry her first husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since the marriage to the deaf-mute is not binding according to Scriptural law, it is as if she had never married a second husband. Based on a difference in the version of the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Yevamot</i> 14:1) that was available to him, the Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's ruling.</i>
A girl who leaves her husband by virtue of the rite of <i>mi'un</i> is not considered to be divorced by him.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Divorce nullifies a marriage from the time of divorce onward. <i>Mi'un</i>, by contrast, voids the marriage entirely, causing it to be considered as if it had never taken place.</i> The laws applying to her relations with her husband whom she rejected are the same as those applying to a man who has never consecrated her. She is permitted to marry his relatives. He is permitted to marry her relatives. Nor is she disqualified from marrying into the priesthood.<br>If she married another man and was divorced or widowed, or she nullified her relationship with him through <i>mi'un</i>, she is permitted to remarry him. Moreover, even if her first husband divorced her [while she was still a minor], remarried her, she then nullified their relationship via <i>mi'un</i> and married another man and was divorced by him, she may remarry her first husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>mi'un</i> that terminated the second marriage reveals that the first marriage was not binding according to Scriptural law, and that a <i>get</i> was not actually required. (See Rashi, <i>Yevamot</i> 108a.)</i><br>[The rationale is that] whenever a girl leaves a marriage via the rite of <i>mi'un</i>, it is considered as if she had never been divorced via a <i>get</i>, and she may remarry her first husband. [This applies] even if she was once divorced [by this man] before <i>mi'un</i>.<br>When, by contrast, a man divorces his wife - who is a minor - with a <i>get</i>, she marries another man and then nullifies the marriage through <i>mi'un</i>, she may not remarry her first husband, because although her final marriage was terminated by <i>mi'un</i>, her marriage [to her first husband] was terminated by a divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 108b explains that she is not permitted to remarry her first husband because we are afraid that he will change his mind and influence her to nullify her marriage to her second husband via <i>mi'un</i>. We suspect that she will be able to be influenced by him, because she still is attracted to him - for it was he who divorced her, not she who nullified the marriage through <i>mi'un</i>. In the first instance, we do not harbor such suspicions, for it was she who rejected her first husband, nullifying the marriage through <i>mi'un</i>.</i> Needless to say, this applies if the second husband divorced her or he died.<br>Similarly, she is forbidden to the father of her first husband, his son and his brothers, as are other divorced women. [This applies despite the fact that] she terminates her marriage to her second husband via <i>mi'un</i>.
When a girl nullifies her connection to a <i>yavam</i> through <i>mi'un</i>, she remains forbidden to his father, for she appears to be his daughter-in-law, since [that was her status] when his son died. She is, however, permitted to marry [her late husband's] other relatives.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The Rashba and the Ramban differ, and maintain that the woman is forbidden to the other relatives of the deceased, with the exception of his brothers. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 155:11) mentions both opinions, but favors that of the Rambam.</i> Thus, although she rejected a potential <i>yavam</i> with <i>mi'un</i>, she is permitted to marry his brother.
Whenever a woman is divorced or widowed, she should neither marry nor be consecrated<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">She may, however, become engaged to be married during this time, provided she does not enter into privacy with her fiance (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:1).</i> until 90 days pass between the day she was divorced or her husband died and the day on which she became consecrated. [This interval was required] to see whether or not she is pregnant, and thus to differentiate between the seed of the first husband and the seed of the second husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The laws governing forbidden marital relationships revolve around paternal relationships, as do the laws of <i>yibbum</i>. For these reasons, it is very important to determine who in fact is the father of a child.</i>
We count [these 90 days] from the day the <i>get</i> is written; [this applies] even when it was written on a conditional basis, or it did not reach the woman until years afterwards. [The rationale is] that from the time [a <i>get</i>] is written, [a woman's husband] no longer enters into privacy with her.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">And thus it is impossible that he be the father of the woman's child. (See Chapter 3, Halachah 5; Chapter 8, Halachah 2.)<br>The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:1) differs and quotes Rabbenu Asher, who maintains that the counting should begin from the day the <i>get</i> reached the woman for whom it was intended.</i>
[Included] in this decree of our Sages is that even a woman who is not fit to give birth, and even one who was divorced or widowed after merely being consecrated, must wait 90 days. I.e., even a minor, an elderly woman, a barren woman or an <i>aylonit</i>, even a woman whose husband was overseas, sick or imprisoned, and indeed, even a woman who is a virgin despite being consecrated, must wait 90 days.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 42b explains that this decree was applied universally lest people begin to search for loopholes.</i>
A maidservant who was freed and a non-Jewish woman who was converted are required to wait 90 days [before they marry].<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:5) interpret this to be referring to a woman who was married as a non-Jew, and not one who was unmarried. Note the Radbaz (Vol. I, Responsa 196), who differs and maintains that this applies even to an unmarried gentile woman.</i> Even a gentile and his wife who convert together are required to separate for 90 days to differentiate between seed that was conceived in holiness and seed that was not conceived in holiness.<br>Similarly, although the Torah prescribed only [an interim of] 30 days for her own sake, a <i>yefat to'ar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">A female captive who is taken as a wife by her captor, as described in Deuteronomy 21:11 and <i>Hilchot Melachim</i>, Chapter 8.</i> must wait 90 days [to marry her captor] for the sake of the definition of her child's [status].<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second time the captor has relations with her. It is possible for a child to be conceived during their first sexual encounter, in which instance the child also has the status of a convert.</i> The 30 [days mentioned by the Torah] are included in the 90-day interim.
A girl who annuls her marriage through <i>mi'un</i> need not wait [before remarrying]; our Sages' decree applied only to a divorcee. Similarly, a woman who has promiscuous relations need not wait, for she guards herself against becoming pregnant. Similarly, a woman who was raped or seduced need not wait.
[The following rule applies when] a girl below the age of majority who is not fit to give birth was married under a mistaken conception, discovered that she is forbidden to remain married to her husband and was forced to separate by the court. She need not wait, for this is an unlikely occurrence, and our Sages did not apply their decrees to situations that are out of the ordinary.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the woman was old enough to conceive a child while married to the man with whom relations are forbidden, she is required to wait. For it is necessary to discern if a child was conceived in the forbidden relationship or not.</i>
When, within this 90-day period, [a man] consecrates [a woman who is required to wait], he is placed under a ban of ostracism.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad states that the intent of the ban of ostracism should be to compel the man to divorce his wife. (See the Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:10, who quotes this opinion.)</i> If he consecrates the woman and then temporarily flees [to a distant country], he is not placed under a ban of ostracism.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that he is advised to flee.</i> If he consummates the marriage within the 90-day period, the couple are forced to separate until the conclusion of the interval, at which time they are allowed to live together as man and wife.
Similarly, our Sages decreed that a man should not marry a woman who is pregnant with a child conceived by another man, or a woman who is nursing a child conceived by another man, even though the parentage of the fetus is known.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:11) mentions that leniency is often shown in instances where a pregnant woman would be likely to engage in sexual relations with the father of the child, or with other men during this interim period, with the hope that marriage will prevent her from unchaste conduct.</i><br>[The restriction concerning] a pregnant woman [was instituted,] lest [the other man] harm the fetus during sexual relations, for he is not concerned about his colleague's child. [And the decree concerning] a nursing mother [was instituted], lest the woman's milk spoil and her second husband fail to show concern for restoring her ability to nurse, by providing her with a diet that will remedy her difficulty.
For how long is the woman considered to be a nursing mother? For twenty-four months.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:11) states that in a leap year, the extra month should be counted as one of the 24. The Ramah, however, states that, <i>a priori</i>, in such a situation, an additional month should be added to the restriction. (Note <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 13:22.)</i> This does not include the day the child was born, nor the day the woman becomes consecrated.
Just as it is forbidden to marry such a woman, it is forbidden to consecrate her until this time period passes. Even if [a woman] gives her child to a nursemaid or weans him during these 24 months, she should not marry.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Ketubot</i> 60b) states that the restriction is upheld lest women be encouraged to take these options rather than continue nursing their children.<br>If, however, the woman gave her child to a nursemaid in her first husband's lifetime or is physically incapable of nursing, there are no restrictions against her remarrying (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:11).</i> If her son dies, she is permitted to marry. We do not fear that perhaps she will kill [her son for this reason].
If a man transgresses and marries a pregnant or nursing woman during this period, he should divorce her,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad states that he is placed under a ban of ostracism until he divorces her. This ruling is cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:12).</i> even if he is a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Who may not remarry his divorcee.</i> If the man is an Israelite, he may remarry her after the 24 months of nursing pass.<br>If [a man] marries [such a woman] and then flees and returns after [the prescribed] period and lives together with his wife, there is no difficulty.<br>[A man who] consecrates a pregnant or nursing woman is not forced to divorce her.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 13:12) is more stringent and equates consecration with marriage.</i> He may not, however, consummate the marriage until the nursing period passes or until the child dies.

Chapter 12

When a woman comes and says, "I was married, and now I am divorced," her word is accepted, because the source for the statements [on which basis the woman was] forbidden [states that she is] permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">She is not contradicting her original statements, but rather adding information, and that information serves as the basis for changing her status. This ruling applies even if she makes her second statement after a significant time has passed since she made her first statement (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). Rav Moshe HaCohen objects, maintaining that her second statement must be made directly after her first statement. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 152:6) quotes both opinions, it appears that the Rambam's opinion is favored.</i><br>If the prevailing assumption is that a woman was married,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we do not know for certain that she has been married, but she and her husband have lived together as man and wife. See <i>Sefer HaKovetz</i>, which notes that the Rambam's wording deviates from his source (<i>Ketubot</i> 2:5), which mentions that witnesses say that the woman was married.</i> and she comes and says, "My husband divorced me," her word is not accepted [as a basis] for her to be allowed to be remarried.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, she is considered to be married to her husband until she brings proof to the contrary. If she remarries, she is forced to leave her second husband (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 152:7). There is a difference of opinion among the authorities regarding whether her second husband is required to give her a formal divorce. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 152:12.)</i> She has, however, disqualified herself [from marrying into] the priesthood forever.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., should her present husband die, she would not be allowed to marry a priest. (See Halachah 3.)</i> If her husband dies [childless], we suspect that there might have been truth to her words, and she performs the rite of <i>chalitzah</i>, rather than the rite of <i>yibbum</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we do not accept her statements entirely, and therefore require her to undergo <i>chalitzah</i>. Nevertheless, because there is a possibility that her statements are true, we do not allow her to undergo <i>yibbum</i>, for if she had in fact been divorced, relations with her late husband's brother would be forbidden.</i>
When a woman has two witnesses who state that she has been divorced, she is permitted to marry <i>a priori</i>, although she does not possess her <i>get</i>. If she produces a <i>get</i> that was in her possession and says: "My husband divorced me with this," her word is accepted, and she is permitted to marry, even though [the signatures of the witnesses to the <i>get</i>] have not been verified as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 7, Halachah 24. As mentioned in the notes on that halachah, the Ra'avad differs with the Rambam concerning this issue. Both opinions are quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 142:13-14), but later (<i>loc. cit.</i> 152:9), the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> mentions only the Rambam's view.</i>
[The following laws apply] when the husband comes and protests. If he states, "I never gave it to her. It fell from me and she found it," his statements are not accepted, because he admits that he wrote it for her sake, and it is now in her possession. If, however, the husband says: "[The <i>get</i>] was given conditionally," "It was entrusted to her for safekeeping," or "I never wrote such [a document]; it is a forgery,"<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., these two phrases are part of a single claim. This translation is based on manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah that state ומזוייף, "And it is a forgery." The standard printed version omits the first vav</i> leading to the conclusion that the Rambam is speaking about two claims. This conception is also reflected in the statement of the law in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 152:10).</i> [the woman] must have the signatures of the witnesses verified or have the witnesses to its transfer testify, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 2.</i><br>If the signatures of the witnesses are not verified, she is not considered to be divorced with regard to the right to marry others. She has, however, disqualified herself [from marrying into] the priesthood, as stated.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 1.</i> For she has disqualified herself by virtue of her own statements and caused herself to be considered as a forbidden object.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although we do not accept her statements, we require her to abide by all the stringencies they imply.</i>
If she comes together with her husband and says, "My husband divorced me, but I lost my <i>get</i>," and the husband says, "I did not divorce her," her word is accepted, even though it has been assumed that she is his wife. [The rationale is:] it can be assumed that a woman would not make such brazen statements in the presence of her husband [if they were not true].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">There are several qualifications to this principle: First, the woman must make this statement in the presence of her husband. If he is not present, we fear that she will speak brazenly (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:13). And, as implied by Halachot 6 and 14 of this chapter, if there is one witness who supports the woman's statements, her word is not accepted. We fear that the support the witness gives her will encourage her to lie. Similarly, as reflected in the following halachah, if her husband supports her, her word is not accepted.<br>In <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Ra'avad states that the woman's word should be accepted only insofar as to require her to receive a <i>get</i> if she remarries. She is not given license to remarry, nor may she collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> from her first husband unless she proves that she has been divorced. The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:2) quotes both views. He states, however, that in the present age, since brazen behavior is more common, the presumption upon which the Rambam's ruling rests is no longer a viable support.</i>
If a husband says: "I divorced my wife," his word is not accepted. Nevertheless, we suspect that it might be true, and therefore, the woman is considered to be one whose divorce is of doubtful status.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachah 3.</i> Even when the woman also admits that she was divorced, his word is not accepted.<br>We fear that he is seeking to create difficulties for her,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., that she will remarry and then he will prove that her second marriage is adulterous, in which case she will be bound by all the stringencies stated in Chapter 10, Halachot 4 and 7.</i> or that he divorced her with a <i>get</i> that was void, and she is not aware of the fact, or perhaps she will brazenly [state that she has been divorced, although that is not the case,] because he accepts her word, or because she is not aware of the seriousness of the prohibition. Therefore, we tell the husband: "If it is true [that you divorced her,] you are both here, divorce her again in our presence."
When two [individuals] say [that a woman] was divorced, and two others say<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected by Halachah 8, in this instance the testimony of the witnesses must be to the effect that the divorce was not carried out in an adequate manner.</i> [that] she was not divorced, she is still presumed to be married. [This ruling applies] even if her husband is present, and she tells him, "You divorced me." Since the witnesses support her, it is possible that she will speak brazenly [to her husband]. Therefore, if she remarries [in such a situation], she is compelled to leave [her second husband], and a child [born to them] is considered illegitimate.
When does the above apply? When the witnesses say: "She was divorced in the immediate past." For in such a situation, we tell her, "If it is true that you were divorced, produce your <i>get</i>."<br>If, however, the witnesses say: "She was divorced several days ago," there is the possibility that the <i>get</i> was lost. [Therefore, different laws apply.] Since she claims that she was definitely divorced, and two witnesses support her claim, although there are two witnesses who deny it, if she marries one of the witnesses, she is not compelled to leave [her second husband]. [The rationale is] that she and her husband, [the witness,] certainly know whether she is permitted [or not], and we assume that they would not create difficulties for themselves.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This assumption has sufficient legal power to counterbalance the prevailing assumption that the woman is still married. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 152:5.)</i><br>Accordingly, [different rules apply] if she marries another person. Since he cannot be certain concerning the matter - and similarly, if she herself is uncertain about the matter, even if she marries one of her witnesses - she should be compelled to leave [her second husband]. The legitimacy of a child born to them is a matter of question.
[The following rules apply when] two [individuals] say, "We saw that she was divorced," and two others say, "We did not see this." If they all lived in a single courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If they do not all live in such proximity, there is no question, and <i>a priori,</i> she is given permission to remarry (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 152:6).</i> she should not marry.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that if in fact she had been divorced, the matter would have attracted the attention of all those dwelling in the courtyard.</i> If, however, she marries, she need not leave [her second husband], and we do not doubt the legitimacy of a child born to them. [The rationale is that] people often divorce in privacy.
[The following rule applies when] we there is no existing presumption that a woman was married, one witness comes and says, "She was married, but divorced," and another witness states, "She was not divorced." Since they both are testifying that she was married, and [only] one witness says she was divorced, she should not marry [a second man]. [The rationale is that] the statements of one [witness] are of no consequence [when they must counteract a definition of status established] on the basis of the testimony of two [witnesses].<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., both the witnesses say that she was married, and only one says that she was divorced. Note the contrast to the parallel situation with regard to <i>kiddushin</i> as mentioned in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 9:31.</i> If she remarries, she should be compelled to leave [her second husband].
[The following ruling is rendered when] a woman and two men come from another country. One [of the men] says: "This is my wife, and this is my servant," the other says: "This is my wife, and this is my servant," and the woman says: "They are both my servants." The woman is free to marry anyone. For although two witnesses testified to her being married, since each one of them gave testimony that concerns himself, their statements are not accepted.
When an agent charged by the woman with receiving her <i>get</i> takes out a <i>get</i> that was in his possession, and the husband states that the <i>get</i> is a forgery, the authenticity of the <i>get</i> should be verified via the signatures of the witnesses, or via the witnesses who observed the transfer, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3. In this respect, there is no difference between the woman's agent and herself.</i><br>If the husband says: "I gave him [the <i>get</i>] for safekeeping," and the agent says, "He gave it to me for the sake of divorce," the agent's word is accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling applies even when the husband, the wife and the agent are all in the same city. Even though one might think that in such an instance, if the husband's intent was to divorce his wife, he would have given the <i>get</i> to her directly and not to an agent, the agent's word is accepted.<br>There is another opinion in <i>Gittin</i> 64a, which maintains that in the same city, the agent's word is not accepted, and several <i>Rishonim</i> follow this view. Both opinions are mentioned by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:55), but the Rambam's opinion appears to be favored. The Ramah states that the agent's word is accepted only while he is in possession of the <i>get</i>.</i> A similar rule applies if the woman is in possession of the <i>get</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) appears to favor an opinion that accepts the woman's corroboration of the agent's statement, even when she is not in possession of the <i>get</i>.</i> and she says, "This agent gave it to me," the agent corroborates her statements and says that it was given to him by the husband for the purpose of divorce. The agent's word is accepted,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even though the agent is no longer in possession of the <i>get</i>, his word is accepted. Since the woman has the potential to return it to him, it is considered as if it is still in his possession (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 141:83).</i> even if the husband protests that he gave it to him for safekeeping, and the woman is considered to be divorced.
When the <i>get</i> is lost,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:56) states that it applies when there are no witnesses who saw the <i>get</i> in the woman's possession.</i> [more stringent rules apply]. Even if the husband says that he gave it to an agent<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah is speaking about an agent who acts on behalf of the husband, giving the <i>get</i> to his wife.</i> for the sake of divorce, and the agent says that he gave it to the woman, the status of the divorce is a matter of question.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">On one hand, there is a legal principle: "One can assume that an agent has carried out his mission," and yet our Sages maintain that this construct should be accepted only with regard to the stringencies it implies (<i>Gittin</i> 64b). As such, with regard to marrying into the priesthood and the like, the woman is considered as if she has been divorced. Nevertheless, she is not given permission to remarry unless she has the signatures of the witnesses to the <i>get</i> verified, or if the witnesses to its transfer testify to that effect.</i> For our prevailing assumption is that the woman is married, and it is only [the statements of] one witness<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachah 32 and the gloss of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> on that halachah.</i> and her husband [who are contradicting that].<br>Even if the woman herself says, "In my presence, [my husband] gave the <i>get</i> to the agent for the purpose of divorce, and he divorced me," [the ruling remains unchanged]. Since her husband and the agent support her, it is possible that she will speak brazenly<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 4 and 6.</i> and in fact, she was not divorced.
[The following rule applies when] an agent appointed by the woman to receive her <i>get</i> receives it from [her husband] and sends it to her, [giving it to an agent to give to her] in the presence of two witnesses. Although the woman does not know whether [the <i>get</i>] was sent to her by her husband, her agent or her husband's agent, she is divorced, as has been explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is to Halachah 11. There is a slight difference in this instance, because the original agent is not necessarily making these statements to the court himself. Nevertheless, as long as witnesses saw the <i>get</i> in the possession of the original agent, the statements of the agent who gave the <i>get</i> to the woman are accepted.</i>
[In the above situation,] if the husband comes and protests that he did not write [the <i>get</i>], or that the <i>get</i> is void, the signatures [of the witnesses] should be verified. [This is sufficient to counter the husband's protest, the rationale being that] there are witnesses that [the <i>get</i>] was in the possession of the agent of the woman, and his aegis is considered to be equivalent to her own.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the notes on Halachah 11, there are opinions that maintain that this applies only when the husband, the agent and the woman are located in different cities. If all three are located in the same city, these opinions maintain that the husband's word should be accepted if he states that he gave the agent the <i>get</i> for safekeeping. In this instance, as well, although both views are mentioned by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:57), the Rambam's view is favored.<br>Moreover, as mentioned in those notes, the Ramah maintains that if the <i>get</i> is no longer in possession of the agent, his word is not accepted.</i> Although she did not know [the purpose for which the <i>get</i> was given to the original agent], the witnesses knew.<br>If, however, the signatures [of the witnesses] cannot be verified, the divorce is not effective.
[The following rules apply when] the prevailing presumption is that a woman is married, and she and her husband travel overseas at a time when their relationship is peaceful, and peace abides in the world at large.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">As will be explained, different rules apply if the couple is known to quarrel (Chapter 13, Halachah 1), or war prevails throughout the world (Chapter 13, Halachah 2).</i> If she comes and says, "My husband died," her word is accepted and she is granted permission to marry or to perform the rite of <i>yibbum</i> on this basis.<br>[The rationale is that] we assume that a woman will not bring difficulties upon herself, causing herself to be forbidden to both her first and her second husbands, causing herself to lose the right to collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> from both husbands and causing her children to be deemed illegitimate when the matter is likely to become openly revealed, and when she will not be able to deny the matter or offer any argument in her defense. For if her husband is alive, he will ultimately return, or [at least,] it will become known that he is alive.<br>Similarly, if one witness comes and testifies that the woman's husband died, she is granted permission to marry by virtue of his testimony, because [the truth of] the matter will ultimately be revealed.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected by the Rambam's statements at the conclusion of Chapter 13, it appears that according to Scriptural law, the testimony of one witness is accepted in instances of this nature.</i> Similarly, the testimony of a servant, a woman, a maid-servant or a witness testifying on the basis of statements he heard from others is accepted regarding a person's death.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The testimony of such individuals is never accepted according to Scriptural law. The acceptance of such testimony in this instance is a leniency adopted by the Sages so that Jewish women will be able to remarry. (See the conclusion of Chapter 13.)<br>This halachah begins the discussion of the subject of <i>agunot</i>, women whose husbands are missing and presumed to have died. In previous generations, this was a recurrent difficulty, for business journeys were replete with danger, and moreover, pogroms and persecution were constant threats. Often, men disappeared and were presumed to have died or to have been killed, and yet there was no conclusive proof to that effect. Indeed, a significant amount of the Rabbinic literature of previous generations was devoted to questions of this nature.<br>In the present age, with the communications revolution and the advent of more highly developed means of identification, these problems have been minimized. But they still arise. After each of Israel's wars, lengthy investigations were necessary before permission to remarry was granted to the wives of the casualties. And when, heaven forbid, there are Jews killed in plane crashes and the like, Rabbis must make careful inquiries based on the principles reflected in the halachot that follow.</i> On the basis of such testimony, the man's wife is granted permission to remarry or perform the rite of <i>yibbum</i>.
Any person who offers testimony is believed with regard to matters of this nature, with the exception of five women, who are presumed to hate each other. Their testimony is not [accepted] with regard to the death of the other's husband,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 24, which states that the testimony of these women is not considered at all, regardless of whether they say that the woman's husband is alive or that he has died.</i> lest they intend to cause her to be forbidden to him, although he is still alive.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we fear that out of hatred, these women will testify that a woman's husband has died so that she will marry another person, and when her first husband returns she will be forced to leave both relationships.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that the phrase "although he is still alive" is meant to exclude an instance in which a woman's husband died and she remarried. The five women who share these close family ties with her first husband are not disqualified from giving testimony with regard to the death of her second husband. The implication is that these women's hatred has a specific objective: to cause the woman to be divorced from her husband. Once she is no longer married to him, they no longer harbor such feelings.<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:13 and Rabbi Akiva Eiger question this ruling on the basis of the Rambam's wording in Chapter 7, Halachah 3, which states: "These are the women who we presume hate each other . . . another woman married to the same man - this applies even if this woman has since remarried." Nevertheless, this halachah can be interpreted to mean that although the other woman has remarried, she wants the woman who was married to her first husband also to be forbidden to him.</i>They are the woman's mother-in-law, the daughter of her mother-in-law,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Who we assume would identify with her mother. Moreover, the daughter of the mother-in-law may have a personal grudge, because the other woman will enjoy the inheritance of her parents' estate.</i>[her husband's] other wife, her <i>yevamah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the woman married to her husband's brother, whom her husband would have to marry if his brother died without children. Since it is possible that they will share the same husband, there may be enmity between them.</i> her husband's daughter [from another marriage].<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., she is jealous of the woman who took her mother's place.<br>Because of the bad feelings that characterize the relationship between these pairs, testimony is also disqualified in the reverse of the above situations. For example, if the husband of the daughter of a woman's husband is missing, the woman may not testify about the matter, although she has no natural reason to hate the other woman.</i><br>[Indeed, with regard to such testimony,] a gentile's statements delivered in the course of conversation are accepted and can serve as the basis for a woman to remarry, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 11, which explains that when the gentile makes statements casually, relating events that took place, his word is accepted.</i> If the gentile makes his statements with the intent that they serve as testimony, his word is not accepted.
Similar laws apply to a person who is disqualified [from serving as a witness] by Scriptural law, because of the commission of a sin.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to two categories of individuals: a) one who transgresses a prohibition punishable by lashes, and b) one who violates a prohibition of the Torah that involves taking money unjustly. (See <i>Hilchot Edut</i>, Chapter 10.)</i> If he comes to give testimony on behalf of a woman, [saying] that her husband died, his word is not accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">We fear that he is able to be bribed to give false testimony.</i> If he makes these statements in the course of conversation, his word is accepted, for he is not regarded as less than a gentile.<br>A person who is disqualified [from serving as a witness] by Rabbinic law,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., a person who takes interest forbidden by Rabbinic law, or a gambler (<i>Hilchot Edut</i> 10:3). (See also <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 11:1-5, which mentions other base individuals whose testimony was disqualified.)</i> by contrast, may give testimony regarding [the death of] a woman's [husband].<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Although our Sages disqualified the testimony of such individuals in other instances, they relaxed this restriction in this regard, so that the woman would be granted the opportunity to remarry.</i>
[The following rules apply if] one witness came and testified that a woman's husband has died, and she was granted permission to remarry on the basis of his testimony, and afterwards another witness came and contradicted the testimony of the first, saying that he did not die. The woman's status is not changed, and she is still permitted to remarry.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:37) follows the ruling of Rabbenu Asher, who maintains that the woman should not remarry because of the gossip that might ensue.</i><br>[The rationale is that] the testimony of one witness is accepted with regard to [the death of] a woman's [husband] in the same way as is the testimony of two witnesses with regard to other matters. [This testimony is being challenged by the testimony of one witness,] and the words of one witness are not considered when there is [testimony from] two [witnesses].<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Or, as in the case at hand, the testimony of one witness that is considered to be equivalent to that of two.</i>
If two [witnesses] come at the same time,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">When the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:37) quotes this law, it states that the witness who says that the husband did not die came before the woman was granted license to remarry. See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:110.</i> one saying "he died," and the other saying, "he did not die," or a woman says, "he died," and another woman says, "he did not die," she should not marry. And if she marries, she should leave her second husband,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous instance, a ruling was already delivered, and the testimony of one witness is not sufficient to detract from that ruling. In this halachah, by contrast, a ruling has not been issued, and one witness has no more halachic power than the other.</i> for the matter is one of doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 8:3, where the Rambam states that the woman and her second husband are obligated to bring the guilt offering sacrificed to bring atonement when one is in doubt of whether or not one committed a transgression.</i><br>If, however, she marries the witness who testified on her behalf, and she herself says, "I am certain that he died,"<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">This is interpreted by most commentaries to mean: "I know that he died." Rashi (<i>Ketubot</i> 22b) states that it means: "I know that if he were alive, he would have returned by now," but this interpretation is not accepted by the later authorities.</i> she need not leave her second husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale for this decision is stated above in Halachah 7. At the outset, however, she should not remarry, not even to this witness (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:113). There are, moreover, significant halachic authorities who rule that she must leave her second husband. Their opinion is also mentioned in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), but the Rambam's opinion appears to be favored.</i> If two witnesses come and say that her first husband did not die, [a more stringent ruling is delivered]. Even though she married, she must leave her second husband.
When does the above<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the stringency mentioned at the conclusion of the previous halachah.</i> apply? When the one witness upon whose testimony [the woman was granted permission to] marry was equivalent to the two witnesses who contradicted his testimony. For example, she married based on the testimony of one man, and two men came and said that her husband did not die. Or she married based on the testimony of one woman or on the basis of her own testimony, and two women or two men who were disqualified from serving as witnesses by Rabbinic law testified that her husband did not die.<br>If, however, one acceptable witness says that her husband died, and many<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the number is not significant. The same law applies whether two or 200 come.</i> women or men who were disqualified from serving as witnesses by Rabbinic law testify that her husband did not die, the situation is considered to be equally balanced.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, she may not marry at all (and if she marries, may not remain married to her second husband unless she marries the witness, as the Rambam continues in Halachah 19). The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:37) emphasize that this applies only when the women or the witnesses who are usually disqualified come before the woman is granted permission to marry. If, however, she is granted permission to marry on the basis of the testimony of an acceptable witness, the testimony of these individuals does not cause that license to be rescinded.</i> Thus, if she marries one of the witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The wording used by the Rambam is slightly confusing, for we are speaking about an instance when only one acceptable witness testifies on her behalf. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:118.)</i> who testified on her behalf, and she herself says, "I am certain that he died," she need not leave her second husband.
When one woman testifies that [her husband] died, or she herself says that [her husband] died, and afterwards,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, this applies even if the woman was granted license to remarry before the other witness came. This opinion is reflected in the ruling of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:38). If, however, two women testify that a woman's husband has died, and permission is granted for the woman to remarry, the testimony of a single acceptable witness is not sufficient for that license to be revoked (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:38).</i> one acceptable witness comes and says that he did not die, [the woman] should not remarry; and if she remarried, she should leave her second husband.
When one woman says that [a man] did not die, and two women say that he did die, [his wife] may remarry. Similarly, if ten women say that [a man] did not die, and eleven women say that he did die, [his wife] may remarry. For we say, "two [witnesses] are considered as 100," only with regard to acceptable witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the same situation occurred with regard to acceptable witnesses, the laws stated in the following halachah would apply. With regard to acceptable witnesses, as long as two witnesses dissent, it makes no difference how many witnesses support the other opinion.</i> With regard to witnesses whose testimony would ordinarily be disqualified, by contrast, [the law is that we] follow the majority, whether this leads to a more lenient ruling or a more stringent ruling.
When two [witnesses] say that [a man] died, and two other witnesses say that he did not die, [his wife] may not remarry. If she has remarried, she should leave [her second husband],<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even when the woman married after receiving license from a Rabbinic court. If two witnesses come afterwards and testify that her first husband is still alive, we give credence to their statements (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:128).</i> because the matter is one of doubt.<br>If she marries one of the witnesses who testified on her behalf, and she herself says, "I am certain that he died," she need not leave her second husband.
[The following laws apply when] a person has two wives and one of them comes and says, "My husband died." She may marry on the basis of her own testimony, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 15.</i> Her husband's other wife is forbidden to marry, for one of a man's wives may not testify on behalf of the other.<br>Even if the woman [who said that her husband died] married first, [the other wife is still forbidden to marry]. We do not say that if her husband had not actually died, she would not cause herself to be forbidden to him. [Instead, we suspect that] perhaps her hatred for the other wife is so great that she desires for them both to become forbidden to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., she is marrying with the intent that the other wife will follow suit and also marry. Then her first husband will return, and they will both become forbidden to him. In describing this sequence, <i>Yevamot</i> 120a recalls Samson's prayer (Judges 16:30 : "May I die together with the Philistines" - i.e., she will harm herself in order to harm her rival.</i><br>If one says, "My husband died," and her husband's other wife denies this, saying that he did not die, [the wife who testifies that he has died] may remarry. Just as the other wife's testimony does not cause her to be permitted, it does not cause her to be forbidden.<br>If one says, "[My husband] died," and the other says, "He was killed," they both are granted permission to remarry, for they both are testifying that he is no longer alive.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that their testimonies contradict each other is not significant.</i>

Chapter 13

[The following rules apply with regard to] a woman who told her husband:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Note the ruling of the <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:93, who explains that the Rambam's decision applies only in an instance where the woman has already made false statements in the presence of a court. The fact that a couple constantly quarrel is not considered serious enough to warrant the stringencies mentioned in this halachah.</i> "You divorced me in the presence of so and so and so and so," and those witnesses came and denied her statements.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">And so, the couple were forced to remain married. Chapter 12, Halachah 15 describes a situation when a man disappears "when their relationship is peaceful, and peace prevails in the world at large." All the laws that follow in that chapter deal with such situations. This chapter describes situations where these conditions do not prevail, and hence more stringent laws apply.</i> If afterwards, she and her husband departed [for a far-removed country] while peace prevails in the world at large,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the stringencies apply even where peace prevails in the world at large, because of the strife that exists between the couple.</i> and then she returns and says, "My husband died," her word is not accepted.<br>[The rationale is] that she is considered to be a liar, who desires to free herself from her ties to her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 116a records a difference of opinion between the Sages regarding the rationale for this ruling. Rav Chananiah states that the reason is that we suspect that the woman is lying. Rav Simi bar Ashi states that we suspect that in an instance where her husband appears to be dying, the woman will not be careful and will conclude that he died, even though he remained alive.<br>The Rambam's citation of the first opinion in this halachah represents a reversal of his thinking. For in his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Yevamot</i> 15:1), he mentions the latter view.<br>According to the Rambam's opinion stated here, even if the woman says that she buried her husband, her word is not accepted (<i>Hagahot Maimoniot;</i> Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:48).</i> If one witness comes and testifies on her behalf that her husband died, she should not [be granted permission to] marry; we fear that perhaps she hired him.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> notes that this issue is left unresolved by <i>Yevamot</i> 116b, and therefore, the Rambam chooses the more stringent view. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> questions why the Rambam ignores the rationale given by the Talmud - that in most cases, a woman who is granted permission on the basis of the testimony of one witness is careful and thoroughly investigates the matter before she marries, but in this instance, because of her hatred of her husband, she will not make such an investigation - and offers another rationale that is not mentioned there.<br>Rav Eliyahu Mizrachi (in a responsum quoted by the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>) offers a resolution to this matter, explaining that the Rambam accepts the testimony of a single witness in such instances without question, because we assume that a person will not lie in an instance where the truth will shortly be revealed. (See the notes at the conclusion of this chapter.) Nevertheless, since the question is left unresolved by our Sages, and a very severe matter is involved, the Rambam does not desire to offer a leniency that was not accepted by our Sages. Therefore, he offers a different rationale, even though it was not mentioned in the Talmud.<br>On the basis of this explanation, Rav Eliyahu Mizrachi explains that if a witness comes to the court on his own initiative, the woman is granted license to remarry even when differences existed between her and her husband previously. The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:95, however, states that this leniency is not accepted by the latter authorities.</i> If, however, she marries, nevertheless, she need not leave [her second husband], for there is a witness who supports her.
Similarly, when there is a state of war in the world at large, if [a woman] comes and says, "My husband died in the war,"<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the woman says that her husband died near the battlefront, but not in battle, her word is accepted (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:48).</i> her word is not accepted, even where the couple's relationship is peaceful.<br>[The rationale is that we fear that] she will rely on a situation in which the likelihood is that he will die - e.g., the people in the front and at the rear were killed, and her husband is in the center. She might think, "Since these were killed, and those were killed, he was killed surely together with them."<br>For this reason, her word is not accepted, even if she says, "He died in the war, and I buried him."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">There is a slight difficulty with the Rambam's ruling, because the rationale that the Rambam mentioned previously - that she suspects that her husband died, and because of the high likelihood is willing to make such statements even though she has no definite knowledge - seemingly does not apply when the woman says: "I buried him." For if a person is buried, he is definitely dead.<br>[For this reason, the Rashba - and his opinion is quoted as a minority view by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:48) - rules that if the woman says that she buried her husband, her word is accepted.]<br>In the responsum of Rav Eliyahu Mizrachi quoted by the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, it is explained that the woman will be afraid to tarry on a battlefield and bury her husband. Nevertheless, since she is certain that he died, she will exaggerate and say that she buried him, although she did not. Alternatively, the <i>Levush</i> states that we suspect that the fear and confusion of wartime is so great that she may have in fact buried a man, but she might have mistaken another man for her husband.</i> If, however, she says that he died in bed, her word is accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although war was raging nearby, her husband was not involved in the war and died at home. Her word is accepted, because we do not suspect that she will intentionally lie. And at home, away from the battlefront, she will know for certain whether or not her husband died.</i>
If we are not aware of a war raging in the world, and a woman comes and says: "There was a war in a particular county and [my husband] died in that war," <i>a priori</i>, she should not marry. If, however, she marries, she need not leave [her second husband].<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">On the one hand, if the woman desired to lie, she could have offered a better lie and not mentioned the war at all (<i>miggo</i>). Nevertheless, when a woman says that her husband died in a war, our suspicion is that she erred and thought her husband died, even when he did not. Therefore, at the outset, she should not be granted permission to marry. If, however, she does marry, since there are grounds to believe her statement, we do not force her to leave her second husband.<br>The Rambam's ruling is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:49). The Ramah, however, follows the ruling of Rabbenu Asher, who maintains that if the woman marries, she should be forced to leave her second husband.</i>
Similarly, if a woman says, "My husband died in a landslide," her word is not accepted. In the same vein, if an area was overrun by swarms of snakes and scorpions, and she says, "He was bitten by a snake - or a scorpion - and died," her word is not accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the woman says that her husband died in bed at those times, her word is accepted. If she says that he died in a landslide or was bitten by a swarming mass of snakes, and she buried him, according to the Rambam her word is not accepted. According to the Rashba, her word is accepted if she says that she buried him [<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:51)].</i> [The rationale is] that she might rely on the fact that most men die because they were bitten in this manner, [and make a statement without knowing for certain whether or not her husband died].
If [a woman] says, "They filled the house - or the cave - [in which we were hiding] with smoke; he died, but I was saved," her word is not accepted. [It is possible that] just as she [was saved] by a miracle, he could have been [saved by a miracle].<br>If it is a year of famine, and she says: "My husband died," her word is not accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies even if she says that he died on his bed (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:53). We fear that she left her husband sick, without any source of nurture, and assumed that he died without knowing for certain (<i>Yevamot</i> 114b).</i> [If she says,] "He died and I buried him," her word is accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous instance, we do not fear that she is lying, but rather merely exaggerating and relying on the likelihood that he died. We do not, however, fear that she would exaggerate and say that she buried him if it were not true.<br>The woman's word is accepted with regard to a famine, while it is not with regard to war, because of the obvious difference between the two instances. In wartime, there is an immediate danger and everyone is worried about saving his own life (<i>Ibid.</i>).</i>
If [a woman] says, "Gentiles - or thieves - attacked us. He was slain, and I was saved," her word is accepted, because it is not common for attackers to kill women.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Yevamot</i> 115a, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 25b) states that the attackers will keep the woman alive in order to rape her. <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 25b) explains that a woman is not considered likely to fight back, and her physical weakness arouses mercy. Therefore, she is left alive.</i> Thus, [it is not fitting] to say: Just as she was saved, he was saved.
If an epidemic was raging throughout the world, and [a woman] says, "My husband died," her word is accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher rules that the woman's word is not accepted. Rabbenu Nissim, moreover, maintains that that is the proper reading in the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. Our translation follows the standard printed texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and the authoritative manuscripts most widely available.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:55) states that the woman's word is accepted, while the Ramah quotes Rabbenu Asher's view.</i> For [there is no widely accepted likelihood] with regard to a plague; everyone knows that in a time of plague some die and some live. And it is possible that strong young men will die from the plague, and elderly infirm people will be saved. And therefore, we do not suspect that she relied on the likelihood that most people died.
It has already been stated<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 12, Halachah 15.</i> that a witness who [did not see evidence of the person's death himself, but who] testifies after hearing the statements of another person is acceptable with regard to [the verification of the death of] a woman's [husband].<br>When does this apply? When the person heard a mentally competent adult - even a servant or a maid -servant - say that so and so died. If, however, a person heard from a mentally incompetent person or from a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">On the basis of <i>Hilchot Yibbum</i> 4:31, the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17) and others explain that here the Rambam is talking about a minor who is not intellectually mature. The testimony of a minor who is intellectually mature is accepted (see the following halachah). In the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:13), Rav Yosef Karo quotes the Rambam's wording exactly. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 17:9 and the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:33, however, make the above distinction.</i> that an individual died, he may not testify [to that effect], nor do we rely on their statements.
[There is an exception to the above principle.] If a person heard children saying: "We just came from so and so's funeral. These and these many people recited eulogies. The sage so and so and these and these others followed the bier. And this and this is what happened to the bier,"<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 121b explains that all these details are necessary, because it is possible that the children are play-acting and buried an insect that they called by the person's name. If, however, their statements reflect sufficient details to indicate that the funeral actually took place, they may serve as the basis for license to be granted a woman to remarry.</i> the person may offer this as testimony [regarding a man's death], and on this basis permission is granted for [the deceased's] wife to remarry.
When a Jew says, "I killed so and so," [the man's wife] is allowed to remarry on the basis of his testimony. [The rationale is] a person's own testimony cannot be used to incriminate him. [Therefore, he is not disqualified, and] he did testify that [the husband] died.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This is one of the classic examples of the principle <i>palginin diburo,</i> "we divide his statement." I.e., we accept his word with regard to the fact that so and so died. We discard the portion of his statement in which he said that he himself killed him.<br>If we accepted that aspect of the statement as well, he would no longer be acceptable as a witness. For his deed would cause him to be disqualified on the basis of Scriptural law. (See also <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 12:2.)</i>
It has already been stated<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 12, Halachah 16.</i> that statements made by a gentile in the course of conversation can be used as a basis to grant [a woman] permission to remarry.<br>What is implied? If a gentile exclaimed: "How terrible is it that so and so died! He was so nice! He did so much for me!" Or if the gentile was talking and said, "We were traveling on our way, and to our amazement, so and so who was traveling with us fell down and died. We all were amazed that he died so suddenly." If he makes statements of this nature that indicate that he has no intention of giving testimony,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If the gentile is making these statements with the intent that attention be paid to them, there is reason to suspect that perhaps he intends to create legal problems for a Jewish person. If, however, he makes statements casually, in the course of conversation, we do not suspect that a gentile will desire to cause problems for a Jew (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:38).<br>As reflected in the halachot that follow, we do fear that a gentile may lie, if doing so will enhance his position, and we fear that he will rush to the conclusion that a man died when he is in fact alive.</i> his word is accepted.
When a Jew hears a gentile [say] in the course of conversation [that a man died], he may testify that he heard these statements, and the man's wife may be granted permission to remarry on this basis.<br>When does the above apply? When there is no rationale to explain [why the gentile would make these statements if they were not true]. If, however, there could be a reason for the gentile's statements, and he is making them with another intent in mind [his word is not accepted].<br>For example, [a gentile] told a Jew, "Do such and such for me, or else I will kill you, as I killed so and so,"<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, a gentile says: "I killed so and so," without any apparent intent to cast fear upon his listeners, the deceased's wife is allowed to remarry (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:7).</i> he is not making these statements in the course of conversation, for his intent is to cast fear upon his listener.
Similarly, if a person heard the gentile legal authorities say, "We executed so and so," their word is not accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, other gentiles relate in the course of conversation that a Jew was killed by gentile legal authorities, their word is accepted (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:14). As indicated by the Ramah, there is a difference of opinion whether it is acceptable if gentile legal authorities report that a person was executed, but do not say that they performed the execution.<br>If gentile government authorities report that a person has died or been killed (but not executed by a court of law), their statements are accepted by many halachic authorities.
