A distinction should be made between the government authorities in democratic countries where freedom of speech prevails, and totalitarian governments that act arbitrarily without consideration of the populace.</i> For they will use falsehood to reinforce their position and to cast fear [among the populace]. The same applies in all similar instances.
When, at first, a gentile made statements in the course of conversation,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:29 states that this law applies even in instances where the gentile's original statements were not sufficient to serve as a basis for remarriage.</i> and afterwards was asked questions [by Jews] until all the details [of the person's death] were disclosed,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the gentile was asked direct questions first, and then in the course of conversation said that a man died, his word is not accepted (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:15).</i> his statements are accepted, and the woman is granted permission to remarry on this basis.
It has already been stated<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 12, Halachah 15.</i> that a witness who testifies: "I heard that so and so died,"<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as reflected by the second half of the halachah, this applies if the witness does not know any details of the person's death at all. Since we do not know any details of the situation, we assume that his statements are true. When, however, the situation can be investigated, the court is obligated to do so.<br>This is the subject of the remainder of the chapter and also the concern of many of the responsa issued by our Sages concerning this issue throughout the generations. For quite often, the evidence of a man's death and the identification of his corpse were only partial, and the Rabbis had to clarify whether the testimony was sufficient to serve as grounds for his wife to remarry.</i> is acceptable with regard to testimony [concerning] a woman's [husband], and the woman may remarry on this basis. [This applies even when] he heard the statements from a woman or from a servant.<br>If, however, a witness, a woman or a servant says: "So and so died; I saw that he died," the witness should be asked:<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 28, the formal process with which witnesses are questioned is not employed with regard to testimony of this nature. Nevertheless, enquiry should be made lest the witness had erred and made his statements on the basis of likelihood and not actual fact.</i> "What did you see? How do you know that he died? " If the witness responds with clear [testimony], his word is accepted. If the witness replies, describing a situation in which it is highly likely that the person would have died, we do not grant his wife license to remarry. For testimony concerning a man's death [can be accepted] only when [the witness can say] with certainty that he saw the man die, and there is no doubt concerning the matter.
What is implied? If a man was seen falling into the sea, even if he fell into the ocean, testimony should not be offered that the man died, because it is possible that he was cast away or escaped [from the sea] from an [unseen] place.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries explain that according to Scriptural law, the man is considered to be dead. Nevertheless, our Sages enforced stringencies and forbade a woman from remarrying in such an instance. (See Halachah 20.)</i><br>If he fell into a body of water with definite boundaries - e.g., a cistern or a cavern in which a person standing [at the edge] could see the entire periphery - testimony that the man died may be offered, and license may be granted for his wife to remarry, provided [the witnesses] remained there for a period of time beyond which it was impossible for the man to live,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Pitchei Teshuvah</i> 17:134 quotes many classical commentaries that maintain (based on <i>Yevamot</i> 121a) that this duration of time is considered to be three hours. Nevertheless, the later commentaries are more lenient and consider a far shorter period to be sufficient.</i> and saw that he did not ascend [from the body of water].<br>Similarly, if he was cast into the sea and a net was cast in after him, and a limb that a person would not be able to live without was raised up,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes the Rashba as stating that this applies only when the man was tied to a rope or chain when cast into the water, and then a limb was lifted out, or if the limb was lifted up in a net afterwards, and it could definitely be identified as the man's. When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:32) incorporates these concepts.</i> testimony may be offered that he died, and his wife may [be granted license] to remarry.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes the Rashba as stating that even when our Sages stated that a person could not live without a particular limb, they did not intend that he would necessarily die immediately, and it is possible that the person could live for twelve months. Therefore, even when such a limb is discovered, the woman is required to wait twelve months before remarrying. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) incorporates these concepts into its restatement of the law.</i>
If [a man] fell into a lair of lions, leopards or the like, testimony that he died may not be offered,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, however, the Rambam states that the likelihood that the man died is so great that his heirs may take possession of his inheritance (<i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 7:3).</i> for it is possible that they will not eat him. If he fell into a pit of snakes and scorpions,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Yevamot</i> 121a, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains the difference between a lion's lair and a pit of snakes as follows: A lion's lair is usually wide, and so the person will not necessarily provoke the lions. A pit of snakes is small, and the man will step on the snakes, and this will provoke them. <i>Tosafot</i> adds that we do not say that perhaps a miracle occurred to him as occurred to Daniel.</i> into a furnace,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes the Rashba as stating that this applies only when the furnace is so deep that he cannot emerge from it. This qualification is quoted by the later authorities (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:57; <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:92).</i> or into a boiling caldron filled with wine or oil,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes the Rashba as stating that the same laws apply with regard to a caldron of water. When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:30) incorporates these concepts.</i> or his esophagus and windpipe were slit - either in their majority<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">For once the majority of these organs are slit, we can be certain that the person will die.</i> or in their entirety - testimony may be offered that he died, even if he arose and fled. For [in these situations], he ultimately will die.<br>Similar laws apply in all situations in which it is impossible that the person will live, but rather will die shortly afterwards. Testimony may be offered that he [died].
If we see a man hanging<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew word צלוב is translated both as crucified (the method of execution often used by the Romans in the Talmudic period) and as hung. We have chosen the latter term to emphasize the rulings of the later halachic authorities. The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17) rules that if a person is seen hanging, testimony concerning his death may be offered. The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:58 and the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:94 dispute this and cite instances in which a person who had been hanged was subsequently rescued.</i> and a vulture eating from his body, testimony may not be offered that he died. [This applies] even if he was stabbed with a lance, or arrows were shot at him.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, he was stabbed or shot in a place that would kill him, testimony that he died may be offered (<i>Beit Yosef, Even HaEzer</i> 17; <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:94).</i><br>If, however, we see the vulture eating from a place that would cause him to die - e.g., his brain, his heart or his intestines, one may offer testimony that he died.
[The following laws apply when] one witness testifies, "I saw that he died in a war or in a landslide, or that he drowned in the ocean<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this refers to an instance where the person remained there and watched for an amount of time sufficient for a person to die without seeing the person emerge from the water. This is substantiated by the later authorities (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:61). If the witness does not observe the sea, watching for the person to emerge for that length of time, more stringent rules apply.</i> and died," or mentioned other causes that would probably lead to death. If he says, "I buried him," his statements are accepted,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">By saying he buried the deceased, the witness erases any questions of doubt that might exist regarding whether or not the person actually died. Alternatively, because he buried him, he will have seen his features closely and will not mistakenly identify him.<br>As stated in Halachah 3, the testimony of a woman who says that her husband died at war is not accepted, even if she says that she buried him. The commentaries, however, differentiate between the two instances, explaining that since the woman's husband is involved, she will be more emotionally upset and less objective. Hence, it is more likely that she would mistake the deceased's identity.<br>Alternatively, she desires to remarry, and thus she will stretch the truth slightly to be granted that license, while an outsider will not take such liberties.<br>With regard to the concept of burial in relation to a person who drowned, there are three opinions mentioned by the Rabbis:<br>a) The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that if the witness sees that the person did not emerge after waiting the length of time in which one would ordinarily drown, he did not have to bury the deceased in order to testify. (<i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i> notes that this interpretation makes the following halachah appear slightly redundant.)<br>b) The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:62 states that the intent was that we saw the corpse after it was taken out from the water.<br>c) The <i>Noda BiY'hudah</i> (Volume I, <i>Even HaEzer</i>, Responsum 28) states that the intent is that the drowned man was actually buried. For there are times when a person is taken out of the sea and presumed dead, and then is revived afterwards.</i> and she may be granted permission to remarry. If he did not say, "I buried him," she should not be granted permission to remarry. If, however, she remarries, she need not leave [her second husband].<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 115a states that the question in this instance is whether the testimony of one witness is accepted, because the matter will surely be revealed in the near future - in which case the woman would be permitted to remarry - or because we rely on the fact that the woman will carefully investigate the matter before she remarries, and in this instance, since it is highly likely that her husband died, she will rely on the likelihood and not make a thorough investigation. Therefore, she should not be granted permission to remarry. Since the question is left unresolved, the ruling given by the Rambam applies.<br>In the responsum quoted by the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rabbi Eliyahu Mizrachi explains that - as reflected by the concluding halachah of this chapter - the Rambam maintains that the testimony of one witness is accepted, because the matter will surely be revealed in the near future. Nevertheless, he still maintains that, at the outset, the woman should not be granted permission to remarry, because in these situations it is possible for a person to think that a man has died, even though he is actually alive.</i>
Similarly, if one witness<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> emphasizes that the same ruling is given even if two or more witnesses offer the same testimony. As long as a body of water does not have a defined periphery, these laws apply.</i> testifies that a woman's husband drowned in the sea or in a body of water that does not have a defined periphery, he did not emerge [from the water], all traces of him have disappeared,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This also reinforces the presumption that he died, for if he had remained alive, most likely he would have returned home or divulged his identity in another manner.</i> and his existence has been forgotten, the woman should not [be granted license to] remarry on this basis, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 16. Note also the Rambam's comments in <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 7:3:<br>When a man drowned in water that does not have a defined periphery, witnesses [testify] that they saw him drown, and all traces of him have disappeared, his heirs may take possession of his estate, despite the fact that <i>a priori</i>, his wife is not granted permission to remarry.<br>The reason stringency was taken in these matters is [the severity of] a prohibition punishable by <i>karet</i>. But with regard to financial matters, if witnesses testify concerning an instance in which we can assume that the man died..., all traces of him have disappeared, and we have heard that he died, the heirs may take possession of his estate.<br></i><br>If, however, she remarries,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:34) states that this applies only when the woman remarried after receiving license from a Rabbinic authority. If she remarried on her own initiative without consulting an authority, she should be forced to leave her second husband.</i> she need not be forced to leave her second husband. Even if a gentile said in the course of conversation, "So and so drowned at sea," and on this basis [the man's wife] remarried, she need not be forced to leave her second husband. Nevertheless, [in the above instances,] the sage who gave permission for her to remarry should be placed under a ban of ostracism.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This applies only when the sage issued this ruling knowingly. If, however, he issued the ruling in error, he is not censured in this manner (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:64, based on <i>Yevamot</i> 121a).</i>
[The following rules apply when the body of a man who was] slain or died of natural causes is discovered. If his forehead, his nose and his facial features are intact, and his identity can be established based on them,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 120a quotes Isaiah 3:9: "The recognition of their countenance testifies against them," as support for this concept.</i> testimony concerning his death may be offered.<br>If, however, any of these identifying factors is missing, even if there are signs [through which he can be identified]<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> differentiate among three types of signs: a) <i>simanim g'ru'im</i>, signs that are not definitive, b) <i>simanim muvhakim</i>, signs that are definitive, c) <i>simanim muvhakim b'yoter</i>, signs that are extremely definitive.<br>Signs that are not definitive are never effective means of identification. Signs that are definitive are effective with regard to the identification of lost objects and other questions of monetary law, as stated in <i>Hilchot Gezelah Va'Avedah</i> 13:3-5. They are not, as stated in this halachah, effective with regard to the laws of marriage and divorce. (See also <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 7:3.) Signs that are extremely definitive - e.g., a <i>get</i> has a hole next to a particular letter, as stated in Chapter 3, Halachah 11 - may be relied upon with regard to the laws of marriage and divorce.<br>Similarly, if there is an extremely definitive sign on a corpse, it may be used as evidence to identify the body. These distinctions are also reflected in the statement of the law in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:24).</i> on his body and on his personal artifacts,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to personal artifacts, there is a further problem. Even if there are very distinctive signs on these articles, they cannot serve as a means of identification, because it is possible that they were lost or stolen and taken by another man. See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>op. cit.</i>).</i> even if one of those signs is a mole,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">There are opinions that maintain that if the person can pinpoint exactly the place of the mole, it may be used as a sign of identification (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:71).</i> testimony concerning his death should not be offered.<br>When does [the leniency mentioned in the first clause] apply? When the corpse was seen within three days of his murder or death. After three days have passed, testimony concerning his death should not be offered, because his facial features [may have] become distorted.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if the witnesses think that they can identify the corpse, their testimony cannot be relied on, because the person's facial features may have been distorted.</i>
[The following rules apply when a man] drowned at sea and was cast out onto dry land. Although several days have passed, if his facial features and his nose can be identified,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 121a explains that lying in water generally causes the flesh of a corpse to shrink, and the person's features remain distinct.</i> testimony may be offered with regard to his [death], for in water the features of a corpse do not become distorted until an extremely long period of time has passed.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot, ibid.</i>, mentions a corpse being identified after being in the Jordan River for 17 days.</i><br>If the corpse lay on dry land for twelve hours<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Once a corpse that has been lying in the water is brought to dry land, its features will begin to lose their distinctive qualities rapidly.<br>There are several different versions of this halachah. We have chosen to follow the standard printed text in our translation. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that the proper version should be "if the corpse lies on dry land for hours" - i.e., it need not be identified immediately, but it cannot wait any significant period. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:26) states that the body must be identified immediately after it emerges onto dry land. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:80, however, mentions that an interval of an hour is granted.</i> after being cast out of the sea and the corpse became bloated,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> notes that the Rambam's statement of this law is conditional, "If the... corpse became bloated," implying that if we see that the corpse has not become bloated, we may identify it. The Rashba, by contrast, states we suspect that the facial features of a corpse will become distorted after a brief amount of time, and does not allow room for leniency.<br>Even according to this conception, leniency has been granted by the later authorities when a corpse was removed frozen from a river, and it took several hours to thaw.</i> testimony may not be offered because [his features] have been distorted.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In restating this law, there are several points mentioned by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i>): a) the corpse may not have a wound. If the body was wounded, we fear that its features will be distorted; b) even if it is less than three days after the deceased's passing, if his corpse lay in water for a significant period, it must be identified directly after its discovery.<br>The Ramah adds that extremely distinctive signs may be used as a means of identification even though the body has lain on dry land for a long period.</i> When we look at the form [of a corpse] in order to identify [a man] to offer testimony with regard to his [death], [it is acceptable if] we look at [the corpse] even by candlelight or by moonlight.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Although these sources of light are not overly bright, our Sages felt that they were sufficient to enable a person to identify a corpse. There is no need to wait until daylight.</i>
[The following rule applies when] we see a person at a distance and he says that he is so and so, the son of so and so, or so and so from a particular place,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17) states that the person must mention both these factors: his father's name and his city. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:66 states that, although in general one of these means of identification would have been sufficient, both are necessary because the person's facial features have changed. The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:40, however, states that when it is obvious that the person knows the place from which the person came, the ruling of the Rambam and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:23) may be followed, and only one factor need be mentioned. This is also borne out by the following halachah and its source in <i>Yevamot</i> 115b.</i> and that he has been bitten by a snake and is dying. Even if we go [to the place where he was standing], find [the corpse and discover] that its features have changed to the extent that it is no longer distinguishable, we may, nevertheless, [grant license for] his wife [to] remarry.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we do not assume that the person was lying, but instead suppose that the poison caused his features to change (<i>Meiri</i>).<br><i>Yevamot</i> 122a, the source for this halachah, mentions that perhaps the statements were made by a demon. The Rambam, however, does not mention this possibility, in keeping with his conception (see <i>The Guide for the Perplexed</i>, Vol. III, Chapter 46), that demons are not a relevant halachic phenomenon.</i>
[A person's testimony is effective in the following instance.] A person came and said: "A court - or people - told me, when you arrive at this and this place,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he was informing the Rabbinical court of that city that one of the Jewish inhabitants had died (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> tell them that Yitzchak, the son of Michael, died." When this person came to that place and delivered the message - although he is not aware of the identity [of the deceased] - since [the people at that place] do know his identity, [the deceased's] wife [may be granted] permission [to remarry]. We do not presume that perhaps it was another person named Yitzchak, the son of Michael, who died.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> mention that this principle applies only when we do not know of another person in the city with that name, or we know that this other person is still alive. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:18) incorporates these additions into its statement of this law.</i>
When a Jew and a gentile left from our [location] for a different destination, and the gentile came and said in the course of conversation: "The man who left [this city] with me died," [the deceased's] wife [may be granted license to] remarry. [This applies] even when the gentile does not know the identity of the man, provided he says: "I buried him."<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam's ruling is that when a gentile knows the name of a Jew, it is unnecessary for him to say that he buried him, when relating that he died. If, however, the gentile did not know the identity of the Jew, it is necessary for him to say that he buried him.<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17) and other authorities do not agree with the Rambam with regard to this point and maintain that the gentile's statements are accepted even when he does not say that he buried the Jew. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:17) mentions both opinions, but appears to favor the latter view.</i>
Similarly, when ten men were taken together while shackled in chains or tied by heavy ropes used to lead camels<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the gloss of Rav Kapach, who cites a similar interpretation mentioned by the Razeh.</i> or the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's words, it appears that the men must be tied together. Otherwise, we suspect that one of them left and was replaced by another. In his commentary on the source for this law (<i>Yevamot</i> 122a), Rashi does not share this conception and maintains that the number of men itself is sufficient to serve as identification.</i> from one place to another,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Noda BiY'hudah</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i>, Volume I, Responsum 36) notes that the Rambam requires that a) a specific number of men were mentioned, b) that their starting point and destination were mentioned, and c) that they were chained together.<br>The <i>Noda BiY'hudah</i> questions these requirements, because the standard text of <i>Yevamot</i> 122a (the Rambam's source) mentions two stories: one of men traveling in chains and one of sixty men traveling to Betar. The Rambam appears to have fused the two stories together, adopting the requirements mentioned by both and thus being more stringent than his source.<br>The <i>Noda BiY'hudah</i> reconciles this difficulty by referring to the statement of these incidents in Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi's <i>Halachot</i>, which follows a different version from our text of <i>Yevamot</i>. Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi mentions "two men traveling in chains to Antioch." Since the Rambam was a student of one of the disciples of Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi, it is likely that the Rambam also had the same version of this passage.</i> and a gentile came and said in the course of conversation that all ten men who went out while tied died, and he buried them,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Here too, the Rambam is referring to an instance in which the gentile did not know the identity of the people who died. (See the notes on the previous halachah.)</i> their wives [may be granted license to] remarry.
If a Jew says: "A Jewish man died in our company in this and this place. These and these were his facial features. These and these were signs of identification," we do not make a guess and say that it was probably so and so.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if the person is missing, and there are no traces of him left.</i> Rather [the person is not considered to have died] until a witness mentions his name and the name of his city.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, alternatively, a person's name and his father's name.</i><br>If, however, [a person]<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Our wording is based on the gloss of the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:19), who states that this law applies even if the report is given by a gentile in the course of conversation.</i> says: "One of the inhabitants<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, the name of the deceased is not mentioned.</i> of the city of so and so journeyed out in our company and died," we check in that city. If only one man left the city, his wife [may be given permission to] marry.
[The following rule applies if] a document was found saying: "So and so, the son of so and so, died," or "So and so, the son of so and so, was killed": If it can be determined that this was written<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the note must also be signed, and we must see that the signature was written by a Jew. The signature need not, however, be verified. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:11 and commentaries.)</i> by a Jew,<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Regardless of the language in which the note was written, if it was written by a Jew, it is acceptable. If, however, it was written by a gentile, it is not acceptable even if it is written in Hebrew. For a gentile's statements are acceptable only when made in the course of conversation (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>In his <i>Bedek HaBayit</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17), Rav Yosef Karo asks why a statement written by a gentile in the course of conversation is not effective. And in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), he rules that it is.</i> [the man's] wife [may be given permission to] marry.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This is a leniency accepted in order to allow a woman to remarry. Generally, testimony must be given verbally and not in writing. (See <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 3:4.)</i><br>Similarly, when a person has lost the power of speech, and he was tested, as he would be tested with regard to a <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 16.</i> and it is proven that he is mentally sound, if he writes that so and so died, we may rely on his writing, and [the deceased's] wife [may be given permission to] marry.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 9:11, which explains that such testimony is ordinarily not accepted by a Rabbinic court. An exception is made in this instance in order to allow a woman to remarry.</i><br>We do not follow the standard process of interrogation<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Which includes questions defining the exact time and place of the event. (See <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 1:4.)</i> with regard to witnesses who testify [concerning the death of] a woman's [husband]. For our Sages did not speak about establishing stringencies regarding such matters. [Indeed, their approach was characterized by] leniency in order to permit a woman without a husband [to remarry].<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Moreover, even if slight contradictions arise regarding minor points of the testimony of two witnesses, the testimony is nevertheless accepted (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:21).<br>In one of his responsa (quoted by the Ramah, <i>op. cit.</i>), the Rambam writes: "Whoever is stringent and interrogates witnesses exactingly is not conducting himself properly, and his approach does not find favor in the eyes of our Sages." The Ramah, however, continues that if testimony appears contrived to the court, they must thoroughly investigate the matter.</i>
Do not wonder at the fact that our Sages discharged<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., these are leniencies that our Sages instituted. The acceptance of the testimony of one witness, by contrast, is a leniency implied by Scriptural law.<br>This follows the interpretation of the Rambam's statements here by the <i>Noda BiY'hudah</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i>, Volume I, Responsa 27 and 33). There is, however, an apparent contradiction based on the Rambam's statements in <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 5:2.</i> the prohibition [against a married woman], which is considered a very severe matter, on the basis of the testimony of a woman, a servant or a maidservant, statements made by a gentile in the course of conversation, a written statement or [testimony] that was not investigated by the ordinary process of interrogation, as we have explained.<br>[These leniencies were instituted] because the Torah requires only testimony of two witnesses, and all the other details of the laws of witnesses with regard to matters that cannot be verified definitively except via witnesses and their testimony - e.g., that one person killed another, or that one person lent money to another. When, by contrast, the matter may be verified definitively without the testimony of a witness, and the witness cannot justify [his statements] if they are not true - e.g, when one testifies that a person died,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the man presumed dead returns, it will be obvious that the witness lied.</i> the Torah did not necessitate [that the requirements of formal testimony be met in these instances]. For it is unlikely that a witness will testify falsely.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">See similar statements in <i>Hilchot Kiddush HaChodesh</i> 3:14 and <i>Hilchot Yibbum VaChalitzah</i> 4:31.<br>The Ra'avad and others note that our Sages (<i>Yevamot</i> 93b, 115a) mention two rationales why a woman should be permitted to remarry, the one cited by the Rambam and also because of the severe consequences that she will bring upon herself if her husband is found alive (see Chapter 10, Halachah 7), a woman will investigate the matter thoroughly and will not remarry unless she is certain that he has actually died. The Talmud appears to imply that these rationales are contradictory and cites cases in which one would apply, but not the other.<br>The <i>Noda BiY'hudah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) explains that the Rambam sees the two rationales as complementary. Ordinarily, when a matter can easily be verified - e.g., a question of whether or not <i>chalitzah</i> was performed - the statement of one witness is acceptable without question. In this instance, however, it is not always easy to verify whether in fact the man died, and therefore the rationale that the woman must investigate the matter is necessary. For this reason, in all the instances where the Talmud implies that one rationale would conflict with another, the Rambam reaches a conclusion that takes both rationales into consideration.</i><br>For this reason, our Sages [extended] the leniency with regard to this matter and accepted the testimony of a single witness that is based on the testimony of a maidservant, [testimony] from a written document, and [testimony] that was not investigated by the ordinary process of interrogation. [These leniencies were accepted] so that the daughters of Israel will not be forced to remain unmarried.<br>Blessed be the Merciful One, who grants assistance.
